# Likkutei Sichos


## במדבר

## Sicha 1

## 1

A. Regarding the verse, "But you shall not count the tribe of Levi, nor take their census among the children of Israel" (which is stated following the census of the "children of Israel"), Rashi quotes the words, "But you shall not count the tribe of Levi," and explains: It is fitting for the King’s legion to be counted separately. Alternatively, Hashem foresaw that a decree would be issued against all those counted from twenty years and up, that they would die in the desert; He said, let these not be included, for they are Mine, as they did not err in the golden calf.

From the plain language of Rashi, it appears that he does not intend to negate the interpretation that "But you shall not count the tribe of Levi" means that the tribe of Levi was not counted *at all*. Rather, he explains that the tribe of Levi *was* counted (as is explicitly stated *later* in the Parsha), but that they were counted "*separately*" — for if that were not the case, Rashi should have emphasized the *novelty* that the tribe of Levi was counted separately (since it had not yet been mentioned in the verse that the tribe of Levi was counted), and only afterward added the reasons why they were counted separately.

Rather, Rashi comes to explain why the tribe of Levi was counted separately, and provides two reasons for this: 1) Because they are the "King’s legion." 2) So that "these would not be included" in the "decree against all those counted from twenty years and up, that they would die in the desert."

It can be said that Rashi did not need to negate the aforementioned interpretation (that the tribe of Levi was not counted at all) — because after he had already explained at the beginning of the Parsha, "Because of His love for them, He counts them at all times... when He came to cause His Presence to dwell upon them, He counted them," it is understood in a simple sense (and there is no doubt) that the tribe of Levi was counted, and indeed, even more so.

Rather, Rashi comes to resolve the difficulty in the verse: Since in the preceding verses (in the detailed account of the census of the children of Israel) it is explicitly stated that the children of Levi were not counted, it is proven that in the command, "Take the census of all the congregation of the children of Israel, etc.," the tribe of Levi was not included — and if so, what is the reason for the command, "But you shall not count the tribe of Levi, etc., among the children of Israel," which implies that he is introducing a novelty—that they should not be counted together with the rest of Israel? Regarding this, Rashi brings two reasons, as mentioned above.

## 2

B. However, it still requires understanding:
1) Rashi’s interpretation (explaining the reasons for why the tribe of Levi was counted separately) applies also to the words at the end of the verse — "(But, etc., you shall not count, etc.) among the children of Israel"; why did Rashi not quote these words as well, neither in his explanation nor in the *dibbur hamaschil* (and he did not even hint at them with "etc.")?
2) The aforementioned question (what is the novelty in not counting the tribe of Levi among the children of Israel?) is already raised earlier, in the verse, "But the Levites, according to their ancestral tribe, were not counted among them" (which also proves that there is a novelty in not counting the Levites "among them") — and yet Rashi did not explain anything there; if so, it is not understood: what is the specific difficulty in the current verse, that specifically here Rashi was compelled to explain why the Levites were counted separately?
3) What is difficult about Rashi’s first reason, such that he was not satisfied with it and brought a second reason?
Especially since, on the face of it, it is far from the path of *peshat* — both in terms of its general content (that the reason they were counted separately is to save them from the decree that "Hashem foresaw would be issued"), which is closer to *Aggadah* than to the plain meaning of the text;

## 3

And both in terms of the sequence of the verses here: in the following verse it is stated, "But you shall appoint the Levites over the Tabernacle of the Testimony, etc.," which is to say, the appointment and authority of the tribe of Levi over the Tabernacle. If so, according to Rashi’s first interpretation, that the tribe of Levi was counted separately because they are the "King’s legion," the continuation of the matter is understood, because the command, "But you shall appoint the Levites over the Tabernacle, etc.," is the reason for "But you shall not count the tribe of Levi, etc., among the children of Israel" (because they are the "King’s legion" appointed over the Tabernacle of the Testimony); but according to the second interpretation, there is no continuation, seemingly, between "But you shall appoint the Levites, etc." and "But you shall not count the tribe of Levi, etc."?
[And according to this interpretation (the second), he should have written here (not "But you shall appoint the Levites, etc.," but rather) the verse that appears later in the Parsha, "And I, behold, I have taken the Levites from among the children of Israel, etc., and the Levites shall be Mine" — in accordance with the reason that they were saved from the decree "because they are Mine, as they did not err in the golden calf" (as Rashi explains that the taking of the Levites was "when they sinned in the golden calf... and the Levites who did not serve idolatry were chosen").]

C. It can be said that the explanation for this is:
In the verse here, there is, seemingly, a redundancy of language — "(But the tribe of Levi) you shall not count, nor shall you take their census." Seemingly, it should have said, "But the tribe of Levi you shall not count among the children of Israel," or "But the census of the children of Levi you shall not take among the children of Israel" (and the like).
From the plain language, it is implied that "you shall not count" and "you shall not take their census" are two different details regarding the matter of the census.
And the distinction between them is — in a simple sense: "Count" (*tifkod*) refers to the act of counting, while taking a census ("their census... you shall take") refers (according to Rashi) to receiving the "sum of their census," which is to say, knowing the total—"how many they are."
And here, according to Rashi’s interpretation, there is a fundamental difference between the manner of counting the children of Israel and the counting of the Levites: regarding the census of the children of Israel, Rashi explains that it was "by means of shekels, a *beka* per head" [and this is in accordance with his interpretation at the beginning of Parshas Tisa: "When you wish to receive the sum of their census... do not count them by head, but let each one give a half-shekel, and you shall count the shekels and know their census"], whereas the census of the Levites was "by the word of Hashem," as Rashi explains: "Moshe went and stood at the entrance of the Tent, and the *Shechinah* preceded him, and a *bas kol* went out from the Tent and said, 'So and so many infants are in the Tent'" (and through this, Moshe knew the "census of their nursing infants" — for the census of the Levites was "from one month and up").

And this is the interpretation of the language of the verse, "But the tribe of Levi you shall not count, nor shall you take their census among the children of Israel" — meaning, not only was the quantity of the Levites' census ("their census... you shall take") not "among the children of Israel" (meaning that the "sum of their census" is not to be included in the sum of the census of the children of Israel), but also the manner of the act of counting ("you shall count") of the children of Levi was completely different from the manner of the census of the children of Israel, as mentioned above.

## 4

D. And now, based on this principle (that there are two matters here—the act of counting and the taking of the census; the act of counting and the summation of the census calculation), we must investigate the interpretation of the verse, "But the tribe of Levi you shall not count" — for it can be said in two ways:
1) In a simple sense — that the end of the verse, "among the children of Israel," is also applied to the beginning of the verse, "But the tribe of Levi you shall not count," and the intent of the verse is that you shall not count the Levites "among (in the same manner of counting as) the children of Israel," because their census was in a different manner.
2) Since the census of the Levites was by means of a *bas kol* that informed Moshe, "So and so many infants are in the Tent" — this is not in the category of "counting" (*tifkod*), the essence of which is a) the counting of the person, b) counting them one by one "by their heads." Based on this, the verse is divided into two: (a) "But the tribe of Levi you shall not count" — the end of the matter, that the tribe of Levi was not to be counted at all; (b) "nor shall you take their census among the children of Israel" — that the knowledge of "the sum of their census" is not "among the children of Israel," but rather by themselves.

And this is the distinction between the two interpretations in Rashi:
According to the first interpretation — "It is fitting for the King’s legion to be counted separately" — even the first part of the verse ("But the tribe of Levi you shall not count") comes to emphasize the obligation, that the tribe of Levi must be counted separately (not "among the children of Israel"), meaning that the Levites were indeed counted, but they were counted by themselves in a different manner.
Whereas according to the second interpretation — "Hashem foresaw, etc., a decree against all those counted, etc., let these not be included" — the verse (in the first part) does not come to emphasize the affection for the children of Levi in their special census, but rather the opposite — because of their affection, they were not counted (at all), so that the "decree against all those counted, etc." would not apply to them.
[Meaning: According to Rashi here, it must be that the reason the decree of the spies did not apply to the Levites is not (only) because they were counted "from one month, etc." and consequently were not included in the census of "from twenty years, etc." (for if that were Rashi’s intent, he should have explained this explicitly, as we had not yet learned that the Levites were counted "from one month, etc.") — but (also) because they were not counted (at all), as in section VI below.]

And according to this interpretation, the statement "But the tribe of Levi you shall not count" means, in its plain sense — that you shall not count the Levites, in order to save them from the decree of the spies.

## 5

E. Based on this, all the questions from above (section II) are resolved — because Rashi’s entire intent is to resolve the lengthiness of the language in this verse (as mentioned above), and therefore:
1) This does not apply to the verse, "And the Levites, etc., were not counted among them."
2) It is simple why Rashi did not bring the end of the verse, "among the children of Israel" — a) because he did not set out here to resolve why the tribe of Levi was counted separately based on the precision of the verse "among the children of Israel" (for they were counted by themselves), but rather to explain the lengthiness of the language, as mentioned above; b) there is no place to bring the end of the verse, since his intent is to say that it should be interpreted (like the second interpretation) that the first part of the verse ("But... you shall not count") is a separate matter, and the end, "among the children of Israel," does not apply to the "you shall not count" at the beginning of the verse.
3) Rashi was not satisfied with the first interpretation, because the language "But the tribe of Levi you shall not count" is more simply suited to the second interpretation, according to which the meaning is literally as it sounds, that "the tribe of Levi you shall not count" at all.

F. However, it still requires explanation regarding what was stated above, that according to the second interpretation, the decree of the spies did not apply to the Levites (not because they were not included in the category of "twenty years and up," but) because they were not counted — for seemingly, after all, the Levites were counted "by the word of Hashem," and what is the connection of the decree of the spies to the manner of the census (whether by shekels or "by the word of Hashem")?

It can be said that this is understood based on Rashi from earlier:
At the beginning of Parshas Tisa it is stated, "When you take the census of the children of Israel according to their numbers, then let each one give, etc., when you count them, and there will be no plague among them when you count them," and Rashi explains, "And there will be no plague among them — for the census is susceptible to the evil eye, and a plague comes upon them" (and therefore the census must be by means of shekels, "and do not count them by head," which saves one from the "evil eye"). Thus, it is found that the census ("when you count them") is liable to cause the influence of the evil eye and a plague (even though the reason for the census is — as Rashi says — "because of His love for them").

And from this, it is also understood for our subject — and by way of introduction:
The reason the Levites required special protection from the decree of the spies is not because they were liable for punishment for a sin (for the Levites did not participate in the counsel of the spies, as is explicit in Rashi), but because a decree was issued against

## 6

the entirety of the people of Israel, and because of the decree, there is no difference between a righteous person and a wicked person (similar to Rashi’s explanation above, "Once permission is given to the destroyer to destroy, he does not distinguish between the righteous and the wicked"); rather, because the Levites "are Mine (because) they did not err in the golden calf," Hashem removed them from the category of the decree by means of the change in the manner of their census.

And the connection between the prevention of the decree of the spies and "But the tribe of Levi you shall not count" is understood:
The decree of the spies applied to "all your counted ones, according to all your numbers" (without distinction between righteous and wicked) because a census provides a place for an undesirable matter (and one requires the giving of shekels ("a ransom for his soul") to save oneself from this). Therefore, when the wrath went out before Hashem and a decree was issued against the entirety of the people of Israel, "all your counted ones, according to all your numbers" were included in this.

And this is what Hashem said, "But the tribe of Levi you shall not count," so that "these would not be included," for since their census is not in the category of "counting" (*tifkod*), they are no longer associated with the plague that affects "all your counted ones."

G. From the "inner dimension of Torah" in this Rashi:
The difference between the two interpretations of Rashi mentioned above — that according to the first interpretation, the Levites were also counted like the children of Israel (only they were counted in a different manner and under different conditions), whereas according to the second interpretation, the Levites were not counted at all, because the census of the Levites is not in the category of "counting" — it can be said that they correspond to two levels in the superiority of the Levites over all of Israel.

This will be understood by first explaining the three aforementioned manners of the census — (a) the census of the children of Israel, (b) the census of the tribe of Levi "separately," (c) "the tribe of Levi you shall not count" — in the service of man:
The service of Hashem is divided into the three lines (pillars) of Torah, service, and acts of kindness, which are, in general, three types of service: (a) the fulfillment of *mitzvos*, (b) the study of Torah, (c) *dveikus* (cleaving) to Hashem (in prayer), the perfection of which is the service of *mesiras nefesh* (self-sacrifice).
*Mitzvos* were given in a number — specifically 613,

## 7

and we were commanded, "You shall not add... nor shall you subtract" [and likewise in every *mitzvah* itself — there is a measure and a limitation in the manner of its fulfillment],
because the essential matter of *mitzvos* is (as explained in *Tanya*), to draw down holiness into the physicality of the world (which is limited — it is said about it "enough"), which is why most of the *mitzvos* of the Torah are practical *mitzvos*, which are clothed in physical things and matters of this world — which are in measurement and limitation, the category of "number."
Torah (in its own right) is above measurement, as it is written, "Its measure is longer than the earth and wider than the sea" — above the physicality of the world, to the point that it is above the measurements and limitations of time and space, as our Sages said that anyone who engages in the study of Torah is as if he offered a burnt offering (without the limitations of time and space).
But on the other hand, from the very fact that it is said "longer than the earth in measure," it is understood that Torah is also in the category of "measure" (for regarding something that is not in the category of "measure," it is not appropriate to say that it is longer than "measure") — and especially since Torah is the source of the *mitzvos*, there must be a value between them.
[And similar to what is said in the *Haftorah* of our Parsha, "And the number of the children of Israel shall be, etc., which shall not be measured nor counted," that there are two matters here: "And the number of the children of Israel shall be," and on the other hand — it is impossible to count them, "for it shall not be counted (because of the multitude)"];
*Mesiras nefesh* is completely above any matter of measure and limit. In a simple sense, the movement of *mesiras nefesh* indicates *dveikus* to Hashem that has no limit or end, to the point that a person is ready and sacrifices his soul for Him, may He be blessed.

H. The three aforementioned services correspond to the three (general) types among the children of Israel — (a) (most) children of Israel, (b) the tribe of Levi, (c) the High Priest.
The conduct of (most) children of Israel is in the manner of "conduct yourselves with them in the manner of worldly custom," and their essential service is the fulfillment of *mitzvos* and the doing of good deeds (*mari ovdin tovin*);
The tribe of Levi, their matter is (in the language of the Rambam) — that "he was set apart to serve Hashem, to minister to Him, and to teach His righteous ways and His just judgments to the masses, as it is written, 'They shall teach Your judgments to Yaakov and Your Torah to Yisrael.' Therefore, they were set apart from the ways of the world, etc. And not only the tribe of Levi, but every single person... whose spirit moved him, etc., to set himself apart to stand before Hashem to serve Him, etc." — *mari Torah* (masters of Torah);
But from the very fact that their service is "to teach... to the masses," it is understood that they are not (completely) set apart from the rest of Israel who engage in the ways of the world, and on the contrary — "they shall teach" them, etc., and as is the simplicity of their conduct, that the Levites circulate in the cities of the children of Israel to receive their tithes, etc., and engage there in teaching the masses (though at special times they come to the Holy Temple and perform the service of the Temple);
Above all of them is the High Priest, who was commanded, "He shall not go out from the Temple," and he never leaves Jerusalem;
The matter of the High Priest is — to be in constant *dveikus* to Hashem, above measurement and limitation (*dveikus* that is drawn down and comes from the essence of his soul, the *yechidah* in the soul, which is unique with Him, may He be blessed, "the *yechidah* to unite You").
And from this is the distinction between these three types also regarding their census:
The counting of the children of Israel, whose (essential) service is in practical *mitzvos* which were given in a (precise) number, was in the manner of a census in the ways of nature, by Moshe and Aharon with "the prince of every tribe," who counted the shekels (physical) that were given by the children of Israel;
The tribe of Levi, whose essential matter is "they shall teach," Torah — which has both matters: it is in the category of number and together with this above the regular measurement and number (similar to "which shall not be counted because of the multitude") — was counted "by the word of Hashem," for since their service is at such a level that it is in the category of "number," therefore they were also counted, but not in a physical and natural census, for they are above the measurement of flesh and blood (for "it shall not be measured nor counted") but "by the word of Hashem."
However, regarding Aharon, it is stated in Rashi that "he was not in the census of the Levites," which the plain language indicates means he was not counted (and included) in the census of the Levites — for the service of Aharon the High Priest is the *dveikus* to Hashem that is above any category of measurement and number.

I. Based on all the above, the difference between the two interpretations in Rashi regarding the census of the Levites will be understood:
The first interpretation — "It is fitting for the King’s legion to be counted separately" — is in accordance with the superiority of the tribe of Levi in the matter of Torah, that because of this superiority, he is also counted, only that he is "counted separately" ("by the word of Hashem").
Whereas according to the second interpretation in Rashi, there is in the tribe of Levi (also) this superiority that they are not in the category of number — "you shall not count." And this superiority comes to them because "they did not err in the golden calf," meaning,

## 9

not only did they not sin in the golden calf, but furthermore, they were removed even from error in thought and conceptualization, due to their *dveikus* to Hashem, where there is no possibility of separation, etc.
And therefore — "they are Mine," for the Levites were acquired for Hashem in such a way that this connection, which is above measurement and limitation, is in them openly, and because of this superiority, they are above the category of number — "you shall not count."

J. The three manners in the census of the children of Israel mentioned above are relevant to, and are a parallel of, the time in which we read Parshas Bamidbar — at the end of the days of the *Sefirah*, "before *Atzeres*":
The explanation of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe is known regarding what is written, "You shall count fifty days" — even though we only count 49 days — that the fifty days of the *Sefirah* correspond to the fifty gates of *Binah*, and therefore in practice we only count 49 days, because the fiftieth gate is above the service of created beings, and it is impossible for them to count (and draw down) the fiftieth gate. Rather, after the created being reaches the perfection of his service (by means of counting the 49 days), the fiftieth gate is drawn down from above.
But in this very matter (in the drawing down of the fiftieth gate) — there are two levels:
1) A level (lower) in the fiftieth gate, which has a relationship and connection to the service and the counting of the 49 days, to the point that it is possible to say, and it is said, "You shall count fifty days," that the drawing down of the fiftieth gate is associated with and is a part of the counting of the children of Israel.
2) A level (higher) in the fiftieth gate, which does not relate at all to the service of created beings, and comes by way of a gift from above, and this is the matter (the essential one) of the festival of *Shavuos*, the time of the giving of our Torah, a drawing down by way of a gift.
And based on this, there are three matters here in accordance with the three aforementioned manners in "number": (a) the counting of the 49 days — the matter of number (which is by means of human beings), (b) the drawing down of the fiftieth gate from above which comes (after and) by means of the counting of the 49 days — in the category of number, but "it shall not be counted because of the multitude," (c) the fiftieth gate which comes completely by way of a gift is that which is not in the category of number (and counting) at all.
And this is the inner and true superiority that is revealed in the children of Israel on the festival of *Shavuos*, the time of the giving of our Torah — for then, in addition to the Torah being given anew, Hashem is revealed to the children of Israel "face to face," *dveikus* to Hashem in perfection (similar to the giving of the Torah the first time, where at every single word their souls departed) — "the *yechidah* to unite You."

(From the *Sichos* of Shabbos Parshas Bamidbar 5726, 5740)


## חג השבועות

## Shavuos

### 18

**A.** The Rambam writes: "It is a simple custom to read on the first day of the festival (of Atzeres) in the third month, and to recite the Haftorah of the Chariot" (and its source is in the Baraisa of Megillah, as stated in the Kesef Mishneh). This is also ruled as halacha in the Tur and Shulchan Aruch.

The reason for reciting the Chariot of Yechezkel as the Haftorah is explained by Rashi (and cited in the Shulchan Aruch of the Alter Rebbe): "Because He was revealed at Sinai with 'myriads of myriads, thousands of angels'." This is explained at greater length in the Levush: "We recite the Chariot of Yechezkel because on that day, at that chosen assembly, everyone attained the level of prophecy. All of them, young and old, heard 'I am' and 'You shall have no other' from the mouth of the Almighty. Without a doubt, everyone at that assembly perceived the Work of the Chariot just as Yechezkel perceived it, as it is written, 'And all the people saw the sounds,' which alludes to the Work of the Chariot. Therefore, we also recite the Work of the Chariot, as it is 'akin to the event'." The Radak on Yechezkel explicitly states in the name of our Sages: "For on the day of the Giving of the Torah, Yisrael saw the Chariot just as Yechezkel saw it, to the point that they said that the smallest among their women was greater than Yechezkel ben Buzi."

Seemingly, this reason (for the Haftorah of Yechezkel’s Chariot) requires explanation: The requirement that a Haftorah be "akin to the event" means, in its simple sense, that the Haftorah discusses (not a side issue of the event, but) the essence of the event. In the present case, the vision of the Chariot at the Giving of the Torah is not, on the face of it, the body and essence of the Giving of the Torah—which is the revelation of Hashem to all of Yisrael and the giving of the Torah to the Children of Yisrael.

Based on this, it would seemingly be more appropriate to read the Parsha of the Giving of the Torah on the first day ("which is the essence of the day on which the Torah was given"), and recite the Haftorah of Chavakuk, which "speaks of the Giving of the Torah: 'G-d comes from Teiman... in the Giving of the Torah (it speaks)'."

Moreover: Even if we were to say that this too was one of the essential matters of the Giving of the Torah—that the revelation of the Shechinah was with the Chariot, as the Levush writes (ibid.), "that on that day, at that assembly... everyone attained the level of prophecy... the Work of the Chariot"—the primary emphasis is surely not on the vision of the Chariot, but on the revelation of the (glory of) Hashem, and the fact that everyone heard "I am" and "You shall have no other" from the mouth of the Almighty. Especially since it is stated by our Sages that from the vision of the Chariot, the sin of the Golden Calf, G-d forbid, subsequently branched out.

And even if we were to say that the Haftorah must be about the Chariot, it would seemingly be more appropriate to recite the Haftorah of Yeshayah, the Chariot that Yeshayah saw, for there the emphasis is on the vision of the glory of Hashem: "And I saw the L-rd sitting on a throne, high and exalted, and His train filled the Temple; seraphim were standing, etc.," which is a summary of the Chariot (as is indeed the practice to recite the Chariot of Yeshayah on Parshas Yisro). This is unlike Yechezkel, which contains length and details regarding the Chariot, and only a brief mention of Hashem.

Furthermore: The revelation of the glory of Hashem at the Giving of the Torah is not comparable to the revelation of the glory of Hashem in the Chariot of Yechezkel, but is more similar to the prophecy of Yeshayah, and even more so, to the level of Moshe, the greatest of the prophets, as it is written: "Face to face Hashem spoke with you on the mountain" (and similarly, as mentioned above, we heard "I am" and "You shall have no other" from the mouth of the Almighty).

According to this, the words of the Radak mentioned above—that at the Giving of the Torah there was a revelation of the Chariot of Yechezkel—require explanation. Since there was a revelation of the (glory of) Hashem that is infinitely higher than that, what is the point of a candle in the daylight?

There is also a need to explain the law itself, that "we recite the Chariot," even though "one may not expound... on the Chariot (even) alone, unless he is a sage and understands from his own knowledge." For this reason, "in some places, the custom is that a great and wise person reads this Haftorah, and support for this is: 'And not on the Work of the Chariot, unless he is a sage and understands from his own knowledge'." Some even hold that one should not recite the Chariot at all, "lest he come to inquire and contemplate it."

The Tiferes Yisrael explains the reason for this: "The reason we are not concerned (that he might come to contemplate it) is, it seems to me, that in any case, if he is a sage, he knows that these matters are beyond the heart of the intellectual to grasp, and his silence is better than his speech. And if he is not of that caliber, then 'even a fool, when he is silent, is counted wise,' and he will remain silent in his foolishness and will not ask about these matters, knowing that he is not worthy of receiving any answer."

But even according to this, it requires explanation: What is the benefit of reading this matter in public? The intention of the reading is not only for the select few whom Hashem has graced with wisdom and who understand the Chariot, but for everyone in the synagogue who hears the reading and the Haftorah—most of whom are not on the level of a "sage who understands from his own knowledge" regarding the understanding of the Work of the Chariot.

We must therefore conclude that there is a special element in the Chariot of Yechezkel that highlights the innovation of the Giving of the Torah (which is why there was a revelation of the Chariot at the time of the Giving of the Torah). Therefore, this Haftorah is "akin to the event," the general innovation of the Giving of the Torah (and even more so than the prophecy of the Chariot of Yeshayah). This special element in the Chariot of Yechezkel (in which it is "akin to the event" of the Giving of the Torah) is something that every Jew can understand (even though the details of the matters in the Chariot of Yechezkel are sealed and are not within the understanding of ordinary people, as "the depth of the Work of the Chariot... is far from the knowledge of the masses").

### 20

**B.** The explanation for this is as follows:
The Gemara explains the difference between the Chariot that Yeshayah saw and the Chariot that Yechezkel saw: "Everything that Yechezkel saw, Yeshayah saw. To what is Yechezkel comparable? To a villager who saw the King. And to what is Yeshayah comparable? To a city-dweller who saw the King."

According to the simple meaning, the difference between them is not in what they saw, but in what they related. Yeshayah "did not care to explain everything, for he was a prince and grew up in the palace, and a city-dweller who sees the King is not startled, nor amazed, nor does he care to relate it." But from the very fact that Yeshayah is described as a "city-dweller" who was a prince and grew up in the palace, while Yechezkel is a "villager" whose place is not in the King's palace, it is understood that the prophecy of Yeshayah is higher and more complete. As our Sages say, Yeshayah is called "the greatest of the prophets," and the level of his prophecy and perception was higher than the perception of Yechezkel.

It can be said that the difference is highlighted by the very fact that in the words of Yechezkel, all the details of the Chariot are set forth and specified, whereas with Yeshayah, the emphasis is primarily on the vision of the King Himself (and not the details of the Chariot). This fits the simple distinction between a villager who saw the King and a city-dweller who saw the King: A city-dweller who sees the King, since he grew up in the King's palace, has some perception of the King's greatness. Therefore, seeing (the many details of) the King's Chariot and the like does not add to his sense of wonder at the King's majesty; he is only moved by seeing the King Himself. This is not the case for a villager who has no knowledge or perception of the King's greatness; when he sees the matters of His greatness and the magnitude of His royal splendor, this awakens in him an awe of the King's greatness (even though even then he has no perception of the essence of the King's greatness).

Such is the difference between Yeshayah and Yechezkel: The prophecy of Yeshayah, "the greatest of the prophets," was like that of a city-dweller who has a perception of the King's greatness, and therefore the greatness of the King's Chariot does not occupy a place for him. This is not the case for Yechezkel, whose primary vision was of the Chariot, but this vision brought him to some knowledge and awe of the King Himself.

This must be reconciled with what is explained in the book *Avodas HaKodesh* regarding the Chariot of Yechezkel: "From the fact that he saw and perceived the lower chariot, he knew of the higher one... for there is a throne above a throne, a chariot above a chariot, a rider above a rider, and glory above glory. From the fact that he saw four living creatures below, he knew and recognized that there is their counterpart above in the true Unity, for so he had received. And from the fact that he saw below the *chashmal* surrounding the throne, called the outer *chashmal*, he knew that there is its counterpart above, the inner *chashmal*, etc. In this way, everything he saw below, he knew had its counterpart above." He continues to explain (in *Avodas HaKodesh*) regarding all the verses, how they speak of the lower and allude to the higher.

In other words: The content and essence of the Chariot of Yechezkel is that the matters below are the "image of the higher ones." Therefore, (Yechezkel) specifies and elaborates on all the details of the Chariot and its form, and emphasizes: "And from within it, the image of, etc... the image of a man... and the image of their faces... and the image of the living creatures, etc."

It can be said that this is the precision of the term "image" (*dmus*), which points to a thing and its opposite: On one hand, seeing the image indicates that he does not see the thing itself, but only its "image," like someone looking into a mirror who sees only the image of the object. On the other hand, "image" indicates that it has no reality other than being the image of the thing. This means that not only is it possible to know exactly how the thing itself appears by seeing the image, but more than that—the entire "reality" of the "image" is not a reality at all, except that it is similar to the thing.

This is what Yechezkel saw in all the details of the matters below: that their entire essence is this, and only this, that they are the "image of the higher ones."

This is not the case with Yeshayah, who, being a "city-dweller," does not explain and specify all the details of the Chariot. For from the outset, he did not see the "image" as it is below, but the essence of the thing above. Because he was a city-dweller who grew up in the palace, all matters other than the King did not occupy a place for him, to the point that they were not felt by him as a reality. Therefore, he says, "And I saw the L-rd sitting on a throne, high and exalted," and specifies only the seraphim who are "standing above Him," meaning that this is a detail in the vision of "the L-rd sitting on a throne."

### 22

**C.** Based on this, the connection of the Haftorah of the Chariot of Yechezkel to the reading of the Parsha of the Giving of the Torah on the day the Torah was given is understood—because it is of the essence of the day and "akin to the event," meaning the content of the innovation of the Giving of the Torah:

The revelation of the Shechinah at the Giving of the Torah was not just a temporary matter, but was intended so that through it, the Children of Yisrael would elevate themselves through their *avodah* and cleave to the Holy One, blessed be He. The strength for this came to them from the revelation of Hashem at the Giving of the Torah, through which the standing at Mount Sinai was fixed in our hearts forever, that our eyes saw and not a stranger, etc.

This is also the content of the words of the Midrash, that the descent of Hashem onto Mount Sinai was not just a temporary revelation, but the beginning of a completely new state for the world, through the permanent nullification of the decree that "the higher ones shall not descend to the lower ones, and the lower ones shall not ascend to the higher ones." The results of the nullification of this decree, in a revealed way, depend upon and are achieved through the *avodah* of Yisrael.

Explanation of the matter:
From the very fact that they are described by the names "higher ones" and "lower ones," it is understood that essentially there is a connection, and more than that—a bond between them. Especially according to what is explained in many places, that the reality of every creature below has its root and source above, from which it was branched out—that the root of the ox below is from the ox in the Chariot, and so on—up to its root and source, which is the word of Hashem and the breath of His mouth, which brings it into being and gives it life at every single moment. And its "growth" afterward also comes from its *mazal* above, as our Sages say: "There is no blade of grass... that does not have a *mazal*... that makes it grow."

However, before the Giving of the Torah, there was a "decree"—from the expression of cutting and severing—between the creature below and its source above. And (at the Giving of the Torah), when the decree was nullified, the power was given to everyone through his *avodah* to draw down and "see" in every creature below its root and source above, until he reveals through this the truth of its reality, that it exists only from the truth of His existence.

And since "all beginnings are difficult," there was at the Giving of the Torah (at which time "I am the beginner") a most lofty revelation [as it is written in the Zohar: "At that time, all the secrets of the Torah and all the secrets of the higher and lower worlds did not depart from them, because they saw eye to eye the splendor of the glory of their Master, which had not been the case from the day the world was created, when the Holy One, blessed be He, was revealed in His glory on Mount Sinai. And if you say, 'But we have learned that a maidservant saw at the Sea what Yechezkel the prophet did not see, can it be like that day when Yisrael stood on Mount Sinai?' It is not so, because on that day when Yisrael stood on Mount Sinai, the filth was removed from them"—for even at the time of the splitting of the Red Sea, "a maidservant saw at the Sea what Yechezkel the prophet did not see," and at the Giving of the Torah, there was even more than that]—but the primary intent for all eternity was the "Chariot of Yechezkel," to reveal in the world how the entire reality of all its details is nothing but an "image" of the reality of the detail above [and therefore there was a revelation of the Chariot at that time]. And that is why this matter was specifically fixed as the Haftorah for the day of the Giving of the Torah.

The question raised above is also answered—why was the Haftorah fixed as the Work of the Chariot, which cannot be understood by most people? Because even though the details of the matters of the Chariot that were specified in the Haftorah are not within understanding and perception as mentioned above, it is within the power of everyone to understand and grasp the general content of the Haftorah: that all the details of the Chariot that Yechezkel saw are an "image," through which Yechezkel knew and recognized the "image of the higher ones."

### 23

**D.** Based on this, one can also explain the reason for the custom "to conclude (this Haftorah) with the verse 'And a spirit lifted me up, etc.'" (skipping a chapter and a half), "even though it is not written at all regarding the Chariot." On the contrary, according to the simple meaning of the verses, the text speaks of the departure of the glory of Hashem after the speech—for in this verse ("And a spirit lifted me up, and I heard behind me a sound of a great rushing: 'Blessed be the glory of Hashem from His place'") the main innovation and content of the Chariot of Yechezkel mentioned above is emphasized.

The matter is:
One of the differences between the Chariot of Yeshayah and the Chariot of Yechezkel is that the Chariot of Yechezkel speaks of the living creatures (*chayos*) and the wheels (*ofanim*), and the Chariot of Yeshayah speaks of the seraphim. The *ofanim* and *chayos* perform their *avodah* with a "rushing" (*ra'ash*), as is known that this "sound of a great rushing" comes from the *ofanim*, as it is said in the blessings of the Shema: "And the *ofanim* and the holy living creatures, with a great rushing, rise up toward the seraphim; toward them they offer praise and say: 'Blessed be the glory of Hashem from His place'." This is not the case for the *avodah* of the seraphim, which is not with a rushing.

The reason for this is explained: The difference between the seraphim and the *ofanim* is like the difference between a city-dweller who saw the King and a villager who saw the King. The rushing comes from the aspect of innovation. Therefore, it is specifically with a villager who saw the King, that the vision of the King and His majesty causes him a rushing, due to the innovation of the matter. This is not the case for a city-dweller, for whom it is not (so much) of an innovation, and there is no rushing for him. The difference between the seraphim and the *ofanim* is similar.

More specifically:
The seraphim perceive how the Holy One, blessed be He, is holy and separate from the worlds, and the worlds do not occupy a place before Him. But the *ofanim* perceive how the Holy One, blessed be He, brings everything into being from nothing to something, and the reality of the world is formed from the truth of His existence. Because of this, their awe is with a great rushing, because they perceive the truth, that it is the opposite of what is seen by the eyes—that the world appears as a "something" and a thing, but in truth it is completely nullified. Therefore, the great rushing comes, like a person who attains a new perception that is the opposite of what is seen by the eyes, and therefore a rushing is created within him.

This is the content of the aforementioned innovation of the Giving of the Torah, that even in the reality of the world, in the lower ones, the truth of His existence will be recognizable.

### 24

**E.** Based on the above, one can also explain why at the Giving of the Torah there were "sounds and lightnings and a very strong sound of a shofar, and all the people who were in the camp trembled":

If we interpret it according to its simple meaning alone—that the intention and purpose of the sounds and lightnings was to effect awe and trembling in the world, in the Children of Yisrael—then seemingly the revelation of the glory of Hashem Himself, "You have seen that I have spoken to you from the heavens," is sufficient to effect this awe and trembling in them. On the contrary, the trembling from the sounds and lightnings, etc., is a trembling that belongs primarily to the body, whereas the awe and trembling from the revelation of the glory of Hashem face to face is a deep and great action, and it acts (also) upon the soul, and even upon the soul of the soul. And yet, were the sounds and lightnings, etc., a primary matter in the revelation of the Giving of the Torah?

Rather, these are the very things: the content of the "rushing" at the Giving of the Torah (the sounds and the rushing, etc., "and all the people who were in the camp trembled," and in the words of the verse, "the earth trembled") is not just a rushing heard by the physical ear, which was an additional detail in the preparation and introduction to the Giving of the Torah. Rather, it is a rushing in its spiritual content: due to the innovation of the Giving of the Torah, that in every detail of creation and in all of creation as a whole, it was seen that its true reality is not what is seen and revealed to the eyes of flesh—that it is a thing and a "something" in its own right—but that its source is that it was brought into being from the truth of the existence of the Creator.

### 25

**F.** Torah is from the word *hora'ah* (instruction), and all the more so when the day of the year arrives on which the Torah was given to Yisrael—for these days are "remembered and performed"—that every Jew must learn an instruction from the reading of the Torah and the Haftorah of the day of Shavuos. Based on the above, it can be said that there is an instruction here in two details: in his *avodah* within himself, and in his *avodah* with others:

Even though regarding the Work of the Chariot it is said, "One may not expound... unless he is a sage and understands from his own knowledge," this is specifically regarding the "Work of the Chariot" as it is in the Written Torah and the Oral Torah—written in the language of the secrets of the Torah (the details of the Chariot of Yechezkel, *Sifra D'Tzniusa*, etc.). But regarding the study of the inner dimension of the Torah as it was revealed by the Arizal and explained by him, and he said that the time has come that "it is a mitzvah to reveal this wisdom," then everyone is obligated in this. Especially after more time has passed—that it has been explained in the teachings of Chassidus, and especially after it has been explained in the language of the Torah sages, in a language of understanding and perception in a way that "they will be sustained," then this is a "lofty and exalted mitzvah... 'Know the G-d of your father,' etc., and it brings one to a whole heart."

Through this, one comes to see and recognize in every single thing its true reality, that it was brought into being from the truth of His existence.

Similarly, it is understood regarding the *avodah* with others, that the dissemination of Torah and Yiddishkeit among all of Yisrael must be done with a "sound of a great rushing."

On the contrary, when a person's *avodah* is still within, within the four cubits of Torah and holiness, sometimes the manner of *avodah* as it is, in a "still, small voice," is sufficient.

But when one goes outside of those four cubits, into the "roaring sound of Rome," then in order to nullify this sound, it is through the "sound of a great rushing" of holiness. Through this, one merits soon to "sound a great shofar"—the great shofar of the future, together with the "voice of the herald, the herald who says"—the herald of the Redemption, very soon.

## Chapter 2

### 26

A. Regarding the covenant established at the time of the Giving of the Torah, the Gemara states that our ancestors "entered the covenant only through circumcision, immersion, and the offering of blood (a sacrifice)... Circumcision, as it is written, 'For all the people who came out were circumcised.' The offering of blood, as it is written, 'And he sent the young men of the children of Israel.' Immersion, from where do we know it? As it is written, 'And Moses took the blood and sprinkled it upon the people,' and there is no sprinkling without immersion."

The verses "And he sent the young men of the children of Israel... And Moses took the blood, etc." were stated at the end of Parshas Mishpatim. The Tannaim in the Mechilta dispute the timing of this passage: According to the first Tanna, it was on the fifth of Sivan, at which time Moses built an altar, offered the sacrifices, and sprinkled the blood, etc. (This is also cited in the Gemara, Maseches Shabbos, and so writes Rashi in his commentary on the Torah). However, "Rabbi Yosi bar Yehuda says, 'All these acts were performed on that day,'" which, in its simple sense, means the day of the Giving of the Torah.

[The Ramban in his commentary on the Torah explains the words of Rabbi Yosi bar Yehuda, "All these acts were performed on that day," to mean, "That is to say, on that day, after the Giving of the Torah," i.e., on the seventh of Sivan. (This is in accordance with his view—and that of other commentators—that the passages of Yisro and Mishpatim were stated in order, and the laws of Parshas Mishpatim were stated on the day of the Giving of the Torah after the Ten Commandments. On that same day, the Jewish people said, "All that Hashem has spoken we will do," and on the following day, the seventh of Sivan, "Moses rose early in the morning and built an altar... and they sacrificed... and he took the Book of the Covenant... and they said, 'We will do and we will hear,' and Moses took the blood and sprinkled it upon the people, etc.")

However, in the Midrash Lekach Tov, it is explicitly stated that the view that "all these acts were performed on that day" refers to the morning of the day of the Giving of the Torah, before the Ten Commandments, and this is the simple interpretation of Rabbi Yosi's words, "on that day," implying the well-known day (and indeed, the day after the Giving of the Torah is not mentioned at all in the entire continuation of the Mechilta there).]

Seemingly, this view—that the immersion of the Jewish people (and the offering of blood) took place "on that day," the day of the Giving of the Torah—requires explanation: How were the Jewish people permitted to immerse for the purpose of conversion on that day? After all, our Sages have said (in Maseches Shabbos) that "everyone agrees that the Torah was given to the Jewish people on Shabbos," and it is a settled ruling in the Rambam and the Shulchan Aruch that one does not immerse a convert on Shabbos (and they had already been commanded regarding Shabbos at Marah or at Alush)?

[We find in the commentators on the Mechilta that they raised a difficulty (regarding the words of Rabbi Yosi bar Yehuda): How could he (build an altar and) offer sacrifices on Shabbos? They answered that Rabbi Yosi bar Yehuda holds that the burnt offerings were the *tamid* offerings, and the *tamid* overrides Shabbos. But it remains difficult regarding (the building of the altar and) the immersion of the Jewish people (for conversion), as this is not a matter of a communal sacrifice.]

Simply put, one could say that Rabbi Yosi bar Yehuda (in the Mechilta) follows the view of the Mechilta (in another place) that the sixth of Sivan fell on a Friday (and this is also explicitly stated in Pirkei D'Rabbi Eliezer that the Giving of the Torah was on a Friday)—and not like the view of the Gemara (that everyone agrees the Torah was given on Shabbos). But in the Midrash Lekach Tov there, it is explicitly stated: "On Shabbos they received the Ten Commandments... and Moses rose early in the morning and built an altar, etc., for the Jewish people did not receive the Torah except through immersion and sprinkling... and all these acts were performed on that day." Thus, it is explicitly stated that "all these acts" were performed on the day of Shabbos.

## 28

B. Seemingly, one could answer that the prohibition against immersing a convert on Shabbos is only a *shevus* (rabbinic prohibition)—for two reasons have been given for the prohibition of immersion for conversion on Shabbos: 1) In the Gemara, it is stated because "we do not perform 'repairs' to a person on Shabbos." 2) The Rambam writes that "since the matter (the immersion of a convert) requires a Beis Din, one does not immerse him on Shabbos"—and both reasons (repairing a person and the prohibition of holding court on Shabbos) are *shevus* prohibitions of rabbinic origin.

However, there is still a strain in this, for it is not reasonable to say that the entry of the Jewish people into the covenant would be through an act (that is the opposite) which contains a trace of prohibition, of any kind (even a *shevus* prohibition), for this is like a thing and its opposite.

[And even though we are speaking of a prohibition that is only a rabbinic decree—our Sages have said that "Abraham our forefather kept the entire Torah... even the *eruvei tavshilin*" ("which is... only a rabbinic enactment that they would establish in the future"), and Rashi in his commentary on the Torah notes that Abraham was careful even regarding "a *shevus* for Shabbos." From this, it is understood that the act of *shevus* itself (even before this decree, before the Giving of the Torah) contains a blemish (a trace of prohibition?)].

Furthermore, it is even more difficult according to what is written in the commentators, that according to the view that circumcision on Shabbos is prohibited because of the labor of "finishing an object" (striking with a hammer), since through circumcision "the child is made a complete Jew"—then the immersion of a convert on Shabbos is also prohibited by Torah law. According to this, it would follow that the immersion of the Jewish people at that time for the purpose of conversion involved a labor prohibited by Torah law.

## 29

C. One can say regarding this, by first noting what must be emphasized in the statement of our Sages that the entry into the covenant is "through circumcision, immersion, and the offering of blood." Seemingly, why did they not also list the acceptance of the mitzvos, which is a fundamental matter in conversion? On the contrary, it is the essence of conversion, as is ruled in the Shulchan Aruch that if there was no acceptance of mitzvos before three (and during the day), it invalidates the conversion (unlike immersion, which according to many opinions does not invalidate if it was not before three).

However, the explanation for this is simple: The acceptance of mitzvos is not (merely) one of the things that brings a person into the covenant; rather, it *is* (part of) the covenant itself. This is as the Rambam precisely phrases it: "In three things did the Jewish people enter the covenant... and likewise for future generations, when a gentile wishes to enter the covenant and take shelter under the wings of the Shechinah and accept upon himself the yoke of Torah, he requires circumcision, immersion, and the offering of a sacrifice." Thus, the matter of "accepting upon himself the yoke of Torah" is part of the desire "to enter the covenant."

From this, it is understood simply why the acceptance of mitzvos is not listed among the conditions of conversion: because when there is no acceptance of mitzvos, it is not a deficiency in the conditions of conversion, but rather there is no conversion here at all. Only after he wishes "to enter and take shelter under the wings of the Shechinah and accept upon himself the yoke of Torah" does the status of conversion take effect through circumcision and immersion. Conversely, if he did not precede it with the acceptance of the yoke of Torah and mitzvos, there is no act of conversion.

Based on this, one can say that the conversion of the Jewish people at the time of the Giving of the Torah is distinct from conversion after the Giving of the Torah: After the Giving of the Torah, since the three things come after the acceptance of mitzvos, they actually bring him into the covenant and make him a convert. This is not the case with the conversion of the Jewish people at the Giving of the Torah, for before the Giving of the Torah (the statement of the Ten Commandments), they had not yet had the acceptance of mitzvos [for even though they said, "All that Hashem has spoken we will do... we will do and we will hear," this statement is not considered an act of acceptance of mitzvos regarding the mitzvos after the Giving of the Torah, as will be explained in section D]. Thus, through their immersion and the offering of blood, they had not yet entered the covenant in practice, and their conversion was not completed until the statement of the Ten Commandments, at which time their acceptance of mitzvos occurred (as will be explained in section E).

[And what our Sages said, that our ancestors "did not enter the covenant except through circumcision, immersion, and the offering of blood," does not mean that immediately after performing these three things they entered the covenant, but rather that for their entry into the covenant they required these three things (and without them, their acceptance of mitzvos would not have been effective for entering the covenant). Therefore, it is learned from this for future generations that "they shall not enter the covenant except through circumcision, immersion, and the offering of blood." However, the conversion at that time and the entry into the covenant in practice took place at the time of the Giving of the Torah itself.]

This is also the reason why there was no prohibition at that time to immerse on Shabbos for the purpose of conversion—for only in conversion after the Giving of the Torah, when the immersion completes the conversion and through it they enter the covenant in practice, is it prohibited because of "repairing a person." This is not the case with the immersion of the Jewish people before the Giving of the Torah, since the conversion was not completed through it; there is no reason to prohibit this immersion on Shabbos because of "repairing a person."

And the reason of the Rambam (mentioned in section B)—that one does not immerse a convert on Shabbos because "the matter requires a Beis Din"—is also not applicable to the conversion of the Jewish people at the time of the Giving of the Torah. For only in conversion after the Giving of the Torah, when the circumcision and immersion, etc., come after the acceptance of mitzvos and they effect the conversion and complete it, are they in the category of a "judgment" that requires a Beis Din. This is not the case with the conversion of the Jewish people at the Giving of the Torah, since through the immersion (and the offering of blood) the status of conversion had not yet taken effect, the immersion (and the offering of blood) is not in the category of a "judgment" that requires a Beis Din.

## 30

D. Explaining the matter:

Regarding the interpretation of the verse, "And (Moses) recounted to the people all the words of Hashem and all the judgments, and all the people answered with one voice and said, 'All the words which Hashem has spoken we will do,'" Rashi writes (based on the Mechilta) that this refers to the mitzvos of separation and limitation, the seven mitzvos that the children of Noah were commanded, and the mitzvos that were commanded at Marah (Shabbos, honoring father and mother, the Red Heifer, and civil laws). Similarly, regarding the verse, "And he took the Book of the Covenant and read in the ears of the people, and they said, 'All that Hashem has spoken we will do and we will hear,'" Rashi explains that the "Book of the Covenant" refers to "from Bereishis until the Giving of the Torah and the mitzvos that were commanded at Marah."

Now, the acceptance of the seven mitzvos of the children of Noah is certainly not in the category of the acceptance of the mitzvos of the Jewish people (the 613 mitzvos), and even the acceptance of the mitzvos that were commanded at Marah is questionable as to whether it is sufficient to be considered the acceptance of mitzvos. For even though one does not need to inform a convert of all 613 mitzvos (it is sufficient to inform him of "the fundamentals of the religion, which are the Oneness of Hashem and the prohibition of idolatry... some light mitzvos and some stringent mitzvos")—he is nevertheless obligated to accept upon himself all the mitzvos of the Torah, for if he accepted upon himself the entire Torah except for one thing, he is not a convert. If so, even at Marah, in the acceptance of the mitzvos commanded at Marah, there is no acceptance of the entire Torah. This remains to be further studied.

It seems one can say that even according to the simple reading of the Gemara’s discussion that the statement of "we will do and we will hear" was the acceptance of the entire Torah (that they accepted it before they heard it, the precedence of "we will do" to "we will hear")—it is impossible to consider this statement made before the Giving of the Torah as the category of "acceptance of mitzvos" (which is necessary for conversion).

By first noting the reason for the order of the acceptance of conversion for future generations, that one must inform him of "some stringent mitzvos... and inform him of the punishment for the mitzvos," the need for this information is simply because (also) if we do not inform him of some of the stringent ones, as well as the punishment for the mitzvos, there is a deficiency in his acceptance of mitzvos (for it is possible that when he learns about the stringent mitzvos or their punishment, he might regret it, etc.).

Similarly, one can say regarding our case, that as long as the Torah was not given at Sinai from the mouth of the Almighty, it is impossible to have an acceptance of mitzvos that are *after* the Giving of the Torah.

For since the mitzvos before that are not in the category of the mitzvos after the Giving of the Torah, and it is understood that the severity of the mitzvos that the Patriarchs kept on their own is not at all comparable to the severity of the mitzvos after they were commanded regarding them. Similarly, the severity of the commands that the Jewish people were commanded by Moses before the Giving of the Torah is not at all comparable to the severity of the commands after the Giving of the Torah, at which time they heard the Ten Commandments from the mouth of the Almighty,

[As the Rambam says, "Everything that we refrain from or do today, we do only by the command of the Holy One, blessed be He, through Moses our teacher, peace be upon him... from Sinai"—and not because "the Holy One, blessed be He, said this to the prophets before him." For even though those were also commands of the Holy One, blessed be He, to the prophets, nevertheless, it is not at all comparable to the commands of the Holy One, blessed be He, at Sinai.]

Therefore, any matter of "acceptance of mitzvos" before hearing the Ten Commandments from the mouth of the Almighty cannot be considered as the "acceptance of mitzvos" after the Giving of the Torah. For before the authority of the Torah and mitzvos after the Giving of the Torah (which are "from the mouth of the Almighty") existed in the world, it is not possible to have an (authentic) acceptance of them, as it is a thing that had not yet come into existence (and they had no concept of such a type of command).

[And even though informing him of the (reward of) the mitzvos and their punishment is not a condition that invalidates the conversion—one must distinguish between informing him after the Torah and mitzvos have already been given to the Jewish people and it is possible for him to know them, but the Beis Din did not inform this convert who is coming to convert (and especially since in this case one can say that since he came to convert, we rely on the fact that he investigated and inquired and knows on his own the nature of the mitzvos that he will be obligated in when he converts); but before the Torah was given, when there is still no reality of Torah and mitzvos (with the authority of after the Giving of the Torah) in the world, there is no possibility for a category of "acceptance" regarding it, since it is a thing that had not yet come into existence, as mentioned above.]

Based on this, an additional explanation is added to the view of the commentators (mentioned in section A) that this entire passage (at the end of Parshas Mishpatim) was stated after the Giving of the Torah—for seemingly, the appropriate time for the acceptance of mitzvos is before the Giving of the Torah (that when they agree to accept upon themselves the yoke of mitzvos, the Torah will be given to them)?

And according to the above, it is understood that, on the contrary, that is the very reason: Since the content of the passage is the establishing of a covenant regarding the fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos, it holds that this matter is possible only after the Giving of the Torah and the hearing of the Ten Commandments from the mouth of the Almighty. This is not the case before that, as a covenant regarding Torah and mitzvos—which are in the category of a thing that had not yet come into existence—is not possible (since everything they knew and were commanded before the Giving of the Torah is not at all in the same category as the mitzvos after the Giving of the Torah).

## 32

E. Therefore, it is reasonable to say that the essence of the acceptance of mitzvos (through which the Jewish people entered the covenant) was at the time of hearing the Ten Commandments, at which time the Holy One, blessed be He, made known the Oneness of Hashem and the prohibition of idolatry—"I am" and "You shall have no other gods" [with their punishment—"a jealous G-d, etc."] and the prohibition of Shabbos and others of the stringent mitzvos (which carry the penalty of death), and also some of the light ones.

Based on this, the difficulty of the Tosafos regarding the statement of our Sages that "He overturned the mountain over them like a cask" (and therefore, "from here there is a great protest against the Torah," that the Jewish people have a defense regarding this, that they did not keep the Torah, "because they accepted it under duress") is simply answered. For "they had already preceded 'we will do' to 'we will hear'"—and according to the above, one can say that the statement of "we will do and we will hear" does not have the legal status of "acceptance of mitzvos," and the acceptance of the mitzvos of the Jewish people was at the time of the Ten Commandments, and at that time it was in the manner of "He overturned the mountain over them like a cask."

This is also the explanation for the view of Rabbi Yosi bar Yehuda mentioned above, that their immersion for the purpose of conversion was on the day of Shabbos—for this act of immersion of the Jewish people did not have the effect of entry into the covenant, since there had not yet been an acceptance of mitzvos. The actual entry into the covenant and the effect of conversion were brought about afterward by the act of the Holy One, blessed be He (by the statement of the Ten Commandments, as mentioned above), at which time the acceptance of Torah and mitzvos by the Jewish people occurred.

F. One should add a source for the matter mentioned (that the conversion of the Giving of the Torah was effected only at the time of the Ten Commandments) in the words of the Midrash, that "when the Holy One, blessed be He, created the world, He decreed and said, 'The heavens are the heavens of Hashem, and the earth He gave to the children of man.' When He wished to give the Torah, He annulled the first decree and said, 'The lower beings shall ascend to the upper ones, and the upper ones shall descend to the lower ones, and I am the beginner,' as it is said, 'And Hashem descended upon Mount Sinai.'"

From the words of the Midrash, it is understood that even though we find the revelation of Hashem to the righteous and the prophets before the Giving of the Torah, nevertheless, since it was before the annulment of the "first decree," it is considered that the "upper ones" (the Shechinah) had not yet descended below. As is explained in several places, before the Giving of the Torah there was no perfection in the revelation of the Holy One, blessed be He, and only at the time of the Giving of the Torah was there the revelation of "I am Hashem your G-d," and therefore only then was the decree annulled that the upper ones would not descend to the lower ones.

From this, it is also understood regarding Moses our teacher himself, that even though the Holy One, blessed be He, was revealed to him in the burning bush and elsewhere, nevertheless, it is not at all in the same category of "revelation of Hashem" as it was at the time of the descent of Hashem upon Mount Sinai and the statement of the Ten Commandments.

With this, one can explain why the Giving of the Torah is considered as the conversion of the Jewish people, for the matter of conversion is that the convert becomes a new reality, "like a child newly born." Seemingly, at the time of the Exodus from Egypt, it was already said, "to take for Himself a nation from within a nation," and it is even more difficult according to the early authorities who hold that the Patriarchs had already left the category of the children of Noah and had the status of Jews, and one cannot say that at the Giving of the Torah was the conversion of the Jewish people.

Based on the above, it is explained that even though they were already connected to Hashem before the Giving of the Torah, the manner of the revelation of the Holy One, blessed be He, to all the Jewish people at the time of the Giving of the Torah worked in them a complete renewal, until they became like a truly new reality, "like a child newly born."

This is also the difference between the mitzvos before the Giving of the Torah and the Torah and mitzvos that were given to us at Sinai, for this revelation of the Holy One, blessed be He, was "given" in the Torah and mitzvos, in such a way that by a Jewish person studying Torah and keeping mitzvos, he connects and joins with the Essence of the Holy One, blessed be He, and in the language of our Sages, that through Torah "you take Me."

(From the sichos of the second day of Shavuos and Parshas Naso, 5739)

## 34

### Sicha 3

A. The Rambam states at the end of *Hilchos Sefer Torah*: "Anyone who sits before a *Sefer Torah* must sit with *koved rosh*, with *eimah* and *pachad*, for it is the faithful witness to all the inhabitants of the world, as it is said, 'And it shall be there as a witness for you,' and he should honor it, etc."

We find the content of the *din* of "eimah and pachad" before a *Sefer Torah* in the *Yerushalmi* — where Rabbi Shimon bar Yosi said (when he saw someone standing and translating the Torah while leaning on a pillar), "Just as it was given with *eimah* and *yirah*, so too must we conduct ourselves with it with *eimah* and *yirah*."

However, the Rambam does not cite this reason, "Just as it was given, etc." Simply put, we can say that this *din* in the Rambam is not the same *din* as in the *Yerushalmi*:

What is stated in the *Yerushalmi*, "We must conduct ourselves with it with *eimah* and *yirah*," refers to the time of the *krias haTorah* and its translation [similar to what is taught in the *Bavli*, that the study of Torah must be with *eimah* — "Just as there it was with *eimah* (as it is written, 'And the people saw and trembled, etc.' Rashi) and with *yirah*, with *retet* and with *zie*, so too here it must be with *eimah*, *yirah*, *retet*, and *zie*"]. This *din* is indeed brought by the Rambam earlier (in *Hilchos Tefillah*): "And the *turgeman* (translator) should not lean on a pillar or a beam, but should stand with *eimah* and *yirah*." In contrast, the Rambam here is discussing the obligation of *koved rosh*, *eimah*, and *pachad* not (only) while one is reading or studying from the *Sefer Torah*, but regarding "anyone who sits before a *Sefer Torah*." This means that even sitting before a *Sefer Torah* must be with *koved rosh*, etc. Therefore, he did not write, "Just as it was given, etc., so too must one sit with *eimah*, etc.," but rather provided a different reason.

However, the matter is still not fully resolved:
The content of this chapter in the Rambam concerns the *dinim* of honoring a *Sefer Torah* and the extreme caution not to treat it in a manner of disrespect, *chas v'shalom*. If so, what is the unique innovation in this *din* (that sitting before a *Sefer Torah* must be with "koved rosh, eimah, and pachad") that necessitated the Rambam to provide a new and specific reason — "for it is the faithful witness, etc."?

Furthermore: In the preceding *halacha*, the Rambam writes, "It is a *mitzvah* to designate a place for a *Sefer Torah* and to honor and beautify it exceedingly; the things that are in the *Luchos HaBris* are the very same things that are in every single *Sefer Torah*; a person should not spit toward a *Sefer Torah*, etc., and should not turn his back to a *Sefer Torah*, etc." — thus, the Rambam has already written that the reason for honoring a *Sefer Torah* ("to honor and beautify it exceedingly") pertains to the "things that are in the *Luchos HaBris*... that are in every *Sefer*." What is the addition here in the fact that a *Sefer Torah* "is the faithful witness to all the inhabitants of the world," such that precisely because of this, one must sit before a *Sefer Torah* with "koved rosh, eimah, and pachad"?

B. One may also ask, seemingly, regarding the statement that a *Sefer Torah* "is the faithful witness to all the inhabitants of the world":
We find the concept of a "witness" also regarding the *mitzvah* of *Tefillin*, of which it is said, "And it shall be for you as a sign upon your hand" (and the Rambam brings this earlier in *Hilchos Tefillin*), for the *os* (sign) implies a witness [and some say that *Tefillin* is "a term of *vikuch* (argument/proof)... because they are a witness and proof that the Name is called upon him and one is in awe of Him, as we say later, 'And all the peoples of the earth shall see that the Name of Hashem is called upon you, etc.' — these are the *Tefillin* that are on the head"]. Nevertheless, the Rambam does not write that the "sign" and the witness cause special *dinim* regarding sitting before *Tefillin*.

Admittedly, we find in the Rambam an elaboration on the *dinim* of the sanctity of *Tefillin* (in addition to the *dinim* of caution against disgracing them), and he writes there, "The sanctity of *Tefillin* is a great sanctity, etc." But this applies when one is fulfilling the *mitzvah* (when the *Tefillin* are worn) — "that as long as the *Tefillin* are on a person's head and on his arm, he is humble and a *yirei Shamayim* and is not drawn to mockery, idle chatter, or evil thoughts, etc.; therefore, a person should strive to have them on all day." However, he does not write: 1) that there is a special *din* regarding the caution of honoring them (even) when they are not on him, 2) that this *din* is for the reason that they are a sign and a witness.

This requires significant explanation: What is different about a *Sefer Torah*, that its being "the faithful witness, etc." necessitates a conduct of "sitting with *koved rosh, etc.*" even when one is not occupied with it?

C. It can be explained that the clarification of all this lies in the precision of the Rambam's language: "Anyone who sits before a *Sefer Torah* must sit with *koved rosh, etc.*" — for seemingly, he should have used the concise language, "And one should sit before a *Sefer Torah* with *koved rosh, etc.*" Why the wordiness, "Anyone who sits before a *Sefer Torah* must sit, etc."?

It appears that with this, the Rambam emphasizes the innovation in this *din* relative to the *dinim* of honoring a *Sefer Torah* mentioned earlier (in this chapter):
The matter of honoring a *Sefer Torah* (simply put) is the obligation that falls upon the person's (conduct) in relation to the *Sefer Torah*: Since a *Sefer Torah* possesses sanctity, etc., this obligates him to conduct himself in a manner of honor, to honor and beautify it, and not to spit toward a *Sefer Torah*, etc. This is the content of the preceding *dinim* — the details of the *dinim* of honor and the modes of conduct toward a *Sefer Torah*.

In contrast, here, the discussion is not about the "*koved rosh, etc.*" that "follows" from the obligations of the person in his conduct and approach to the *Sefer Torah*, but rather, on the contrary — the "*koved rosh, etc.*" that the *Sefer Torah* itself awakens and activates in the person. For even when the discussion is about a time (when he is not standing to be occupied in any way with the *Sefer Torah*, but rather) he is simply "sitting before a *Sefer Torah*," this alone must activate in him that he "sit with *koved rosh, eimah, and pachad*."

Therefore, the Rambam is precise: "Anyone who sits before a *Sefer Torah* must sit with *koved rosh, eimah, and pachad*," and he did not write concisely, "And one must sit before a *Sefer Torah* with *koved rosh, etc.*" (similar to the language of the *Yerushalmi* mentioned above, "We must conduct ourselves with it with *eimah* and *yirah*") — because his intent here is not the obligation of the person's conduct to honor the Torah, but rather regarding the effect of the Torah upon the person (in "anyone who sits before a *Sefer Torah*").

[And based on this, the continuation of the Rambam's words is also understood, that after this *din*, he continues, "And he should honor it according to his ability, etc. Anyone who honors the Torah, his body is honored by people, etc." — which also refers to the effect of the Torah upon the person, that through the Torah, "his body is honored by people," as will be discussed in section 8].

D. And therefore, the Rambam was necessitated to provide a new and specific reason — "for it is the faithful witness, etc.":
Regarding *Tefillin* as well, the Rambam brought (as mentioned above) the effect of the *Tefillin* upon the person wearing them — that through this, "he is humble and a *yirei Shamayim* and is not drawn to mockery, etc." But there, there is no need to provide a special reason for this, since the discussion is about the time when the *Tefillin* are on the person; when a person fulfills the *mitzvah* of *Tefillin* and places them on his body, it is self-understood that the great and severe sanctity of the *Tefillin* acts upon the person wearing them.

In contrast, regarding a *Sefer Torah*, where the Rambam is precise to say "anyone who sits before a *Sefer Torah*" — even though he is not occupied with the Torah — he must sit with *koved rosh, etc.*, meaning that the presence of the *Sefer Torah* before the person must activate in him that he sit with "koved rosh, eimah, and pachad." And what is the power of this unique effect of the *Sefer Torah*?
