To this, the Rambam explains that the *Sefer Torah* "is the faithful witness to all the inhabitants of the world, as it is said, 'And it shall be there as a witness for you.'"

And the explanation of this:
The Rambam is precise here that the *Sefer Torah* "is the faithful witness to all the inhabitants of the world" — and seemingly, the simple meaning of the continuation of the verses implies that what is stated, "And it shall be there as a witness for you," means that the *Sefer Torah* is a witness among Israel, a witness and a warning to *Bnei Yisrael* regarding the observance of the Torah and *mitzvos*.

From this, it is understood that the Rambam's intent regarding the "witness" in the *Sefer Torah* is (not as it seemingly appears from the simple meaning of the verse "And it shall be there as a witness for you," a witness and warning to *Bnei Yisrael* regarding the observance of the Torah and *mitzvos* — but rather) a witness to the fact that "He chose us from all the nations and gave us His Torah." That is to say: the reality of the *Sefer Torah* is a "witness" to Hashem's choosing of the Jewish people. Therefore, when a Jewish person sits before a *Sefer Torah*, this knowledge that the *Sefer Torah* is a "witness" must activate in him *koved rosh*, *eimah*, and *pachad* before Hashem, who "chose us from all the nations" to be His treasured people, etc.

And this is also the innovation in the witness of the *Sefer Torah* relative to the sign of *Tefillin*:
Regarding the sign of *Tefillin*, Rashi writes, "That Israel must establish a sign upon themselves to recognize that they hold fast to the Torah of Hashem." Therefore, it is connected specifically with the act of wearing *Tefillin*, which is specifically by day and not by night, and not on *Shabbos* and *Yom Tov*, and it does not apply to women and children. Therefore, the *din* of sitting with *koved rosh, eimah, and pachad* due to the *Tefillin* itself does not apply.

However, the witness of the *Sefer Torah* is not due to the person's action, his reading in the Torah, and the like, but rather the *cheftza* (object) of the *Sefer Torah* itself "is the faithful witness to all the inhabitants of the world," that *Bnei Yisrael* are distinct from the other nations in that they are the people whom Hashem chose. Therefore, this applies at all times and in all moments and to every single one of Israel, as per the precision of the Rambam's language, "Anyone who sits, etc." For this distinction of "You have chosen us from all the nations... and exalted us from all the tongues" includes every single one of *Bnei Yisrael* without any distinction at all.

E. Based on this, the difference between the Rambam's words here, "for it is the faithful witness, etc.," and what he wrote in the preceding *halacha*, "It is a *mitzvah* to designate a place for a *Sefer Torah* and to honor and beautify it exceedingly; the things that are in the *Luchos HaBris* are the very same things that are in every single *Sefer Torah*" (meaning, this *din* is for the reason that the *Sefer* (and every *Sefer* within this *Sefer*) contains "the things that are in the *Luchos HaBris*") is also understood:

The difference between "covenant" (*bris*) and "witness" (*edus*) is simply: "Covenant" is the title for something that creates a bond and connection between two parties of the covenant, and "witness" implies only proof and evidence of a matter (the covenant that has already been forged between two parties).

And this is the difference between the "Luchos HaBris" and a *Sefer Torah* which "is the faithful witness, etc.":
The (primary) essence of the *Luchos* is that they are the "Luchos HaBris", which are called so because they are the covenant between Hashem and *Bnei Yisrael*, as it is written, "The *Luchos HaBris* which Hashem forged with you," meaning the covenant regarding the observance of the Torah and *mitzvos*, through which a bond and covenant is created between Hashem and *Bnei Yisrael*.

Therefore, "It is a *mitzvah* to designate a place for a *Sefer Torah*, etc.," which is (one of) the obligations of the person toward the Torah, is that the person must honor the Torah. Since the Torah is what connects the person with Hashem, one must honor it.

And the innovation in this *halacha* ("It is a *mitzvah* to designate a place for a *Sefer Torah*, etc.") — is that for this reason, the Rambam adds here a special reason ("the things that are in the *Luchos HaBris*, etc."): Until this *halacha*, the Rambam detailed the obligations of honoring a *Sefer Torah* from the perspective of the effect of the Torah's sanctity (and as he phrases it at the beginning of the chapter (halacha 2 and onwards), "A kosher *Sefer Torah* is treated with extra sanctity and great honor" — and he continues with all the obligations of honoring a *Sefer Torah*); and here he adds that there is a *din* of honor from the perspective of the *cheftza* of Torah, which obligates and adds to the *mitzvah* of honoring a *Sefer Torah* the *din* of "place" — "It is a *mitzvah* to designate a place for a *Sefer Torah* and to honor and beautify it, etc." — and for this, he brings the reason that "the things that are in the *Luchos HaBris* are the very same things that are in every single *Sefer Torah*," for since a *Sefer Torah* contains the "things that are in the *Luchos HaBris*," this obligates that it have a special "place."

But nevertheless, even though this *din* is from the perspective of the *cheftza* of the *Sefer Torah* — it is still among the obligations of the person toward the Torah, that he is obligated to honor the *Sefer Torah* by designating a place for it, and the *cheftza* of the *Sefer Torah*, since the things within it are "the things that are in the *Luchos HaBris*," demands that the "place" of the Torah also be honored by the person.

In contrast, in the following *halacha*, the Rambam does not discuss the obligations of the person to honor the Torah at all, but rather the effect of the Torah upon the person. Therefore, he writes the reason — that "it is the faithful witness, etc.," meaning that since the *cheftza* of the *Sefer Torah* is a "witness" to Hashem's choosing of *Bnei Yisrael*, therefore, when a person sits before a *Sefer Torah*, he must sit with "koved rosh, eimah, and pachad."

F. Explanation of the matter in the style of *Chassidus*:
These three matters in the *Sefer Torah* — its sanctity, the *Luchos HaBris*, and the faithful witness — point to three modes and levels in the connection of *Bnei Yisrael* with Hashem, which were created and arrived at in their perfection at *Matan Torah*: 1) The sanctity of Israel, 2) Connection in the manner of a covenant, 3) Essential connection.

And the explanation of this:
The sanctity of Israel that was created at *Matan Torah* is the sanctity of the *neshamah* that is in every single one of Israel. For although from the perspective that the Patriarchs are the *Merkavah*, they bequeathed to every one of their descendants after them until the end of all generations a holy *neshamah* (which is not in the nations of the world), nevertheless, the completion and perfection of the sanctity of the *neshamah* was achieved at *Matan Torah*. And as it is in every one of Israel, that immediately upon being born there is a holy *nefesh* in him, and (afterwards) the beginning of the entry of this *nefesh* of sanctity is at the *mitzvah* of *Bris Milah*, nevertheless, "the completion and the main entry of the holy *nefesh* into a person is at thirteen years and one day for a male and twelve years and one day for a female, which is why they then became obligated in *mitzvos* from the Torah and became 'sons of punishment' (accountable)."

The covenant at *Matan Torah* is the connection to Hashem that is through the observance of the Torah and *mitzvos*. The Torah is the covenant that connects Israel with Hashem, as the *Zohar* says, that Israel is connected to the Torah and the Torah to the Holy One, blessed be He, for through the Torah, Israel is connected to the Holy One, blessed be He.

The essential connection at *Matan Torah* is that which Hashem chose us from all the nations, for the matter of choice is not from the perspective of sanctity or level, but its root is in the *Atzmus* of Hashem, which is above all definitions and levels.

And the difference between these three levels is: although all three modes of connection exist always in Israel, nevertheless, there are differences in the manner of their effect in Israel:
Regarding the sanctity in Israel — there are those among *Bnei Yisrael* whose sanctity of their *neshamah* shines for them in a revealed way, and those for whom it does not shine as much. And among those for whom it shines in a revealed way, there are different levels and modes in the revelation of sanctity at different times of the year — on weekdays, on *Shabbos*, on the *Yomim Nora'im*, and on the festivals of the year.

And so it is regarding the matter of the covenant in the Torah, the connection with Hashem that is through the observance of the Torah and *mitzvos*, for although this exists always in every one of Israel, regardless of his status and situation, as is hinted also in the word "Yisrael" — an acronym for "There are 600,000 letters to the Torah", meaning that in every Jewish person, in whatever situation he is, there is a connection to the Torah (and through it to the Holy One, blessed be He) and he is drawn by the letter in the Torah that belongs to him. Nevertheless, there are differences between letter and letter, and consequently between the children of Israel, and likewise in the revelation of this matter, and likewise in each person himself, from time to time (in accordance with his status and situation in the study of Torah and the observance of *mitzvos*).

However, the essential connection of *Bnei Yisrael* to Hashem has no division or levels, for it is the connection of the *etzem* (essence) of Israel with the *Atzmus* of Hashem.

G. And this is the content of the matter that the *Sefer Torah* "is the faithful witness to all the inhabitants of the world":
It is known from what is written (by the *Alter Rebbe*, etc.) that a witness is for a hidden and concealed thing, but for a revealed thing, one does not need the matter of a witness. And furthermore, even for a "matter that is destined to be revealed," the Torah does not require a witness; a witness is specifically for a hidden thing (as is explained at length (also) regarding the matter of "You are My witnesses").

And based on this, it is also understood regarding this witness of the Torah, that it is the faithful witness to *Bnei Yisrael*, that this witness is not for a revealed thing, meaning for this connection of *Bnei Yisrael* that is through the Torah and *mitzvos*, which is a recognizable and visible thing, that *Bnei Yisrael* are distinct in this from the nations of the world;
And likewise, the witness is not for the sanctity of Israel — the holy *nefesh* that is in every one of Israel, for although the sanctity is not always recognizable in a revealed and felt way, nevertheless, it is in the category of a "matter destined to be revealed," for every one of Israel, from time to time, this *nefesh* of sanctity shines for him, is awakened in him, and shines during times of favor in the year (or through an awakening from above, as is known).

Rather, the witness of the *Sefer Torah* is for the essential connection of Israel with the *Atzmus* of Hashem, which is a hidden thing, yet it exists in every one of Israel in any situation whatsoever.

And based on this, the precision of the Rambam's language, "is the faithful witness," is also understood: In the covenant between Hashem and Israel regarding the observance of the Torah and *mitzvos*, when there is a lack, *chas v'shalom*, in the observance of the Torah and *mitzvos*, it damages the covenant; in contrast, the essential connection between Israel and Hashem is an eternal thing forever and in whatever situation *Bnei Yisrael* may be, and therefore, "it is the faithful witness."

H. Based on this, one must also explain (in the inner dimension of the matter) the words of the Rambam in the continuation of the *halacha*, that after this *din*, he concludes (*Hilchos Sefer Torah*), "And he should honor it according to his ability. The early Sages said, 'Anyone who desecrates the Torah, his body is desecrated by people, and anyone who honors the Torah, his body is honored by people.'"

For seemingly, one must be precise in the details of the language:
1) This matter that a person honors the Torah for the sake of a reward (that "his body should be honored by people") is not a lofty level in the service of Hashem — for this is service "not for its own sake" (*shelo lishmah*). And it is puzzling: is it possible that after an extensive explanation regarding the greatness and majesty of a *Sefer Torah*, that it contains "the things that are in the *Luchos HaBris*," that it "is the faithful witness, etc.," the Rambam should conclude with the matter of the benefit and reward that will come to the person through this and from the fact that he honors the Torah?
2) And the puzzlement grows — that regarding the reward itself, the Rambam chose the reward of "his body is honored by people," meaning that the discussion here is not about spiritual honor (which pertains to a person's *neshamah*), but that (even) his (physical) body should be honored by people [and it can be said that this is also the precision, "his body is honored by people," meaning that the discussion here is about honor that people in general will accord him (and not masters of Torah and greatness), for the honor that comes from "people," who have no concept of spiritual matters, pertains to the person's body] — and how is it appropriate to conclude all the *Hilchos Sefer Torah* with this reward?!

And based on the above, it can be said that his intent is not to inform the reward that comes to the person by honoring the Torah, but rather that this comes as a continuation of what the Rambam wrote earlier, that the *cheftza* of Torah acts upon the person, because "it is the faithful witness to all the inhabitants of the world."

And the explanation:
A witness is for a hidden thing — in order to reveal this hidden thing; and likewise in our case, that the *Sefer Torah* reveals the essential connection of Israel and the Holy One, blessed be He, until through this, the importance of Israel is recognized in a revealed way "to all the inhabitants of the world."

And in continuation of this, the Rambam writes (and concludes *Hilchos Sefer Torah*) regarding this "witness" of the *Sefer Torah*, which reveals the greatness of Israel to the inhabitants of the world — "Anyone who honors the Torah, his body is honored by people," for through the person honoring the Torah, this acts that even "people" will recognize his greatness, which is expressed in that they honor his body.

I. And now it is understood and simple, that the effect of the witness of the *Sefer Torah* — to reveal the essential connection with Hashem — is first and foremost in the person himself, that it activates in the person that in all his actions this matter will be revealed and felt, until it is recognizable in his physical body, and from this, this witness is drawn also to all the inhabitants of the world.

And this is the empowerment that we "take" from the time of the giving of our Torah on the festival of *Shavuos*, that in addition to the awakening in the revelation of the sanctity of Israel, the holy *nefesh* in him, and in addition to the strengthening in the "(Luchos) HaBris" — through the study of Torah and the observance of *mitzvos*, there is yet more and primarily — that then there is an addition in the revelation of the essential connection of Israel with Hashem, which influences his service with all his inner powers, and in thought, speech, and action throughout all the days of the year,

Until it is recognizable and revealed to "all the inhabitants of the world," which will influence and activate them to observe their seven *mitzvos*, for this too was given at *Matan Torah*, as the Rambam writes, "And likewise, Moshe Rabbeinu commanded from the mouth of the Almighty to compel all the inhabitants of the world to accept the *mitzvos* that the sons of Noach were commanded... Anyone who accepts the seven *mitzvos* and is careful to perform them, he is of the pious of the nations of the world and has a share in the World to Come, provided that he accepts them and performs them because the Holy One, blessed be He, commanded them in the Torah and informed us through Moshe Rabbeinu that the sons of Noach were commanded in them beforehand."

And through this, we also hasten the coming of *Mashiach Tzidkeinu*, who "will rectify the entire world to serve Hashem together (as one), as it is said, 'For then I will turn to the peoples a clear language, that they may all call upon the Name of Hashem, to serve Him with one consent,'" speedily in our days, truly.


## נשא

## Parshas Naso

### 1

The Midrash Rabbah at the beginning of our Parsha states: "Take the sum of the sons of Gershon, etc. This is what the verse says: 'It is more precious than pearls, and all your desires cannot compare to it.' ... A Torah scholar takes precedence over a King of Israel, etc. ... If he is a *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar, he takes precedence over a High Priest who is an *am ha'aretz*, as it is said, 'It is more precious than pearls' — even over one who enters the Holy of Holies. Another interpretation: 'It is more precious than pearls' speaks of Kehas and Gershon; even though Gershon was the firstborn, and we find that in every place the Torah accords honor to the firstborn, because Kehas carried the Ark, where the Torah was, the Torah gave him precedence over Gershon, etc."

According to the literal meaning, the first interpretation of the verse "It is more precious than pearls" is brought here only as a preface to the second interpretation (that the verse "speaks of Kehas and Gershon"). Nevertheless, it stands to reason that since this interpretation is brought here regarding the verse in our Parsha—and especially since it is brought as the first interpretation (before the interpretation that it "speaks of Kehas and Gershon")—this interpretation also pertains to the understanding of the nature of the Torah’s superiority alluded to in our Parsha (just as the second interpretation does).

### 2

This can be understood by first explaining the language of the Rambam, who brings the essence of this Midrashic statement (the source of which is in the *Shas*) in the Laws of Torah Study: "Israel was crowned with three crowns: the crown of Torah, the crown of Priesthood, and the crown of Kingship. Aaron merited the crown of Priesthood, etc. David merited the crown of Kingship, etc. The crown of Torah, however, is laid out, standing, and ready for all of Israel, etc. Lest you say that those crowns are greater than the crown of Torah, it says: 'By Me kings reign and princes decree justice; by Me princes rule.' Thus you have learned that the crown of Torah is greater than both of them." In the following law, he continues: "The Sages said: A *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar takes precedence over a High Priest who is an *am ha'aretz*, as it is said, 'It is more precious than pearls'—even over one who enters the Holy of Holies."

This requires explanation: Why did the Rambam divide this statement, "The Sages said: A *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar takes precedence, etc.," into a separate law? Seemingly, this statement is merely a conclusion and proof for the content of the previous law, that "the crown of Torah is greater than both of them." The fact that the Rambam wrote it as a separate law implies that there is an additional matter and a separate legal point in this statement.

Furthermore, it is not understood: The Rambam does not explain in what way a " *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar takes precedence over a High Priest who is an *am ha'aretz*"—even though it is explicit in the Mishnah, where this matter is brought, that "a Priest takes precedence over a Levite, a Levite over an Israelite... When? When they are all equal. But if there is a *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar and a High Priest who is an *am ha'aretz*, the *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar takes precedence," in continuation of the law that "a man takes precedence over a woman in saving a life and returning a lost object, etc."—yet in the Rambam, there is no law here at all, only proof and reinforcement for the previous law.

### 3

The explanation for this is as follows:
The "crown of Torah" indicates the "exaltation" that Torah study effects in the person who studies it, as per the words of R. Yosef regarding the Giving of the Torah: "If not for this day that causes [the existence of the world], etc.,"—"I have studied Torah and have been exalted." In this, there are two manners (and levels):

1) Engaging in a lofty matter (including Torah) effects "exaltation" in a person. Since the Torah is "more precious than pearls," and is higher than other lofty matters, the "exaltation" that comes through Torah is higher than other types of "exaltation." According to this, the "crown of Torah" is in the same category and classification as the other "crowns," only it is higher than them.

2) The "crown of Torah" effects an "exaltation" that has no comparison at all in the world. This is what the Sages meant by "Who are the kings? The Rabbis... for it is written, 'By Me kings reign,'" meaning that only the "Rabbis" are the true "kings." According to this, the other "crowns" are not considered at all in comparison to the "crown of Torah."

As is explained in *Torah Or* regarding the superiority of Torah study over the performance of *mitzvos*: A person who performs *mitzvos* is "like a servant who fulfills the commandments of the king and does his bidding." But when studying Torah with the intention of reaching the truth of Torah, the words of Torah being studied are the word of Hashem. The words of Torah that he utters with his mouth are truly the words of Hashem, as it is written, "And I have put My words in your mouth"—literally My words, for the word of Hashem, which is the *halacha*, is what is revealed within him and is what speaks from his throat.

This is the perfection of the "crown of Torah": the exaltation and the "kingship" are not his own, but rather that of the Torah itself (which is revealed through him). In the words of the Alter Rebbe there: "Therefore it is said of the Torah, 'By Me kings reign,' and we say, 'Who are the kings? The Rabbis...' For because he is in a state of *bitul* to the word of Hashem, His will, and His wisdom, and he does not occupy any space of his own at all, the word of the King is the authority, and he is in the state of a king who decrees and says, etc."

### 4

It can be said that this is the essence of the distinction between the two laws in the Rambam:
In the first law, he speaks of the effect of Torah study on the person, which is similar to the effect of other lofty matters. In this sense, the crown of Torah is in the same category as the crown of Priesthood and the crown of Kingship (where the King and High Priest are distinct and elevated above the rest of the people), to the point where one might have thought that those crowns are greater than the crown of Torah. Therefore, we must make it known that the crown of Torah is greater than both of them—the exaltation that comes through Torah is higher than Priesthood and Kingship.

However, in the second law, he speaks of the second manner of the "crown of Torah," which is an entirely different category, not of the same type as the crown of Priesthood and the crown of Kingship. This is the reason why this matter is emphasized by the fact that a " *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar takes precedence over a High Priest who is an *am ha'aretz*, as it is said, 'It is more precious than pearls'—even over one who enters the Holy of Holies." This means that there is a superiority in Torah study over the High Priest entering the Holy of Holies. Even though that is the holiest place in the world, where the *Shechinah* rests, and consequently it is understood that this is the closest manner in which a person can approach the *Ribono shel Olam*, nevertheless, it is not comparable to Torah study. For entering the Holy of Holies is only like a servant or a minister entering before the King and being close to the King. But through Torah study in a state of *bitul*, the Torah scholar is in the category of a "king" himself—"Who are the kings? The Rabbis," as mentioned above.

[Furthermore, it can be said that this is also emphasized by the fact that a " *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar takes precedence over a High Priest who is an *am ha'aretz*," because only from the perspective of the incomparable elevation effected by Torah, even a person who is among the lowest of the ten lineages, of whom it is said, "He shall not enter the congregation," is higher than a High Priest (*am ha'aretz*) who enters the Holy of Holies.]

### 5

With this, we can also understand the distinction between the two interpretations in the Midrash on the verse "It is more precious than pearls":
According to the first interpretation in the Midrash, it refers to the perfection of the exaltation in Torah, which has no comparison at all to anything in the world. This is what it says at the end of the matter: "If he is a *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar, he takes precedence over a High Priest who is an *am ha'aretz*, as it is said, 'It is more precious than pearls'—even over one who enters the Holy of Holies," as mentioned above.

In contrast, according to the second interpretation in the Midrash, this verse refers to the first manner of "exaltation" in Torah mentioned above. Even when a person's Torah study is not in the state of ultimate perfection (to the point of being in the category of "Who are the kings? The Rabbis"), nevertheless, the very fact that a person studies Torah effects in him an exaltation that is higher than all other types of exaltation in the world.

This is what it says in this interpretation: "Even though Gershon was the firstborn, and we find that in every place the Torah accords honor to the firstborn, because Kehas carried the Ark, where the Torah was, the Torah gave him precedence over Gershon, etc." This means: (a) The comparison is (only) to a firstborn; (b) in this itself, it only says that "the Torah gave him precedence"; (c) the superiority of Kehas here is (not that they are Torah scholars, but only) that he is "carrying the Ark"—yet the superiority of the Torah is so great that even one who is "carrying the Ark, where the Torah is" has a superiority even over a firstborn (even though "in every place the Torah accords honor to the firstborn").

Based on this, it can be said that this is the intention of the Midrash here in bringing both interpretations of "It is more precious than pearls," and concluding with "Another interpretation: 'It is more precious than pearls' speaks of Kehas and Gershon." First, it establishes that the true essence of the exaltation in Torah is like the matter of "a *mamzer* who is a Torah scholar taking precedence over a High Priest... who enters the Holy of Holies." But what is relevant here (regarding Kehas and Gershon) is only the lesser level of the Torah's superiority (as per the second interpretation in the Midrash).

The reason: The verse says, "Take the sum of the sons of Gershon, also them," where "take" (*nasso*) indicates the matter of elevation and exaltation. Since even among the sons of Gershon (even though their matter is not Torah) there is the matter of elevation (though they are secondary in this to the sons of Kehas, "also them"), it must be that the intention is for an exaltation that is comparable to other matters of exaltation, only higher than them, as mentioned above.

### 6

More deeply, it can be said that the first interpretation in the Midrash also comes to explain our verse, "Take the sum of the sons of Gershon, also them."
This can be understood by first adding an explanation of the difference between the two manners mentioned above regarding the exaltation effected by Torah study.
These two manners, even though both come through Torah study, differ in their details: The first matter comes (primarily) through the very act of Torah study with understanding and comprehension; but the second matter depends on the person studying Torah in a state of ultimate *bitul*, for only then are the words of Torah that he studies a revelation of the word of Hashem.

This is the reason why there had to be the precedence of "we will do" (*na'aseh*) to "we will hear" (*nishma*) before the Giving of the Torah, and likewise, there must be the precedence of "we will do" to "we will hear" before Torah study at all times and in all places. As our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe explains: "The word *nishma* means to be *batel* to the word of Torah that speaks from his mouth, which is His will and His wisdom, as if he hears the *Shechinah* speaking from his throat, as it is written, 'My tongue shall answer Your word'... a total *bitul* of will." This *bitul* comes through the precedence of "we will do," which is the matter of *bitul* to the "Owner of the Will... Himself."

This is what the Sages meant by "Great is study, for it leads to action," that the greatness in study is what leads to action (in the sense of "we coerce them regarding charity"), which is the matter of *iskafya* and *bitul*.
Simply put: The aspect where Torah study is in true *bitul* is that the study itself leads him to detach himself from his study (even though there is much *taanug* and vitality in it) and engage in matters of action. Then there is the greatness in study, for when he studies from a state of *bitul*, the words of Torah he studies are the word of Hashem revealed through him, and he is in the category of a "king"—"Who are the kings? The Rabbis."

[More deeply, one can explain the Sages' statement "Great is study, for it leads to action" as follows: When one studies in complete *bitul*, this also affects his action, so that his performance of *mitzvos* will be in the same *bitul* as his Torah study. He does not perform the *mitzvos* merely as a servant who fulfills the commandments of the king (which is not complete *bitul*, because the existence of the servant remains, even though he does the king's decree, as mentioned in section 3), but rather he is in a state of ultimate *bitul*, similar to the Sages' statement, "He bowed of his own accord." This means that it is not a case of a person performing the will of Hashem, but rather it is as if the will of Hashem is done by itself without a "person" doing it, because the person is *batel* to the point of nothingness to the will of Hashem, to the extent that it is as if there is no existence other than His will.]

### 7

Based on this, it can be said that this matter is emphasized in "Take the sum of the sons of Gershon, also them":
The fact that the true exaltation of Torah is effected through Torah study in *bitul*—which is when the study leads to action—is seen in the fact that after the sons of Kehas come the sons of Gershon. Gershon indicates the performance of *mitzvos*, as explained in many places (they carried the curtains, etc., and only after the erection of the *Mishkan* by the sons of Gershon and Merari did the sons of Kehas come and place the Ark and the vessels inside the *Mishkan*). Therefore, only after the matter of the sons of Gershon exists does it emphasize the true elevation of the "sons of Kehas" (Torah).

This is why the verse specifies, "Take the sum of the sons of Gershon, also them," meaning that it is not speaking of the superiority of the sons of Gershon in and of themselves (i.e., from the perspective of the performance of *mitzvos* itself), but rather that they are secondary and *batel* to the sons of Kehas—the matter of action that comes as a result of study. [Furthermore, "also them" means that even in "action," the *bitul* of Torah is drawn down, as mentioned above.]
According to the above, one can also explain the hint in the fact that specifically this Parsha, which speaks of the sons of Gershon, is called by the name "Naso" (Take/Elevate) in general—even though the "Naso" of the sons of Kehas is higher than that of the sons of Gershon (as emphasized in the verse itself, "Take the sum of the sons of Gershon, also them")—because only after the service of the sons of Gershon exists (in the manner of "also them") is the true essence of the "elevation" and exaltation that comes through Torah revealed, as explained at length above.

### 8

Parshas Naso is always read in proximity to the festival of Shavuos, the time of the giving of our Torah. According to the well-known words of the *Shelah* that "the festivals of the entire year... all have a connection to the Parshios that fall during them," it is understood that there is a hint and a lesson in this Parsha regarding the festival of Shavuos, the time of the giving of our Torah.

[Even though, simply put, the festival of Shavuos belongs to Parshas Bamidbar, as per the *halachic* ruling that we always read Parshas Bamidbar before *Atzeres*, as explained at length elsewhere, nevertheless, there is a special connection in Parshas Naso to the festival of Shavuos (even more than in Parshas Bamidbar). For Parshas Bamidbar (a) is read before *Atzeres*, (b) sometimes it is not on the Shabbos before the festival of Shavuos, but on the Shabbos before that—whereas Parshas Naso (a) is sometimes (in most years) read after the festival of Shavuos, and it turns out that the festival of Shavuos falls in the week of Parshas Naso, (b) is always read on the Shabbos adjacent to the festival of Shavuos, whether on the Shabbos after it, in which the days of *Atzeres* are elevated and included, or on the Shabbos before it, from which the days of the festival of Shavuos are blessed.]

Seemingly: Of the three families of the sons of Levi—Kehas, Gershon, and Merari—the primary superiority of Torah is found specifically with the sons of Kehas (which is why they were the carriers of the Ark). If so, why do we always read Parshas Naso (specifically) in proximity to the festival of Shavuos?
Based on what was explained above, it is understood that, on the contrary, that is the very reason: The primary innovation of the Giving of the Torah is (not the understanding and comprehension of the words of Torah, but) the revelation of the Torah of Hashem, "He gave us His Torah." The vessel to receive the Torah of Hashem is through the matter of *bitul*, the precedence of "we will do" to "we will hear," and study in a manner that leads to action (as mentioned above)—and only then does the Torah effect a matter of true "elevation" and exaltation, "Take the sum." Therefore, we always read the Parsha of "Take the sum of the sons of Gershon" in proximity to the festival of Shavuos, because here the superiority of the Torah studied in *bitul* is expressed, as explained at length above.

### 9

This matter (the superiority of Torah study in *bitul*) is also hinted at in the middle of the Parsha, in the section of the offerings of the Princes. Rashi states that the reason "He offered" is said twice regarding "Nachshon ben Amminadav" is because "for two things he merited to offer for two tribes: one, that they were knowledgeable in Torah, as it is said, 'And of the children of Issachar, who had understanding of the times,' and one, that they gave advice to the Princes to volunteer these offerings."

In the Midrash Rabbah on this Parsha, it says: "The Prince of Issachar came and offered on behalf of the Torah, because they loved the Torah more than all the tribes, as it is said, 'And of the children... to know what Israel ought to do, the heads of them were two hundred'—these were the two hundred heads of the Sanhedrin that the tribe of Issachar produced, and all their brethren were at their word, for they would agree on the *halacha* according to their word. And it says, 'And he bowed his shoulder to bear,' for they would bear the yoke of Torah, 'and became a tributary servant,' for whoever was mistaken in a *halacha* would ask the tribe of Issachar, and they would explain it to them."

However, even though the Prince of Issachar offered first (due to the superiority of Torah), even before the tribe of Reuben the firstborn, he nevertheless offered second, and not on the first day.
The explanation for this:
Although Issachar offered on behalf of the Torah, as mentioned above, the primary superiority of Torah that leads to greatness and elevation is when the study is connected with the *bitul* of the student's existence [as was the case with Issachar, for it is written of them, "And he bowed his shoulder to bear," that they would bear the yoke of Torah, as is also hinted at in the intention of "And for the sacrifice of peace offerings, two oxen"—corresponding to the two Torahs, the Written and the Oral, for whoever examines and sacrifices his *yetzer* to do all that is written in them, he makes two peaces, peace above and peace below]. This *bitul* of Issachar was expressed in the fact that he offered second. The first to offer was Nachshon ben Amminadav, whose matter is (Nachshon—meaning) *mesiras nefesh*. "Why was his name called Nachshon? Because he went down first into the surging waves of the sea," for "Nachshon jumped into the waves of the sea and went down," because he knew that "when you have brought forth the people out of Egypt, you shall serve Hashem upon this mountain." Therefore, he jumped with *mesiras nefesh* into the sea in order to approach Mount Sinai to receive the Torah.

### 10

It can be said that this is the hint in the three manners of the scheduling of the reading of Parshas Naso: (a) sometimes we read Naso before the festival of Shavuos, (b) in most years after the festival of Shavuos, (c) when Parshas Naso is two full weeks, and the festival of Shavuos falls (in the Diaspora) on Friday and Shabbos:
The reading of Parshas Naso before the festival of Shavuos is an instruction and a source of strength that in order to reach the greatness and "elevation" of Torah, there must be beforehand an additional strengthening in the matter of *bitul* before the Giving of the Torah, the precedence of "we will do" to "we will hear." The reading of Parshas Naso after the festival of Shavuos is an instruction and a source of strength regarding the *bitul* in action after the Giving of the Torah, that the greatness and elevation in Torah must come through *bitul* in action. And when the festival of Shavuos is on Friday and Shabbos, meaning that Parshas Naso is both in the week before the festival of Shavuos and in the week after the festival of Shavuos, this is an instruction regarding the connection of both matters together. This is the perfection of the elevation of Torah, that there is the *bitul* of the precedence of "we will do" to "we will hear" before the Giving of the Torah, and the *bitul* in action ("Great is study, for it leads to action") after the Giving of the Torah.

(From the *sicha* of Shabbos Parshas Naso, 5735)


## בהעלותך

## Sicha 1

### 56

A. Regarding the law of Pesach Sheni—where the Torah established the "make-up" for the first Pesach on the fourteenth of Iyar specifically, one month after the first Pesach—an explanation is required: How is the Korban Pesach different (in that its time for completion comes after a month-long interval) from other Korbanos of Yom Tov, whose days of completion occur in immediate succession to the first day of the festival?

This is especially so according to Chazal, who state that the "those who were tamei through a human corpse" in the desert (who came with the argument, "Why should we be diminished, so as not to offer the Korban of Hashem in its appointed time?" and merited that this passage was stated through them) reached the seventh day of their tumah on Erev Pesach. According to this, it would have been possible to set the time for the "make-up" during the days of the festival itself.

It can be said that (according to the inner dimension of Torah) this is the foundation for the explanation of the Tzemach Tzedek, that the concept of Pesach Sheni in the avodah of man relates to the content of the avodah of the month of Iyar, the "second month."

**The content of the Tzemach Tzedek’s explanation:**
The concept of Pesach Sheni in the avodah of man is the ascent from the first level of Yetzias Mitzrayim (the first Pesach in the month of Nissan, the first month) to the second level (in the month of Iyar, the second month); that is, the avodah of "do good" which comes after the prerequisite of "turn away from evil."

In other words: Yetzias Mitzrayim is the beginning of the avodah, "turn away from evil"—the departure from Mitzrayim. As explained in Tanya, regarding Yetzias Mitzrayim it is said, "for the people fled," because "the evil within the souls of Yisrael was still in its full strength, etc.," and their primary avodah was in the manner of "turn away from evil" (the flight from the tumah of Mitzrayim). After completing the avodah of "turn away from evil," they ascended to the second level—"do good."

In the spiritual avodah within the soul of man, Yetzias Mitzrayim (the first Pesach) is the avodah of *iskafya*, the suppression of the evil in one's soul. From this avodah, one ascends to the second level—the avodah of *is'hapcha*, transforming evil into good, "with all your heart—with both your inclinations". This level of Pesach Sheni corresponds to the general innovation in the avodah of the month of Iyar, the second month (relative to the month of Nissan, the first month)—Sefiras HaOmer (for "the essence of Sefiras HaOmer is the entire month of Iyar")—as the avodah of Sefiras HaOmer is the refinement of the *midos* and their transformation into holiness.

Based on this, he explains the difference between the first Pesach and the second Pesach, that "the first is forbidden in 'it shall not be seen and it shall not be found,' while the second, 'chametz and matzah are with him in the house'":

Since the avodah of the first Pesach is "turn away from evil," a flight from evil, therefore the obligation applies then to remove the chametz (which symbolizes evil, "the leaven in the dough"), so that no chametz remains in one's possession. Not so with Pesach Sheni, whose concept is the transformation of evil into good; one is not required at that time to remove the chametz from one's possession, but rather "chametz and matzah are with him in the house," because through Pesach Sheni, the power is given (in addition) to transform the chametz into holiness.

[It may be said that this is analogous to the elevation of the *shtei halechem* on the festival of Shavuos (relative to the Korban Omer of the festival of Pesach), whose virtue is specifically that "they shall be baked leavened"—for after the completion of the avodah of Sefiras HaOmer, the chametz is completely transformed into holiness, and one can and does make it a sacrifice to Hashem.]

B. However, there is a difficulty in the very explanation of the Tzemach Tzedek. Seemingly, this explanation—that Pesach Sheni is a second and more elevated level of avodah (do good, *is'hapcha*) which comes after the prerequisite avodah of the first Pesach (turn away from evil, *iskafya*)—is the opposite of what is explicitly stated in the plain meaning of the verse (and Halacha): that the entire concept of Pesach Sheni is only for a person who did not bring the first Pesach (and one who offered his sacrifice on the first Pesach, not only is he not obligated to offer a second Pesach, but he is forbidden to do so)!

According to the Tzemach Tzedek’s explanation, this is the reverse—because in addition to the fact that after the avodah of the first Pesach, the avodah of Pesach Sheni is relevant and even required, it is further implied that one cannot reach the avodah of Pesach Sheni except specifically by first completing the avodah of the first Pesach?!

C. The explanation for this is as follows—and by way of introduction:

What was stated above, that the entire concept of Pesach Sheni applies only when the first Pesach did not precede it, refers only to the Korban Pesach—that if one did not offer it on the first, he offers it on the second, and if he offered it on the first, he is forbidden to offer it on the second. But it is understood that this does not apply to the other matters and mitzvos of the festival of Pesach, such as the prohibitions of chametz and the eating of matzah—for even one who does not offer a Korban Pesach on the fourteenth of Nissan (according to Torah law, due to being "tamei" or "on a distant journey") is obligated in the prohibitions of chametz and to eat matzah, etc.

[It should be added that in the case described in the verse, the "men who were tamei through a human corpse" were (as Chazal say) "Mishael and Eltzafan... who were occupied with Nadav and Avihu," meaning that their tumah was according to the command of Moshe, "Come near, carry your brethren, etc."

(It may be said that this is the explanation of their argument, "Why should we be diminished"—for seemingly it is not understood: what room is there for the argument "Why should we be diminished, so as not to offer the Korban of Hashem in its appointed time"—did they not themselves provide the answer to this argument by saying "We are tamei through a human corpse," and therefore they cannot offer the Korban of Hashem in its appointed time?

Rather, their argument was based on the fact that their tumah was according to the command of Moshe, and therefore they argued: Is it possible that through the command of Moshe, we should be "diminished," and especially from "offering the Korban of Hashem in its appointed time?")

Based on this, it is clear and simple that all the matters of Pesach for these "men" (except for the offering of the Korban Pesach) were in absolute perfection.]

Based on this, the words of the Tzemach Tzedek are understood: that Pesach Sheni relates to the second level of avodah, which comes after the first level of the first Pesach, meaning that in order to arrive at Pesach Sheni, it is necessary that there first be a first Pesach (even though Pesach Sheni is only for one who did not offer the first Pesach):

The spiritual avodah of the festival of Pesach—"turn away from evil," the suppression of evil—is connected to the fulfillment of the (other) matters and mitzvos of the festival (the prohibitions of chametz, and the obligation to eat matzah—"bread of affliction"). And since even one who did not offer the first Pesach in its time is performing his avodah in the (other) matters of Pesach, it follows that for him, Pesach Sheni is the second level, which comes after the avodah of the festival of Pesach (the first), as the Tzemach Tzedek explains that in Pesach Sheni one arrives and ascends to the second level of avodah, "do good," *is'hapcha*.

D. However, the matter is still not fully explained:

It is true that even one who did not offer the first Korban Pesach is not diminished in his avodah (including the spiritual one) at the first level of the festival of Pesach (since he had all the matters of the first Pesach except for the Korban Pesach), and from there he ascends to the second level, that of Pesach Sheni—but on the other hand: a) Why is one who offered the first Pesach forbidden to offer a second Pesach (for according to the Tzemach Tzedek’s explanation, the avodah of Pesach Sheni is required of him), and b) How and in what way does he ascend to the second level in the avodah of Hashem when it comes (only) through the offering of the first Pesach in its time?

The explanation for this is:

After offering the first Korban Pesach, there is no longer a need to bring a sacrifice on Pesach Sheni in order to reach the second level (do good, *is'hapcha*)—because this has already been done in perfection through the sacrifice on the first Pesach. In other words: the fact that it is within a person's power to reach (in the month of Iyar, the second month) the second level in the avodah of Hashem (do good, *is'hapcha*) comes through the Korban Pesach of the first Pesach.

**Explaining the matter:**
"Korban" is from the word *kiruv* (closeness), for through sacrifices, a person draws close to Hashem. As explained in many places, the concept of a sacrifice in the avodah of man is that a person must offer to Hashem the "animal, etc." within him, until he is worthy of being offered on the altar of Hashem and being consumed by the fire from above, to be included in holiness, etc.

The offering of the first Pesach is what provides the strength and causes the continuation of a person's avodah (Sefiras HaOmer, whose time begins immediately "on the morrow" of Pesach) to be in such a manner that through it he will reach "do good," *is'hapcha*.

There is no contradiction between the Tzemach Tzedek’s explanation (that the concept of Pesach Sheni is the second level in the avodah of Hashem) and the concept of Pesach Sheni (that it comes to rectify and complete the deficiency of the first Pesach)—for the avodah of Pesach Sheni (the second level, *is'hapcha*) is dependent on the offering of the Korban Pesach. Therefore: one who offered the first Pesach will certainly reach the second level (in the second month, including the day of Pesach Sheni) and there is no need (and consequently—no place) for an additional sacrifice; not so for one who did not offer a Korban Pesach on the first; it is impossible for him to reach the level of *is'hapcha* without rectifying and completing the deficiency of the first Pesach by bringing a sacrifice on Pesach Sheni (and he should not be "diminished" from offering—similar to the first Pesach).

E. Based on this explanation, the reason why even in this time Pesach Sheni is established as a special day for all of Yisrael will also be sweetened, as our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe writes in his Siddur that on Pesach Sheni one does not say *Tachanun*, and there is even a custom to eat matzah on it as a remembrance of Pesach Sheni.

For seemingly: the concept of Pesach Sheni is a rectification and completion for one who did not offer the first Pesach.

[And even according to the opinion (and such is the Halacha) that Pesach Sheni is a festival in its own right, which is why even one upon whom the obligation to bring the first Pesach did not fall, and who became obligated between the first and second (a minor who reached maturity, or a convert who converted between the first and second) is obligated to bring Pesach Sheni—nevertheless, it must be said (and it is explicit in the verse that it came through the argument "Why should we be diminished") that the concept of Pesach Sheni is a "make-up" for the deficiency of the first, and anyone who performed the first cannot be obligated in the second; rather, the Torah innovated (and added in Pesach Sheni—a festival in its own right) that even though the minor, etc., was not obligated in the first, nevertheless he was given the possibility to offer the Pesach on the fourteenth of Iyar—and similarly for a convert];

Therefore, in this time (and especially in the Diaspora) when we do not have a Korban Pesach—seemingly the entire concept of Pesach Sheni is no longer relevant—so what place is there for the celebration of Pesach Sheni (in any manner)?

Furthermore: "An individual is deferred to Pesach Sheni, but the community is not deferred to Pesach Sheni", meaning, the concept of the "make-up" of Pesach Sheni is given only to an individual and not to the community; if so, how is it appropriate to establish Pesach Sheni as a day of special customs, that we do not say *Tachanun*, etc.—for all of Bnei Yisrael together?

[And it is a great strain to explain that this is only according to the view of R' Yehuda (in the Yerushalmi) that when "it is given to Yisrael to build the Chosen House (between the first Pesach and the second Pesach)"—the community performs Pesach Sheni (and especially according to what is explained elsewhere that it can be said that even the Bavli holds in this like the Yerushalmi, that in such a case the community performs Pesach Sheni)—and if so, even though there is no Korban Pesach in the first, it is possible that there could be a sacrifice on Pesach Sheni—

For what we celebrate as Pesach Sheni does not relate to the possibility of offering a Pesach Sheni, but rather as a remembrance of the Pesach Sheni that existed in the time of the Beis HaMikdash, and what place is there to establish a remembrance for the community for a "make-up" of a matter that was only given to an individual?]

It must be that Pesach Sheni contains a matter that relates to all of Yisrael. And according to what was explained above regarding the content of Pesach Sheni in the avodah of man—the matter is understood simply, for on this day it is in the power of every person of Yisrael to reach the second level in the avodah of Hashem, the avodah of *is'hapcha*. In other words: even those who offered the first Pesach, the perfection of the effect of this offering (which is the avodah of *is'hapcha*) comes in the second month, and especially on Pesach Sheni [on which begins the counting that relates to the *midah* of *Hod*];

Therefore, even in this time, when we have the spiritual avodah that is in the Korban Pesach, we celebrate the special time for the ascent to the second level in the avodah of Hashem, *is'hapcha* and "do good."

(From the Sichos of Pesach Sheni 5747, 5744)

## Sicha 2

1. It is stated in the Sifrei on our Parsha (regarding the verse, "And on the day of your gladness, and in your appointed seasons... you shall blow with the trumpets..."): "‘And on the day of your gladness’—these are the Shabbosos." We learn from this that Shabbos is a day of joy. This is also implied in the Yerushalmi, Megillah, where the law is derived that when Purim falls on Shabbos, the Purim feast is not held on Shabbos (but rather after Shabbos), based on the verse, "To make them days of feasting and joy"—this excludes [Shabbos], whose joy is dependent on the hands of Heaven, as opposed to [Purim], whose joy is dependent on the Beis Din.

However, Admur HaZaken writes in his Shulchan Aruch (in the Laws of Yom Tov), "It is customary to increase the variety of foods on Yom Tov more than on Shabbos, for regarding Shabbos, joy is not mentioned." Seemingly, this implies that there is no obligation of joy on Shabbos at all (contrary to the aforementioned Sifrei and Yerushalmi). The Poskim also agree that there is no obligation of joy on Shabbos, as is explicitly stated in Tosfos, Moed Katan: "Regarding the festivals... joy is written; regarding Shabbos... joy is not written" (and a similar concept is found in the She'iltos and elsewhere).

But it can be said that Admur HaZaken’s intent in saying, "On Shabbos, joy is not mentioned," is (not to exclude Shabbos from all matters of joy, but) only to exclude the specific type of joy associated with Yom Tov (which is the subject of the Shulchan Aruch there). For the joy of Yom Tov has a specific definition, and this definition of joy does not exist on Shabbos. In this, Admur HaZaken’s innovation in this paragraph regarding the definition of the joy of Yom Tov is emphasized, as will be explained below.

2. The words of Admur HaZaken in the Laws of Yom Tov there are: "Throughout the seven days of Pesach and the eight days of the festival (Sukkos), and likewise on the Yom Tov of Atzeres (Shavuos), a person is obligated to be joyful and good-hearted—he, his children, his wife, his household, and all who are attached to him. This joy is a positive commandment from the Torah, as it is written, ‘And you shall rejoice in your festival, you, your son, your daughter, etc.’ How do we make the children joyful? By giving them roasted grains and nuts. And the women? By buying them clothing and jewelry according to his means. And the men? At the time when the Beis HaMikdash was standing, they would eat the meat of the Shelamim (peace offerings) for the sake of joy. Now that the Beis HaMikdash is no longer standing, we do not fulfill the obligation of joy except through wine, as it is written, ‘And wine gladdens the heart of man.’ However, there is no obligation to eat meat now, since we do not have the meat of the Shelamim. Nevertheless, there is a mitzvah in eating meat on Yom Tov, since joy is mentioned regarding it. And since we cannot rejoice in it through the primary joy—which is the eating of the meat of the Shelamim—we must rejoice in it through other types of joy. Therefore, it is customary to increase the variety of foods on Yom Tov more than on Shabbos, regarding which joy is not mentioned. Likewise, the clothing of Yom Tov must be better than the clothing of Shabbos."

According to its plain meaning, Admur HaZaken follows the opinion of the Rambam, who wrote: "Yomim Tovim... a person is obligated to be joyful and good-hearted—he, his children, his wife, his household, and all who are attached to him, as it is written, ‘And you shall rejoice in your festival, etc.’ Even though the joy mentioned here is the Korban Shelamim, as we explain in the Laws of Chagigah, included in that joy is that he, his children, and his household should rejoice, each as is appropriate for him. How? The children—by giving them roasted grains, nuts, and sweets; the women—by buying them fine clothing and jewelry according to his means; and the men—by eating meat and drinking wine, for there is no joy except with meat, and there is no joy except with wine."

Several details regarding the obligation of joy on Yom Tov are clarified in the words of the Rambam, which Admur HaZaken also adopts in his Shulchan Aruch:
a) That even in the present time, although we do not have a Beis HaMikdash and the Korban Shelamim of joy, there is still a mitzvah of joy from the Torah (and not like the opinions that the joy of the festival in the present time is Rabbinic, for "‘And you shall rejoice’ refers to the Shelamim of joy").
b) That even in the present time, there is a mitzvah to eat meat on Yom Tov.
c) That making children joyful with roasted grains and nuts, and women with the purchase of clothing and jewelry according to his means, is included in the Torah-mandated "And you shall rejoice in your festival."

3. However, there is a fundamental difference between the words of Admur HaZaken and the words of the Rambam:
The Rambam wrote generally, "And the men eat meat and drink wine, for there is no joy except with meat, and there is no joy except with wine." This implies that both meat and wine are (always) equal in fulfilling the mitzvah of the joy of Yom Tov. But Admur HaZaken writes and specifies, "And now that the Beis HaMikdash is no longer standing, we do not fulfill the obligation of joy except through wine... However, there is no obligation to eat meat now... Nevertheless, there is a mitzvah in eating meat on Yom Tov, etc." This means that although there is a mitzvah to eat meat on Yom Tov, in the present time, eating meat on Yom Tov is different from drinking wine, in that with wine one fulfills the obligation of joy, whereas with meat one does not (as "there is no obligation to eat it now").

Simply put, it could be said that Admur HaZaken does not disagree with the Rambam, but rather comes (with this addition) to explain his words (and to resolve the difficulty of the Beis Yosef regarding the Rambam, for in Perek Arvei Pesachim it is taught: "Our Rabbis taught: A person is obligated to make his children and household joyful on the festival. With what does he make them joyful? With wine. Rabbi Yehuda says: Men with what is appropriate for them, and women with what is appropriate for them... It is taught: Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira says: At the time when the Beis HaMikdash was standing, there is no joy except with meat, as it is written, ‘And you shall slaughter Shelamim and eat there and rejoice.’ And now that the Beis HaMikdash is not standing, there is no joy except with wine, as it is written, ‘And wine gladdens the heart of man.’... (The Beis Yosef wondered about the words of the Rambam) Why did he require that they eat meat and drink wine? For in the Beraisa it is taught that in the present time there is no joy except with wine, which implies that wine suffices without meat").

Admur HaZaken interprets the words of the Rambam to mean that now, when the Beis HaMikdash is not standing, there is a difference between wine and meat: the primary obligation of joy is through wine (as stated in the Gemara), unlike meat, where there is no obligation to eat it, only a mitzvah.

Seemingly, this interpretation of the Rambam—that there is also joy in meat—is based (as the Bach writes) on Chullin and Shabbos: "And the passage in Arvei Pesachim is to be interpreted as follows: At the time when the Beis HaMikdash was standing, the primary joy was in the meat of the Shelamim, as the verse writes, ‘And you shall slaughter Shelamim, etc.,’ for there were two types of joy: one, the joy of bringing the Shelamim to Hashem; the other, the joy of eating the meat. This is not the case with wine, where there is only one joy. But now that the Beis HaMikdash is not standing, the primary joy is only in wine, as the verse writes, etc. However, meat is not the primary joy, for we do not find a verse that writes of joy regarding meat that is not of the Shelamim. This is the opinion of Tosfos, who wrote... ‘With what does he make them joyful? With wine’—this refers to the present time. But at the time of the Beis HaMikdash, there is no joy except with meat, end quote. Meaning: in the present time, the primary joy is in wine, and at the time of the Beis HaMikdash, the primary joy is only in meat."

However, it is difficult to interpret the Rambam this way (and to say that Admur HaZaken’s words are [merely] an explanation of the Rambam’s words), for the plain language of the Rambam implies that there is no advantage in the joy of drinking wine over eating meat, always, even in the present time.

Furthermore: the plain language of the Rambam implies that even at the time of the Beis HaMikdash, the mitzvah of "And you shall rejoice in your festival" obligated the eating of meat and the drinking of wine equally, for he did not mention any difference between the time of the Beis HaMikdash and the present time. He only distinguishes between the joy of the Korban Shelamim and the details of the joy that "is included in that joy, that he and his children, etc., should rejoice, each as is appropriate for him," which he goes on to specify afterward. Regarding the men, this is the eating of meat and the drinking of wine (meaning even through the eating of non-sacred meat). But from Admur HaZaken’s words, "And the men, at the time when the Beis HaMikdash was standing, would eat the meat of the Shelamim," it is understood that the fulfillment of the mitzvah of joy at the time of the Beis HaMikdash was only through the meat of the Shelamim (and there was no obligation to eat meat and drink wine).

4. The difference between Admur HaZaken and the Rambam can be explained by noting another difference between their respective wordings:
a) In the beginning of the matter, the Rambam wrote, "And a person is obligated to be joyful and good-hearted—he, his children, his wife, his household, and all who are attached to him, as it is written, ‘And you shall rejoice in your festival.’" He continues, "Even though the joy mentioned here is the Korban Shelamim... included in that joy is that he, his children, etc., should rejoice," and he specifies in the following Halacha, "How? The children, etc." Admur HaZaken, however, concludes immediately in the first paragraph, "A person is obligated to be joyful and good-hearted, etc., and this joy is a positive commandment from the Torah, as it is written, ‘And you shall rejoice in your festival, you, your son, your daughter, etc.’" In the following paragraph, he writes, "How do we make them joyful, etc.," and afterward, "And the men, at the time when the Beis HaMikdash was standing, would eat the meat of the Shelamim for the sake of joy, and now that the Beis HaMikdash is not standing, we do not fulfill the obligation of joy except through wine." He did not mention at the beginning of the matter that the joy mentioned in the verse is the Korban Shelamim, but only after he specified the joy of each type separately.
b) The Rambam wrote, "That the joy mentioned here is the Korban Shelamim," while Admur HaZaken wrote, "They would eat the meat of the Shelamim for the sake of joy."

5. It appears that the opinion of Admur HaZaken differs from the opinion of the Rambam regarding the foundation of the positive commandment of "And you shall rejoice in your festival." According to the Rambam, the joy of the Korban Shelamim and the joy of rejoicing—he, his children, and his household, each as is appropriate for him (which includes the men eating meat and drinking wine)—are two separate laws and two types of joy. Admur HaZaken, however, holds that they are one (law and) type of joy.

This is the precision in the Rambam’s language: "Even though the joy mentioned here is the Korban Shelamim... included in that joy is that he, his children, etc., should rejoice, each as is appropriate for him." This means that although this is included in the Torah-mandated positive commandment, "And you shall rejoice in your festival," it is only "included in that joy"—meaning it is an additional joy included in that same joy, but the joy mentioned in the verse is the Korban Shelamim.

But according to Admur HaZaken, "This joy (that 'a person is obligated to be joyful and good-hearted—he, his children... and all who are attached to him') is (the very) positive commandment from the Torah, as it is written, ‘And you shall rejoice in your festival, you, your son, your daughter, etc.’"

This means that the joy of each person as is appropriate for him—the children with roasted grains and nuts, the women with clothing and jewelry, and the men, etc.—is the very joy mentioned in the verse, "And you shall rejoice in your festival, you, etc." It is just that for the men, the manner of fulfilling the joy appropriate for them changed: at the time when the Beis HaMikdash was standing, they would eat the meat of the Shelamim for the sake of joy, and now that the Beis HaMikdash is not, etc., the obligation of joy is through wine.

6. The explanation for this:
According to the Rambam, "The joy mentioned here is the Korban Shelamim." This means that the obligation of joy is not the eating of meat that arouses joy, but a specific joy in the fact that one brings a sacrifice to Hashem. This itself is the act of joy of "And you shall rejoice in your festival," as the Rambam writes in the Laws of Chagigah: "And the joy mentioned regarding the festivals is that one should offer Shelamim beyond the Shelamim of the Chagigah. These are what are called the Shelamim of the joy of the Chagigah, as it is written, ‘And you shall slaughter Shelamim and eat there and rejoice before Hashem your God.’" From the plain language, it is implied that the act of joy is not only the eating of the meat of the Shelamim, but the totality of the "Korban Shelamim" (which also includes bringing it before Hashem) is the act of joy of "And you shall rejoice in your festival."

Beyond this, "Included in that joy is that he, his children, and his household should rejoice, each as is appropriate for him." This joy is not a mitzvah of an act of joy, but the doing of things that arouse and bring joy to each person as is appropriate for him. This mitzvah the men fulfill by eating meat and drinking wine, even at the time of the Beis HaMikdash (even through non-sacred meat), because this eating of meat is a different type of joy: that he eats meat and drinks wine, which bring and arouse one to joy.

Thus, at the time of the Beis HaMikdash, there were two fulfillments of the mitzvah of joy for men: 1) The obligation of the Korban Shelamim—bringing the sacrifice and eating it; 2) The eating of meat (non-sacred) and the drinking of wine.

However, according to Admur HaZaken, there is one fulfillment of the positive commandment, "And you shall rejoice in your festival," which is that "a person is obligated to be joyful and good-hearted—he, his children, his wife, his household, and all who are attached to him."

It is just that for men, the "primary joy" "is the eating of the meat of the Shelamim." Therefore, "at the time when the Beis HaMikdash was standing, they would eat the meat of the Shelamim for the sake of joy, and now that the Beis HaMikdash is not standing, we do not fulfill the obligation of joy except through wine." But the fulfillment of the joy of eating the meat of the Shelamim at the time of the Beis HaMikdash does not differ in its definition from the fulfillment of the mitzvah of joy in the present time for men. The foundation of the obligation is the eating of meat (of the Shelamim) and the drinking of wine, which bring and arouse the person to joy. It is only that the eating of the meat of the Shelamim is the "primary joy," as mentioned above.

It can be said that they differ in the interpretation of the verse, "And you shall rejoice in your festival (Chagecha)." For the Rambam, "Chagecha" means "your sacrifice (Korban)," meaning that one is obligated to rejoice in (bringing) the sacrifice. Unlike Admur HaZaken, for whom the meaning of "(And you shall rejoice) in your festival (Chagecha)" refers to the time of the festival, that there is an obligation to rejoice during the appointed seasons, but the Torah added and established that the primary fulfillment of the joy comes through the eating of the meat of the Shelamim.

7. Based on this, it is easily understood why Admur HaZaken concludes here, "Therefore, it is customary to increase the variety of foods on Yom Tov more than on Shabbos, regarding which joy is not mentioned."

According to the above, for Admur HaZaken, the definition of the mitzvah of joy on Yom Tov is not an act of joy belonging to the Heavens (as the Rambam holds, that the joy mentioned here is the Korban Shelamim), but a (natural) joy that comes through the eating of meat and the drinking of wine by the men (and for women and children as is appropriate for them, as mentioned above). It is only that the primary joy is the eating of the meat of the Shelamim at the time of the Beis HaMikdash. Unlike the joy mentioned regarding Shabbos, which is "And on the day of your gladness," meaning that the essence of this joy is a joy of the Heavens, not a natural joy that comes through the eating of meat and other things that bring joy.

This is also understood from the opinion of Rabbi Nasan in the Sifrei there, who disagrees with the Tanna Kamma and holds that "‘And on the day of your gladness’—these are the Tamid offerings." Tamid offerings are burnt offerings (Olos) which do not involve human consumption, and their joy is only the joy of offering a sacrifice. It can be said that from this, it is understood even for the Tanna Kamma that what is said, "‘And on the day of your gladness’—these are the Shabbosos," means a joy that is entirely a joy of the Heavens.

8. However, although according to the above explanation of Admur HaZaken, the definition and foundation of the joy of Shelamim is not a joy of the Heavens but is included in the natural joy through things that bring and arouse one to joy, each as is appropriate for him—nevertheless, on the other hand, it is precisely according to Admur HaZaken that the joy of eating meat on Yom Tov is connected with the eating of sacred offerings (Kodshim).

For the Rambam, the joy of the men "as is appropriate" for them has no connection with the joy of the Shelamim; rather, it is a separate obligation fulfilled even through the eating of non-sacred meat and the drinking of wine (as mentioned above), and it is nothing but a physical joy. Unlike Admur HaZaken, who wrote explicitly (as mentioned above) that the primary joy is the eating of the meat of the Shelamim. This is not merely a physical joy, but a joy of a mitzvah, which contains holiness (the eating of Kodshim).

It can be said that even the joy in the present time must be (according to Admur HaZaken) in a manner that emphasizes that it is not just a physical joy, but a joy of mitzvah and Torah. This clarifies Admur HaZaken’s language regarding the law that now we do not fulfill the obligation of joy except through wine, for which he brings the verse, "As it is written, ‘And wine gladdens the heart of man’" (even though the Rambam did not bring the verse). It can be said that his intent in this is to emphasize that the fulfillment of the obligation of joy must be based solely on Torah, and therefore it is only through the drinking of wine (and not through "other types of joy," such as eating meat)—because only regarding wine "do we find a verse" (in the words of the Bach) that its nature is to gladden "the heart of man" (unlike "meat (that is not of the Shelamim)... we do not find a verse written regarding it of joy").

This also fits the continuation of Admur HaZaken’s language regarding the eating of meat in the present time: "There is a mitzvah in eating meat on Yom Tov, since joy is mentioned regarding it. And since we cannot rejoice in it through the primary joy—which is the eating of the meat of the Shelamim—we must rejoice in it through other types of joy." From the length of his language, it is implied that the eating of meat in the present time is (not just a simple joy, but) a kind of continuation of the eating of the meat of the Shelamim at the time of the Beis HaMikdash. Based on the above, it can be said that even the joy from the eating of meat in the present time must be in a way of holiness, etc. (and not just a physical joy), similar to the joy in the eating of the meat of sacred offerings.

In this, one can also explain the difference in the order of the laws between the Rambam and the Shulchan Aruch of Admur HaZaken:
The Rambam, after specifying the modes of joy and concluding, "And the men eat meat and drink wine, for there is no joy except with meat, and there is no joy except with wine," continues in the same Halacha: "And when he eats and drinks, he is obligated to feed the convert, the orphan, and the widow, etc. But one who locks the doors of his courtyard and eats and drinks—he, his children, and his wife—and does not feed and give drink to the poor and the bitter of soul, this is not the joy of a mitzvah, but the joy of his belly, and regarding these it is said, etc."

Unlike the Shulchan Aruch of Admur HaZaken, who, although he also brings these laws, did not write them in the same paragraph, but after several paragraphs (some of which also originate in the Rambam, but in the Rambam they appear after this Halacha).

Based on the above, it is explained that according to the Rambam, there are two joys here: 1) The joy of the Korban Shelamim, which is not a natural joy but an act of joy of the Heavens. 2) The joy of each and every person, which is a natural joy, and it is explained in Halacha 18. Therefore, the Rambam needs to add immediately in that same Halacha that regarding this natural joy, "And when he eats and drinks, he is obligated to feed, etc. But one who locks, etc., and does not feed and give drink, etc., this is not the joy of a mitzvah, but the joy of his belly, etc."

Unlike the opinion of Admur HaZaken, for whom even the joy of each person as is appropriate for him is not a simple natural joy, but is a part (and branch) of "the primary joy, which is the eating of the meat of the Shelamim." This means that even in the eating of meat and the drinking of wine, it is felt that the primary joy is a continuation of what they would fulfill at the time "when the Beis HaMikdash was standing, (when) they would eat the meat of the Shelamim for the sake of joy." Therefore, although one must say and inform us of the law that "when he eats and drinks, he is obligated to feed the convert, the orphan, and the widow... But one who locks the doors of his courtyard, etc., and does not feed and give drink, etc., this is not the joy of a mitzvah, etc.," nevertheless, it should not be added in the same breath as the actual obligation and mitzvah of the joy of Yom Tov.

9. An explanation of the dispute between the Rambam and Admur HaZaken in the inner dimensions of the matter:
In the discourses of Admur HaZaken, the reason why the joy of Yom Tov is through the offering of the Shelamim of joy is explained: The primary joy is to rejoice in Him, may He be blessed, as the saying goes, "And all of Israel will rejoice in You." Although such joy should be present all year round, nevertheless, the Yom Tov is called "appointed seasons for joy," for then the joy of the mitzvah is with greater intensity and strength due to the additional revelation of light. However, in order for the revelation of joy in Hashem to be also in the animal soul—which hides and conceals the Divine spark in the person’s soul and prevents the joy in Hashem—they would offer Shelamim of joy, which is to make the animal soul rejoice as well.

But regarding this very thing—the action in the animal soul—it can be explained in two ways: 1) The primary joy of Yom Tov, which is the joy in Him, may He be blessed, is only in the Divine soul, and the action of offering the Shelamim is only to remove the concealment and hiding that comes from the animal soul, which prevents the revelation of the joy of the Divine soul. 2) The action in the animal soul is not only to remove the obstacle within it, but that it itself should also feel the joy in Hashem.

It can be said that this is the difference (in the inner dimension) between the opinion of the Rambam and the opinion of Admur HaZaken:
According to the Rambam, the joy mentioned in the verse, "And you shall rejoice in your festival," "is the Korban Shelamim." This is not a natural and physical joy, but only a joy in Hashem, and it is the joy of the Divine soul in Him, may He be blessed. However, "included in that joy is that he should rejoice... each as is appropriate for him... eating meat and drinking wine, etc." Because in order that there should not be an obstacle from the side of the animal soul to the joy of the Divine soul, one also needs a special action to nullify its opposition to the joy of Yom Tov, and this is by making it rejoice with a natural and physical joy, "each as is appropriate for him."

But the opinion of Admur HaZaken is that the joy of Yom Tov must be in a way that even the animal soul feels the primary joy that is in the joy of Yom Tov, which is the joy in Him, may He be blessed. Therefore, he holds that "And you shall rejoice in your festival" is through the eating of the meat of the Shelamim, in which there are two extremes: the joy is from the eating of meat, which effects a natural joy in the body and the animal soul, but together with this, it is the eating of sacred offerings, in which a matter of holiness is felt in this eating—meaning that even in the animal soul, the matter of joy in Him, may He be blessed, is felt.

It can be said that the Rambam and Admur HaZaken follow their respective consistent views:
The opinion of the Rambam is that in the World to Come there is no eating, etc., for the ultimate perfection of the reward is for souls without bodies.
