Therefore, he holds that the joy mentioned here is the Korban Shelamim, which is not a joy connected to the body and the animal soul, but a Divine joy of the soul (and the joy of the body and the animal soul in eating meat and drinking wine is only so that they do not disturb the joy of the Divine soul, as mentioned above).

But the conclusion in the teachings of Chassidus is like the opinion of the Ramban, that the ultimate perfection of the reward is for souls in bodies specifically. The intent is to make for Him, may He be blessed, a dwelling place in the lower worlds, and the ultimate perfection of the revelation will be specifically at the time of the resurrection of the dead for the soul in the body. This is because the service during the six thousand years of the world’s existence, and especially during the time of exile, is not only to effect the revelation of the soul, but also the refinement and purification of the body and the animal soul. Likewise, it is also regarding the joy of Yom Tov: the primary joy to rejoice in Hashem is not only for the soul, but the intent is to effect this joy also in the animal soul.

Therefore, according to Admur HaZaken in the Shulchan Aruch, the definition of the joy is a joy that belongs to the body—drinking wine and eating meat. Also, the foundation of the joy and the primary joy is not the joy of the Korban Shelamim, but the eating of the meat of the Shelamim, because the joy of Hashem must be felt and must penetrate the physical body in the literal sense.

(From a Sicha of Simchas Torah, 5740)

## Beha'aloscha

### 3

A. "We remember the fish that we ate in Egypt for free, the cucumbers, the melons, the leeks, the onions, and the garlic". Rashi, in his commentary on the words "the cucumbers", cites the Sifri: "Rabbi Shimon says: Why did the Manna change into every flavor except for these? Because they are harmful to nursing mothers. They tell a woman, 'Do not eat garlic or onions because of the infant.' This is like a king, etc., as it is found in the Sifri."

Now, the Ramban explains the complaint of Bnei Yisrael, "We remember... that we ate in Egypt," as "recalling the forgotten," for they had not eaten fish from the day they left Egypt until then. He explains the term "for free" according to its literal meaning: the Egyptian fishermen would force them to pull the fish, etc., and would give them some of the fish. "And the cucumbers, melons, leeks, onions, and garlic were very abundant in Egypt, for it is like a vegetable garden, and when they would dig in the gardens for them, they would eat from the vegetables." According to his interpretation, there was only one complaint: that in the desert, they lacked those foods they had eaten in Egypt "for free" (and most literal commentators on the Torah explained it similarly).

However, Rashi divides the verse into two. Regarding the words "that we ate in Egypt for free," he explains (based on the Sifri) "free from mitzvos". Regarding "the cucumbers," he explains (as above) that the Manna would not change its taste to these five species (cucumbers, melons, leeks, onions, and garlic). This means there were two complaints against the Manna: 1) That the Manna was dependent upon the performance of mitzvos, whereas the fish they ate in Egypt was "free from mitzvos". 2) In Egypt, they also had these five species, whereas in the desert, they lacked them, because "the Manna would change into every flavor except for these."

It can be said that Rashi’s necessity to divide the verse into two is hinted at by the word he quotes from the verse—"the cucumbers" (*es ha-kishu'im*). According to the Ramban’s interpretation, that the entire verse is one continuous thought, it should have said "and the cucumbers" (*ve-es ha-kishu'im*). The fact that it says "es" proves that a new subject (complaint) begins here.

B. However, there is a difficulty with his actual interpretation:
The words of Rabbi Shimon in the Sifri are: "Rabbi Shimon says: Why did the Manna change for them into every flavor they desired, except for these five species? It is like a king of flesh and blood who entrusted his son to a tutor, and he would sit and supervise him, saying, 'See that he does not eat harmful food,' etc. And that son would complain against his father, saying, 'It is not because he loves me, but because he does not want me to eat [these delicacies].'"

There is a significant difference between the language of the Sifri and the language of Rashi. The Sifri only brings the parable, whereas Rashi adds an explanation and interpretation to the words of Rabbi Shimon: "Because they are harmful to nursing mothers; they tell a woman, 'Do not eat garlic or onions because of the infant.'"

It is difficult to understand: where does Rashi derive that Rabbi Shimon’s intent is that these foods are harmful only to nursing mothers, and not as the simple implication of the parable, that they are simply "harmful foods" for everyone?

Even more puzzling: the Gemara explicitly states, "Why are they called *kishu'im* (cucumbers)? Because they are as hard (harmful) to the entire body of a person as swords." It is explicitly stated that cucumbers are harmful food for every person (and for their entire body), to the point that it is hinted at in their name. If so, surely the words of Rabbi Shimon could be interpreted literally, that these species are "harmful foods" in general [and the Rambam in *Hilchos De'os* lists "leeks, onions, and garlic" (the last three species here) among "harmful foods... that a person should not eat except for a very small amount, and only in the rainy season"]. Where does Rashi get the authority to limit the general words of Rabbi Shimon and say that his intent is that they are harmful only to nursing mothers?

[The source for this matter, "They tell a woman, 'Do not eat garlic or onions because of the infant,'" is in the Sifri (as also cited in Rashi to the Shas), but it is in the Sifri later on—on the verse, "Its taste was like the taste of a cake baked with oil." One of the explanations in the Sifri is: "Just as this breast (*shad*—the word *leshad* is related to *shadayim*, breasts) is one species that changes into many flavors, so too the Manna would change for Israel into every flavor they desired. It is like one who says to a woman, 'Do not eat garlic or onions because of the infant'" (and as explained by the commentators, the intent of the Sifri is that it would change into every flavor except for those that are harmful to nursing mothers "because of the infant").

But this matter was not brought in the statement of Rabbi Shimon. Furthermore: there, only two species were mentioned, "garlic and onions"—and there is no proof from that that the other three species are also harmful to nursing mothers.]

C. One must also understand several points of precision in Rashi’s language, among them:
1) Rashi does not copy the core of Rabbi Shimon’s statement, which is the parable of the king of flesh and blood, etc., and only brings its beginning: "It is like a king, etc., as it is found in the Sifri." Even though Rashi adds the explanation for why the taste of the Manna did not change to these species (as above), nevertheless, there is no explanation in Rashi’s words for the content of the complaint of Bnei Yisrael. That is only explained in Rabbi Shimon’s parable, where the son thinks the reason his father commanded him not to eat these foods is because he does not want him to enjoy their quality ("he does not want me to eat").
2) Why does Rashi also copy the name of the author of the statement—Rabbi Shimon?
As has been explained many times, Rashi’s intent in citing the author of a statement in select places in his commentary (even though in most places he does not cite things in the name of their author) is to hint at an additional explanation in the content of his commentary, through the knowledge that this statement was said by a specific Tanna (or Amora).
[These two points of precision are more striking when comparing this Rashi to his commentary a few verses earlier, on the verse "The people cried out to Moshe," where he wrote, "It is like a king of flesh and blood who was angry at his son, and the son went to his father’s friend and said to him, 'Go and plead for me to my father.'" This parable also has its source in the Sifri on that passage, and the author of the statement is Rabbi Shimon—and there (a) Rashi copied the entire parable (and did not note that its source is in the Sifri), (b) he omitted the name of the author of the statement!]
3) Here, Rashi wrote "because they are harmful to nursing mothers," but in his commentary to the Shas, he wrote that the Manna did not change to these five species because they are "harmful to pregnant and nursing women." It is difficult to understand why Rashi omitted "pregnant women" in his commentary on the Torah [since the reason "because of the infant" seemingly applies to pregnant women just as it does to nursing mothers. Furthermore: it is logical—especially when learning according to the *peshat*—to increase as much as possible the number of Bnei Yisrael for whom the Manna did not change to these species; if so, Rashi certainly should have added in his commentary on the Torah that they are also harmful to pregnant women].
4) Why does Rashi place "garlic" before "onions" ("Do not eat garlic or onions")—the opposite of their order in the verse—"the onions and the garlic"?

D. The explanation for all of this is, simply:
In this verse, the complaint of Bnei Yisrael against Moshe Rabbeinu is explained, that there is a deficiency in their situation in the desert compared to their situation in Egypt. According to this, if you say that these foods are harmful and dangerous to a person’s body, there is no place for the complaint "We remember... that we ate in Egypt". On the contrary, they should have been grateful that now there is no place for harmful foods!

Even though Rabbi Shimon brought the parable of the king’s son who was "complaining against his father, saying, 'It is not because he loves me [and wants to prevent me from eating harmful foods], but because he does not want me to eat,'" and similarly in the subject matter, that Bnei Yisrael complained against Hashem "saying, 'It is not because He loves me, but because He does not want me to eat'"—seemingly, this is not a sufficient explanation. For in the parable, where it concerns a small child, it is understood that he complains against his father (even though his "tutor" tells him it is harmful food) because he craves harmful foods. But in the subject matter, it is difficult to say that Bnei Yisrael would make a claim that has no basis (since, according to the well-known reality, all of this was for their benefit, to prevent them from eating foods that are harmful to a person’s body). In any case, Moshe should have answered them that their complaint is based on a mistake, and on the contrary, the prevention of these foods is for their benefit.

And one cannot answer based on Rashi’s commentary on the verse (a few verses earlier) "Who will feed us meat"—"Did they not have meat? Has it not already been said... 'and flocks and herds'... but they are looking for a pretext"—because it is not similar to our case. The fact that they had flocks and herds was in a limited number, to the point that even Moshe Rabbeinu said to Hashem, "Six hundred thousand footmen... shall flocks and herds be slaughtered for them to suffice them?". If so, there was some room for this pretext. Not so here; the entire complaint has no basis, for on the contrary, it is from the goodness and kindness of Hashem that He prevented them from eating harmful foods!

Therefore, Rashi explains that these species are harmful only to nursing mothers, but they are not harmful foods for every person. And this was the complaint of Bnei Yisrael: Why was the flavor of these foods withheld from them solely because they are not good foods for nursing mothers? Could Hashem not have made it so that only for nursing mothers the Manna would not change into these species, while for all of Israel the quality of those foods would not be lacking?

For this reason, Rashi did not copy the entire parable of Rabbi Shimon—because the parable does not align with the subject matter. The intent of the father (in the parable) is to prevent his son from eating harmful foods, whereas in the subject matter, these foods were withheld from all of Bnei Yisrael (not because they are not good for them, but) only because of the minority of Bnei Yisrael, the nursing mothers, for whom they are harmful ("because of the infant").

Therefore, Rashi brings only the beginning of the parable (and notes the Sifri) to hint that the content of Bnei Yisrael’s complaint is similar to the complaint of the son, who thinks that the father’s intent in withholding these foods from him is only to afflict and punish him (and not for his benefit). Similarly in our case, Bnei Yisrael complained that Hashem does not want them to enjoy these foods (except that in the parable, the father’s reason is for the benefit of the son, all his children, and in the subject matter—it is for the benefit of the minority, the nursing mothers, as follows).

E. Based on this, the aforementioned points of precision in Rashi are also resolved:
1) He places "garlic" before "onions" ("Do not eat garlic or onions")—because with "garlic," it is emphasized and certain that it is a food that is harmful only to nursing mothers and not to all of Israel. For the Sages enumerated several good qualities in eating garlic, to the point that Ezra ordained that "they eat garlic on Erev Shabbos" (and because of this, all of Israel are called "garlic eaters"). Therefore, it must be that garlic is a food that is harmful only to nursing mothers, and from this, we learn about the rest, that they are foods that are harmful only to nursing mothers and not to all of Israel.
2) Rashi omits "pregnant women"—because according to the *peshat* of the verse, it is logical to minimize as much as possible the number of Bnei Yisrael for whom these foods are harmful, because only then is the complaint of Israel understood. It can be said that Rashi holds that according to the *peshat*, it is not appropriate to say (clearly) regarding the Manna that these species (as they were in the Manna) are harmful to pregnant women, because the harm from foods to pregnant women can be (primarily) from the smell of the foods, as it is in the Gemara that for a pregnant woman, what they smell touches and takes hold, "a pregnant woman who smelled." Therefore, this is not applicable regarding the Manna, where only its taste would change and not its smell.

In this way, one can resolve another point of precision in the continuation of Rashi’s language in this verse, where he explains the first three species: "The cucumbers—they are *cucumbers* in the vernacular. Melons—*burkes*. The leeks—*krisin*, *porils*. And its translation is *yis butzinia*, etc."

It is not understood:
1) What is the precision of Rashi’s language regarding "cucumbers"—"they are *cucumbers*," that he did not write this regarding melons and leeks?
2) What is Rashi’s intent in bringing "and its translation is *yis butzinia*, etc.," that he brings only the translation for "cucumbers" (*yis butzinia*), and the rest he only hints at with "etc."? For either way, he should have copied the translation also for melons and leeks [especially since "leeks" (*chatzir*) can be interpreted as the word for grass, as it is written "causing the mountains to sprout grass (*chatzir*)," and if so, Rashi certainly should have proven that here its meaning is "leeks" from the translation where he wrote *karti*]—or if he relied on the student to look up the translation himself (as we find in several places that Rashi only notes the translation), why did he copy (the beginning of) the language of the translation "*yis butzinia*"?

Based on the above, it can be said that Rashi’s main innovation is indeed regarding cucumbers. For in Rashi to the Shas, he explains *butzin* as "pumpkin," and we do not find that "pumpkin" is harmful to nursing mothers. Therefore, Rashi explains, by way of innovation, that "cucumbers" here "are *cucumbers* in the vernacular" (which are harmful to nursing mothers, as explicitly stated in Rashi), and regarding this, he concludes that "its translation is *yis butzinia*," that the translation disagrees with this, for it translates cucumbers as "*yis butzinia*," which are not harmful to nursing mothers. If so, according to the interpretation of the translation, the common denominator of these five species is not that they are harmful to nursing mothers (and it must be that the translation interprets the complaint of Israel in a different way).

This is also Rashi’s intent in copying in the commentary only "*yis butzinia*" and hinting at the rest with "etc."—because regarding the interpretation of the species, Rashi disagrees with the translation only regarding cucumbers, but from this very fact, a practical difference emerges in the interpretation of the entire verse (the "etc."), what was the content of Israel’s complaint regarding these five species.

F. However, after all this, there is room for a question (at least for a "sharp" student)—why did Hashem truly do this, to withhold from all of Israel the taste of these species because of the nursing mothers, who are a minority of a minority of all of Israel?

Therefore, Rashi copied the name of the author of the statement—Rabbi Shimon—who follows his own view, that sometimes it is possible for the minority to outweigh the majority.

As we find with emphasis regarding Rabbi Shimon, who said, "I am able to exempt the entire world from judgment from the day I was created until now, and if Elazar my son were with me, from the day the world was created until now, and if Yotam ben Uziyahu were with us, from the day the world was created until its end." Even though they are truly individuals, nevertheless, it is in their power to outweigh the entire world. This is because Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai "sees" all of Israel, and of all the generations, as one single reality, and therefore the deed of every individual and detail in Israel touches the entire collective.

Similarly, Rabbi Shimon holds the opposite: that when there is something that can harm the minority, it is worthwhile to withhold this matter also from the majority, so that there will be no concern or even a concern of a concern that this harm will reach the minority.

Simply put: so that the nursing mothers will not notice that all of Israel are enjoying these good foods and crave them, etc., it is worthwhile to withhold these foods from the majority of Bnei Yisrael.

From this, there is a lesson as to how much effort must be made for the benefit of an individual person. Furthermore—even when it concerns an infant in Israel, as in our case, where Hashem withheld these good foods from all of Bnei Yisrael so that there would be no concern of harm to nursing infants—how much more so must there be effort, even to the point of *mesiras nefesh*, for the spiritual well-being of the young boys and girls of Israel, to care for them, and especially that each one of them should receive an education on the purity of holiness, in a way that "even when he grows old, he will not depart from it".


## שלח

## Sicha 1

A. In Rashi’s commentary on our Parsha regarding the spies, we find contradictions concerning their status at the time they were sent. At the beginning of the Parsha, on the verse "all of them were men," Rashi explains, "At that time they were righteous." However, later on, regarding the verse "And Moshe called Hoshea bin Nun, Yehoshua," he explains, "He prayed for him: 'May Y-ah save you from the counsel of the spies.'" This implies that even then, Moshe sensed the wickedness of the spies. This is also explicitly stated in Rashi later on the verse "And they went and came to Moshe"—"Just as their coming was with evil intent, so too their going was with evil intent."

The commentators have already addressed this contradiction in Rashi on the verse "And they went and came" by pointing to Rashi’s earlier precision: "At that time they were righteous." This means that only at the moment they were chosen and appointed "were they righteous"—"to exclude the time after that". Once they set out on the path ("And they went"), they turned and became wicked.

But this does not resolve what Rashi says about Moshe praying for his student Yehoshua, "May Y-ah save you from the counsel of the spies," since this occurred at "that time" when they were chosen, before they began their journey.

Some commentators suggest that "immediately after they were selected" they were no longer righteous. They explain that "initially, when they were selected, they were righteous, and Moshe commanded them a mission according to the word of Hashem to see the land, how it was to be conquered. But the yetzer hara sparked within them, and they returned to Moshe to have him give them signs regarding the land: whether it was good or bad, whether the people were strong or weak, and whether the cities were fortified. Moshe sensed that their intent was for evil, for why should they need to investigate more than what they were commanded?... And therefore he wrote... 'And Moshe called Yehoshua'; when he sensed their yetzer hara, he prayed for Yehoshua."

However, this interpretation is difficult:
1) It is extremely difficult to say that Rashi’s intent in saying "at that time they were righteous" refers only to a very short duration, from the moment of appointment until they "returned to Moshe to have him give them signs regarding the land" (as, in simple terms, there was no time lapse between their appointment and Moshe’s words to them, "Go up into the Negev... and see the land," etc.).
2) According to their interpretation, Rashi should have said, "At the time of their appointment they were righteous," or something similar.
3) Most importantly: If "Moshe sensed that their intent was for evil," why did he persist in his choice to send these specific spies instead of sending others in their place whose intent would not be evil (which was certainly within Moshe’s power) ?
[The explanation offered by some commentators —that Moshe prayed for Yehoshua to be saved from the "counsel of the spies" but did not prevent him from going with them because he thought perhaps Yehoshua and Kalev could turn them back from their evil thoughts—does not resolve this question. How could he put the entire Bnei Yisrael at risk just because perhaps Yehoshua and Kalev might succeed in turning them back to good? Especially when he could have chosen not to send them at all.]

B. Seemingly, one could ask a similar question regarding the entire matter of sending the spies, since according to Rashi, Moshe knew from the very beginning that it was not a desirable matter, yet he did not refrain from sending them.

For on the verse "Send for yourself men," Rashi explains: "'Send for yourself'—according to your own judgment; I am not commanding you. If you wish, send. Because the Israelites came and said, 'Let us send men before us...' Hashem said, 'I told them that it (Eretz Yisrael) is good... By their lives, I will give them a place to err through the words of the spies, so that they will not inherit it.'" This implies that even though Moshe heard from Hashem that there was no need to send spies, Moshe’s own judgment ("according to your own judgment") agreed to it.

This is stated even more explicitly in Rashi later in Parshas Devarim (on the verse "And the matter was good in my eyes"). The reason Moshe agreed to the sending of the spies was only because he thought, "Perhaps you will change your minds when you see that I am not obstructing you" (like the parable he brings there of "a man who says to his friend, 'Sell me this donkey of yours.' He says, 'Yes.' 'Will you give it to me for a trial?' He says, 'Yes.'... Once he saw that he was not obstructing him at all, the buyer said to himself, 'He is confident that I will not find any blemish in it.' Immediately he said to him, 'Take your money, I will not try it.'"). This shows clearly that even though Moshe sensed that the intent of Bnei Yisrael was not for the good (as can also be understood from the very manner of Bnei Yisrael’s request, as Rashi says there that they all approached "in confusion, children pushing the elders, etc."), he nevertheless agreed to the request of the Israelites (contrary to his own desire).

Since we see that even before that, he did not refrain from sending them (for whatever reason) even though their intent was not for the good, then one cannot ask in our case why he did not prevent the sending of the spies after "Moshe sensed that their intent was for evil."

But it is impossible to say this, because there is a great difference:
In the aforementioned Rashi, the discussion concerns the request of Bnei Yisrael, "Let us send men before us," because they did not believe in the goodness of the land, and therefore it was not good in the eyes of Hashem, for "I told them that it is good." In this, it is understood why Moshe agreed to their words: he thought that by seeing he was not afraid to send men to see the land, this itself would prove to Bnei Yisrael that the land is good, and consequently, they would turn back from their desire.

And even that which Moshe agreed to send the spies after he saw that Bnei Yisrael did not turn back—this can be explained by the fact that he chose righteous men, "the heads of the children of Israel" (as Rashi says in Parshas Devarim, "from the chosen ones among you, from the elite among you"), relying on them to see the goodness of the land and also bring back some of its fruit. Through this, it would be proven to Bnei Yisrael with tangible evidence that the land is indeed very, very good, and on the contrary, their desire to go up to the land would be strengthened.

But if you say that Moshe sensed in the very men he chose that "their intent was for evil," then there is no room to send them at all—and why did he not endeavor to prevent them ?

Therefore, it seems to me that the two matters, "at that time they were righteous" and Moshe’s prayer "May Y-ah save you from the counsel of the spies," are not inherently contradictory, as follows.

C. This can be understood by noting the precision of Rashi’s language, "May Y-ah save you from the counsel of the spies," and similarly later regarding Kalev, where Rashi notes that "he prostrated himself on the graves of the forefathers so that he would not be enticed by his colleagues to be in their counsel." Seemingly, it would have been more appropriate to use language describing their sin—such as "slander" (dibah), as it is written "And they brought out a slanderous report of the land," [and as Rashi himself says at the beginning of the Parsha, "Why is the section of the spies adjacent to the section of Miriam? Because she was stricken for matters of slander, etc.," and similarly later, "They intended to bring out a slanderous report"], or "the sin of the spies." Why did Rashi specifically use the term "counsel" (eitzah) ?

It can be said that Rashi chose this term specifically to emphasize that he does not mean that Hashem would save him from the sin of the spies, for at "that time they were righteous" (and they were not yet associated with the sin of "slander", etc.), but only from the "counsel of the spies." "Counsel" in general—not necessarily evil counsel (which only came about later during their journey, "their going was with evil counsel," as mentioned in section A from the commentators). On the contrary, since "at that time they were righteous," it was certainly "good counsel" for them. Nevertheless, Moshe prayed for Yehoshua, "May Y-ah save you from the counsel of the spies," because even though their intent was for the good, their "counsel" could potentially lead to error, etc. (as indeed happened in practice), and therefore he prayed that he be saved from it.

D. Explanation of the matter:
Throughout the entire Parsha, the mission of the spies is described with the term "to scout" (latur)—"And they scouted the land of Canaan", "scouting the land", "scouts" (tarim), and so on. The term "spies" (meraglim) is never mentioned in our Parsha in connection with this mission. [This is not because there is a flaw or undesirable content in sending spies, for we find (later in Parshas Chukas) that even after this, Moshe himself sent men "to spy out Ya'azer," and Yehoshua also sent spies].

The reason for this is:
The difference between the two terms—"to scout" (latur) or "to spy" (leragel) —is : "To scout" describes (primarily) only the act of searching and seeing alone, and in our context—seeing the land and its inhabitants. In contrast, the term "spying" denotes investigation and searching for a hidden purpose, such as spying that assists in the conquest of enemy territory [similar to what we find earlier (in Parshas Mikeitz) regarding Yosef and his brothers—"And he said to them (Yosef to his brothers), 'You are spies; you have come to see the nakedness of the land,'" and Rashi explains: "The exposure of the land, from where it is easy to conquer"]. Generally, the matter of spying is associated with the wisdom of the spy, so that based on his investigations and searches, it is possible to devise a plan (eitzah) on how to fulfill a specific mission (conquering enemy territory, etc.).

This is the reason that in our Parsha, only the term "to scout the land of Canaan" and similar terms are used—because the entire mission (in Moshe’s intent) was only regarding the act: that they should see the land and its inhabitants and tell Bnei Yisrael what they saw (and also bring back from the fruit of the land to show Israel the goodness of the land), and nothing more. But not "to spy out the land," to involve themselves in searching for "counsels" and strategies for the conquest of the land and the like.

With this, we can understand the content of Moshe’s prayer, "May Y-ah save you from the counsel of the spies":
Even though Moshe emphasized to the men he chose that the purpose of the mission was only "to scout the land of Canaan", immediately upon their gathering with him, he sensed that they viewed themselves (not merely as "scouts," but) as "spies." That is, that besides the actual deed (to go and see the land), they felt they must devise counsels and draw conclusions (based on the spying) regarding the conquest of the land, etc.

Regarding this, Moshe prayed, "May Y-ah save you from the counsel of the spies." Even though there was not yet any sin in their counsel, because at "that time they were righteous," and their entire intent was only to devise good counsels on how to assist through their spying in the conquest of the land, etc. —nevertheless, Moshe prayed that Yehoshua would have no part in the "counsel of the spies," but would remain faithful to the purpose of the mission, which was only and exclusively "to scout the land of Canaan", without mixing in the matter of "giving counsels," etc., because the conduct of "counsel (of spies)" is liable to lead to a fall (as in section E).

[Based on this, it can be said that this was also the intent of Kalev in his prayer "that he would not be enticed by his colleagues to be in their counsel"—and he did not write "in their evil counsel" (or similar)—for it can be said that at that time, it was not yet known to Kalev that "their going was with evil counsel," but he prayed that he would not be enticed by his colleagues even in their "good counsel," because he was faithful to his mission from Moshe, without deviating in the slightest from the goal, only "to scout the land," and not to be a partner to the "counsel (of spies)"].

But on the other hand, it is understood that this was not a sufficient reason to cancel their mission, because the fact that they viewed themselves at "that time" (when they were still "righteous") as "spies" does not necessarily lead to undesirable results, and it was merely a concern.

E. Based on this, another puzzling matter in the Parsha of the spies, which we seemingly do not find Rashi explaining, can be clarified:
How is it possible that these elite men, whom Moshe Rabbeinu himself chose for this mission (from which itself it is understood that they were the most suitable of all Bnei Yisrael to fulfill this mission), would turn, from one extreme to the other, to become sinners and cause others to sin, to the point that they rebelled against Hashem and said, "We cannot go up... for it is stronger than we" (as Rashi says, "As it were, they spoke against the One Above")?

According to the above, it can be said that in the very precision of Rashi’s language, "counsel of the spies," is hinted the root and the cause that brought them to decline after decline, until the rebellion against Hashem:
The term "counsel" denotes the need for searching and finding a strategy, a specific thing with a hidden and concealed intent (that the opponent should not know about it, etc.). And this is the case with the matter of the "counsel of the spies": the matter of spying requires "counsel" and strategy; spies must devise counsels and strategies on how to deceive and hide the purpose of their arrival, so that in this way they will succeed in carrying out their goal without it being known to the one who could prevent it [similar to what we find earlier regarding Yosef and his brothers—"And he said to them (Yosef to his brothers), 'You are spies; you have come to see the nakedness of the land.' And they said to him, 'No, my lord, your servants have come to buy food,'" that is, according to Yosef’s words, they hid the true purpose for which they came to Egypt ("to see the nakedness of the land") by saying they came "to buy food"].

Thus, the matter of spying itself requires conduct of cunning and hiding the truth, and only through this can they succeed in carrying out the spying.

This is the reason that Moshe wanted their mission to be limited only to the act, "to scout the land of Canaan," without them becoming "spies," entering into counsels and strategies of cunning and hiding the truth, which could potentially distance them from the trait of truth, etc. And even when the intent from the beginning is for a good purpose, to think thoughts and strategies of "good counsel" [and sometimes it is necessary to send spies, because otherwise it is impossible (naturally) to defeat the enemy, which is why, as mentioned, Moshe sent to spy out Ya'azer]—nevertheless, ultimately, the trait of cunning (and hiding the truth) can bring a person down from one level to the next, and extreme caution is required so that one does not stumble, etc..

Therefore, after Moshe sensed that those men considered themselves "spies," he prayed (immediately) for Yehoshua (his student), "May Y-ah save you from the counsel of the spies"—that he should have no part in their counsel (even if it is "good counsel"), so that he would have no connection to the "counsel of the spies".

F. This matter can be sweetened based on what is explained in Chazal, that the trait of Moshe is the trait of truth—"And truth is Moshe" (which is why the Torah was given through him, for this is the essence of Torah—"Torah of truth"). Therefore, to be a messenger of Moshe—a messenger of a person like him, "like him, truly like the sender" —it is impossible for the messenger to have any deviation from the trait of truth.

This is why Moshe’s mission was only "to scout the land" and not "to spy it out"—because in this essential matter, a mission connected to entering the land, the power of the sender (the power of Moshe) had to be in a revealed manner.

And through the fact that the messengers deviated from the line of their mission and considered themselves spies, the power of the sender was nullified in them—because in addition to the nullification of the mission due to the change, for when a messenger changes his mission (in an essential matter) the mission is nullified —here, they were disqualified in essence from being messengers of Moshe, because it is impossible for them to be "like him," like Moshe, whose trait is the trait of truth.

Only Yehoshua and Kalev, who were not enticed by the "counsel of the spies," remained messengers of Moshe, and this itself kept them from deviating from the intent and purpose of their mission, and they said, "The land is very, very good... do not fear the people of the land, for they are our bread... do not fear them". And because of this, they merited to enter the land, and even "lived from those men"—for "they took the portion of the spies in the land and rose in their stead to life".

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Shelach, 5745)

## Sicha 2

### 85

1. From the fact that the spies were called (in our Parsha) by the name "Edah" (congregation) and consisted of ten men (since Yehoshua and Kalev were not included among them), our Sages derived that any ten individuals from Yisrael, when joined together, are considered a complete "Edah".

Seemingly, it requires study why the source for this Halacha—regarding the unique status of an "Edah" in matters of holiness (that "any matter of holiness must not be less than ten")—is derived specifically from the spies (as it is written regarding them, "How long shall this evil Edah...")?

Even though the law of a holy Edah (that "there is no matter of holiness with fewer than ten") dictates that even a wicked person "joins the quorum of ten for any matter of holiness... (for) a Yisrael, even though he has sinned, remains a Yisrael and stands in his holiness, and has not departed from the category of Yisrael", this is seemingly not a sufficient reason. For [in addition to the fact that if you were to say (as is implied by the literal meaning of the text) that the spies were in the category of rebels against Hashem (as Kalev said, "Do not rebel against Hashem"), it is not simple in terms of Halacha whether one may include "rebels" in a quorum of ten for a matter of holiness, and all the more so when the entire ten are "rebels"—nevertheless,] the derivation from the spies that an "Edah" is ten also applies to the law of an "Edah" of the Sanhedrin ("And the Edah shall judge... and the Edah shall save..."), which consists of ten, where it is obvious that a wicked person is not included.

It can be said that this is one of the hints in the *Nigleh* (revealed) Torah to what is explained in the *Pnimiyus* (inner) Torah: that the spies were on a lofty level. Meaning, not

## 86

only was their intention, Heaven forbid, not rebellion in the simple sense, but on the contrary, "they were on a very high level", as will be explained later at length.

[As has been explained in many places, the *Nigleh* of Torah and the *Pnimiyus* of Torah are one Torah. Therefore, even though each part has its own definitions and rules—similar to the principle that one does not derive Halacha from Aggadah—nevertheless, since in essence they are one Torah, it is understood that there is a connection, a bond, and a unity between them (a unity, for they are like body and soul to one another, as is stated in the Zohar). Furthermore, even those matters in the *Pnimiyus* of Torah that, at first glance, do not align with the literal meaning and the Halacha, have a basis and source in the *Nigleh* of Torah, whether as a hint or explicitly.

And so it is in our case: although according to the literal meaning (and the *Nigleh* of Torah in general), the spies committed a grave sin, and because of it they themselves and, through them, the entire generation of the desert were punished, etc.—nevertheless, it must be said that even in the *Nigleh* of Torah there is at least a hint of the greatness of the spies as explained in the *Pnimiyus* of Torah.]

2. In the teachings of Chassidus (*Pnimiyus* of Torah), it is explained that the fact that the spies did not want to enter Eretz Yisrael was due to the greatness of their level in the *avodah* of Hashem. They did not want to enter Eretz Yisrael because they refused to lower themselves to the lifestyle of "six years you shall sow your field...", to occupy themselves with physical and material matters, similar to the statement of Rashbi, "If a person plows... and sows... what will become of Torah?" Their entire desire and wish (as a continuation of the Giving of the Torah) was to remain in seclusion in the desert, detached from settled life, without the burdens of livelihood, etc. (for Hashem provided their needs in the desert through the *Man*, bread from heaven, and water from the well of Miriam, and through the Clouds of Glory that accompanied them, killed the snakes and scorpions, and pressed their garments, etc.). This, indeed, is the true way to study Torah and be *dveikus* (attached) to Hashem, as the Sages said, "The Torah was only given (to be studied) to those who eat the *Man*."

[However, they erred in this, because there is a greater level of *avodah* in the lifestyle of Eretz Yisrael—the fulfillment of the *mitzvos* dependent on the land, and practical *mitzvos* in general—even compared to the level of *dveikus*, etc., in the desert.]

Based on this, it can be said that in the aforementioned matter—that it is specifically from the "Edah" of the spies that we derive that ten individuals from Yisrael are considered a holy "Edah"—it is hinted that the sin of the spies was not in its simple sense, but rather that they erred in thinking that the path (aforementioned) they chose was the primary path in the *avodah* of Hashem.

## 87

However, due to the greatness of their level and standing in the *avodah* of Hashem—and furthermore (and it can be said that this is the main point) their effort to influence Bnei Yisrael in this—this was a great and grave sin.

3. Furthermore, it can be said that the fact that the root of the spies' conduct is a matter of holiness is hinted at and learned also from the fact that the people of the generation of the desert remained in the desert for forty years, and did not live lives of suffering and burdens, but on the contrary, lives of rest. For all forty years they had *Man* for food, water from the well of Miriam, etc., and the Clouds of Glory protected them, etc. (as mentioned above).

For seemingly: this was the very claim and desire of (the spies and) the generation of the desert—to remain in the desert and not enter Eretz Yisrael—so how is it fitting that their request was fulfilled and they remained in the desert in rest, with the cloud of Hashem upon them day and night, etc.?

[And furthermore: according to what is known that the punishments of the Torah serve to rectify the blemish of the sin, until the situation becomes the opposite of the sin—it is most wondrous that in this case, their desire was not denied, nor was their sin (not entering the land) rectified and negated; rather, the conduct toward them was as they desired, that the entire generation remained all their years in the desert! And even if you say that due to their sin they were not worthy of entering Eretz Yisrael (since they despised Eretz Yisrael, they did not merit to enter it)—nevertheless, what is the explanation that the punishment would be to remain in the desert in a life of rest, etc., within the Clouds of Glory, with Moshe and Aharon at their head, etc.?]

According to the aforementioned explanation (in *Pnimiyus* of Torah), the reason for this can be said to be that the spiritual level in the desert (which was their intention, and for the sake of Heaven) is not a mistake (it is just that from this, a mistake in action branched out).

Meaning, the dwelling in the desert within the Clouds of Glory, etc., in itself contains holiness. It is just that they thought this was the final goal, even though this level is (it should be) a preparation for entering Eretz Yisrael. For in order to reach the lofty stage of entering Eretz Yisrael, the preparation and training that came through their stay in the desert for forty years was necessary.

4. One may attempt to clarify this using the words of the Rambam in *Hilchos Teshuvah*, through which one can explain this interpretation (of *Pnimiyus* of Torah) in the style of Halacha:

In Chapter 10 of *Hilchos Teshuvah*, the Rambam writes: "A person should not say, 'I will perform the *mitzvos* of the Torah and occupy myself with its wisdom so that I may receive all the blessings written in it, or so that I may merit the life of the World to Come, and I will abstain from the sins that the Torah warned against so that I may be saved from the curses written in the Torah, or so that I will not be cut off from the life of the World to Come.' It is not proper to serve Hashem in this way, for one who serves in this way serves out of fear, and it is not the level of the prophets nor the level of the wise. Only the ignorant, women, and children serve Hashem in this way, whom we educate to serve out of fear until their knowledge increases and they serve out of love."

And in the chapter before that, he wrote: "And because of this, all of Yisrael, their prophets, and their wise men longed for the days of Moshiach, so that they would rest from the kingdoms that do not allow them to occupy themselves with Torah and *mitzvos* properly, and they would find rest for themselves and increase in wisdom so that they would merit the life of the World to Come."

Seemingly, this requires explanation: How can it be said in Chapter 10 that "it is not proper to serve Hashem in this way... and it is not the level of the prophets nor the level of the wise," when just before that he writes explicitly that this is the way of the prophets and the wise (for which reason "all of Yisrael, their prophets, and their wise men longed for the days of Moshiach")? Thus, it *is* the level of the prophets and the wise!

Simply, it can be said that there are two matters: In Chapter 9, he is not referring to the manner of fulfilling Torah and *mitzvos*, but rather explaining the content and essence of the reward for *mitzvos* in the life of the World to Come, which is "the end of all reward and the final good that has no cessation or diminution". Therefore, "all the prophets longed for it", and "David longed for the life of the World to Come", and for this reason also, all of Yisrael, their prophets, and their wise men longed (for this state in the world) for the days of Moshiach, because specifically through this (they would be able to occupy themselves with Torah and *mitzvos* properly and) they would merit the life of the World to Come.

Whereas in Chapter 10, the Rambam explains the desired manner of fulfilling Torah and *mitzvos*, that the intention of occupying oneself with Torah and *mitzvos* should not be in order to merit the life of the World to Come, but out of love. Thus, even though one must long for the life of the World to Come, that is not the goal of fulfilling Torah and *mitzvos*, but a separate matter.

However, this distinction is seemingly forced, because from the language "(and they would find rest for themselves) and increase in wisdom so that they would merit the life of the World to Come," it implies that ultimately their thought and intention (even if not at the moment of fulfilling the *mitzvos*) is that the occupation with Torah and *mitzvos* is in order to merit the life of the World to Come.

5. Further on in Chapter 10, the Rambam continues (in a separate Halacha): "One who serves out of love occupies himself with Torah and *mitzvos* and walks in the paths of wisdom, not because of anything in the world, not because of fear of evil, and not in order to inherit the good, but he does the truth because it is truth, and the good will ultimately come because of it. And this level is a very great level, and not every wise man merits it. And it is the level of Avraham Avinu, whom the Holy One, Blessed be He, called His lover, because he served only out of love."

And it requires explanation, for seemingly here too he contradicts himself: In Halacha 1, he wrote that serving out of fear is the way of the ignorant, women, and children, "and it is not the level of the prophets... (and) the wise," meaning that the service and level of the wise and the prophets is service out of love. And in Halacha 2, he wrote regarding service out of love that "this level is a very great level and not every wise man merits it," implying that this is not the level of (all) the prophets and all the wise.

And furthermore: in Halacha 1, he concludes that even the ignorant, etc., who serve out of fear, "we educate them to serve out of fear until their knowledge increases and they serve out of love," implying that service out of love is relevant to everyone. Yet in Halacha 2, it is explicit that "this level is a very great level and not every wise man (even) merits it."

6. Therefore, it seems appropriate to say that the occupation with Torah and *mitzvos* "so that they would merit the life of the World to Come" in Chapter 9 does not mean in order to receive the reward of the World to Come, but in order to reach the continuation of the occupation with Torah and *mitzvos* in a manner that is in the World to Come.

The explanation of this: Regarding the love of Hashem, the Rambam continues: "And what is the proper love? It is that one should love Hashem with a great, exceeding, very strong love, until his soul is bound in the love of Hashem and he is found to be obsessed with it always... (and at the end of the chapter:) It is a known and clear matter that the love of the Holy One, Blessed be He, is not bound in the heart of a person until he is obsessed with it always... one does not love the Holy One, Blessed be He, except through the knowledge with which he knows Him... therefore a person must dedicate himself to understand and comprehend the wisdom and insights that inform him of his Creator, etc."

Meaning, the definition of love for Hashem is that his soul (and his knowledge) is bound to Hashem—the (perfection of) this matter is in the World to Come, as the Rambam wrote in Chapter 8 regarding the World to Come, that "the righteous sit with their crowns on their heads and enjoy the radiance of the *Shechinah*... (and the explanation of) 'their crowns on their heads' means the knowledge that they knew, because of which they merited the life of the World to Come, is present with them, and it is the crown... (and) they enjoy the radiance of the *Shechinah* because they know and comprehend the truth of the Holy One, Blessed be He, which they do not know while they are in the dark, lowly body." And therefore the Rambam wrote that "this is the reward, and there is no reward higher than it, and the good after which there is no good."

And together with this, they have no rest (the opposite of "sitting" and "enjoying").

Meaning, the life of the World to Come is not (only) a "reward" for the occupation with Torah and *mitzvos*, but a continuation, an ascent, and a constant drawing closer to the perfection of *dveikus* in Hashem that is achieved through Torah and *mitzvos*.

Based on this, one can understand the difference between what the Rambam wrote at the beginning of Chapter 10, "A person should not say, 'I will perform the *mitzvos* of the Torah and occupy myself with its wisdom so that I may receive all the blessings written in it, or so that I may merit the life of the World to Come,'" and what he wrote in Chapter 9, that "all of Yisrael, their prophets, and their wise men longed for the days of Moshiach (is) so that they would rest from the kingdoms that do not allow them to occupy themselves with Torah and *mitzvos* properly, and they would find rest for themselves and increase in wisdom so that they would merit the life of the World to Come":

What he wrote, "all of Yisrael, their prophets, and their wise men longed for the days of Moshiach so that they would rest from the kingdoms, etc., and increase in wisdom so that they would merit the life of the World to Come," does not mean that the goal "in order to merit the life of the World to Come" is (to receive) a prize, receiving a reward for their work, but in order to merit the ultimate purpose and perfection of the love and *dveikus* in Hashem (which he speaks of earlier), to be obsessed with it always, which is through increasing and adding constantly to wisdom in the days of Moshiach.

Whereas in Chapter 10, the Rambam rejects performing the *mitzvos* of the Torah, etc., with the intention of meriting the life of the World to Come as a receipt of reward for his work, which is why the Rambam included it among other matters of benefit and reward for his work—"so that I may receive all the blessings written in it... so that I may be saved, etc." And since his intention is not for the ascent to the perfection of the *avodah* but for receiving a reward, therefore the Halacha is "A person should not say, etc."

[However, in this itself there are two modes:
In what he wrote at the beginning of Chapter 10, "A person should not say, etc., so that I may receive all the blessings, etc., or so that I may merit the life of the World to Come, and I will abstain from the sins, etc., so that I may be saved from the curses, etc., or so that I will not be cut off from the life of the World to Come," the Rambam's intention is to reject service out of fear, meaning that the intention of the person serving is because he fears for the life of his soul, and therefore he serves Hashem so that he will have a life of rest in this world (and be saved from the curses, etc.), or (for one for whom the life of the soul is relevant) so that his soul will merit the eternal life of the World to Come (and not be cut off from the life of the World to Come).

And further on in Halacha 4, the Rambam writes, "The early Sages said, 'Lest you say, "I will study Torah so that I will be rich, etc., so that I will receive a reward in the World to Come," the Torah says, "to love, etc."'"—meaning that his intention "to receive a reward" is (not because he fears for the life of his soul, but) because he wants to receive compensation and payment for his work (a physical payment—"so that I will be rich," or a spiritual payment—"so that I will receive a reward in the World to Come"), which is a lower level of one who serves not for its own sake.]

7. However, even though the fact that the wise and the prophets longed to merit the life of the World to Come is a continuation and a greater ascent in their service out of love, there is a higher level in service out of love that "not every wise man merits." And this is what the Rambam innovates in Halacha 2 regarding

## 91

the "level of Avraham Avinu" who "does the truth because it is truth."

It can be said that the superiority in this is:
When the service is out of love in general (which exists among all of Yisrael (and all the more so) their prophets and wise men), the occupation with Torah and *mitzvos* is in order to reach connection and *dveikus* with the Holy One, Blessed be He. Thus, the occupation with Torah and *mitzvos* is in a manner where the occupation is not for and for the sake of itself, but for a desired object, which is like an additional thing. But when the service out of love is in a manner that he "does the truth because it is truth," this rejects not only service for the sake of receiving a reward, but there is no other desired object in his service, not even the desired object of being attached to the Holy One, Blessed be He, which is the content of knowing Hashem and loving Hashem; rather, he "does the truth because it is truth," because it is the command and will of Hashem, and the fulfillment of Hashem's command is truth in and of itself.

And regarding this level, the Rambam wrote, "And this level is a very great level" (and not as he wrote in the previous Halacha, simply "the level of the prophets," "the level of the wise")—because regarding this level, one cannot say it is the level of all the wise; on the contrary, as he continues, "and not every wise man merits it," but "it is the level of Avraham Avinu, whom the Holy One, Blessed be He, called His lover." For in order to reach this great level ("does the truth because it is truth"), one must ascend to a very lofty level of love.

8. And based on all the above, one can also explain regarding the spies and the generation of the desert:
The spies (and the generation of the desert in general) were, as mentioned, "on a very high level," and "did not want to lower themselves" to enter Eretz Yisrael, into the order of service of "six years you shall sow your field, etc., a person plows, a person sows" (as mentioned in section 2), because this order of service does not allow them to occupy themselves with Torah, etc., "properly," for most of their days would be occupied with "things that the body needs". And they wanted to remain and continue and travel and ascend in the lifestyle of the desert, where the good is bestowed abundantly (as mentioned: *Man* from heaven, water from the well of Miriam, and the Clouds of Glory that went with them, as mentioned)—and there were no distractions in the *avodah* of Hashem, and they could sit free to find rest for themselves and increase in wisdom to merit the life of the World to Come. Similar to what "all of Yisrael, their prophets, and their wise men longed for, etc., so that they would rest, etc., and find rest for themselves, etc."

But even though their desire and longing for the aforementioned in the desert is a lofty level of *dveikus* in Hashem, service out of love, nevertheless, entering Eretz Yisrael brings one to a higher level, which is the fulfillment of Hashem's command without any desired object at all, "does the truth because it is truth."

However, in order to reach this level, an even higher love is required. And since the spies and the generation of the desert wanted to remain in the desert and did not want to enter the land, this means that they had not yet reached this level of the perfection of *dveikus* and the ascent of love.

And therefore, Bnei Yisrael remained in the desert in a state of the absence of all things that prevent the study of Torah and *dveikus* in Hashem to the ultimate degree, a mode of

## 92

rest and tranquility in order "that they would increase in wisdom," through which they reached the perfection of the *dveikus* of the soul, etc.. And after this, in the next generation, in a new generation and level, they entered Eretz Yisrael to fulfill the command of Hashem, both in the very act of entering and in the fulfillment of the practical *mitzvos* in the land, for specifically in this is the matter of "does the truth because it is truth" felt.

It can be said that this is also the content of the words of Yehoshua and Kalev in their response to the entire congregation of Bnei Yisrael: "If Hashem desires us, then He will bring us, etc."—that the entry into the land is connected with "Hashem desires us," this is the desire and will of Hashem, and consequently, in its fulfillment, "Hashem desires us" is revealed, the *dveikus* of Bnei Yisrael in Hashem in a higher manner than the *dveikus* through "they will increase in wisdom" in the desert.

9. And here, the Rambam, when speaking about one who "does the truth because it is truth," concludes, "and the good will ultimately come because of it." Seemingly, what is the relevance of this conclusion here—for we are talking about such a wise man who does not think at all about the good that is coming to him from his work.

It can be said that this is understood from the precision of the Rambam's language, "and the good will ultimately come because of it". Meaning, when his work is in such a manner that he "does the truth because it is truth," then the good that comes is also higher than the good of the World to Come in the regular sense. And consequently, also from the aspect of the matter of connection and the *dveikus* of his soul in Hashem, he reaches a higher perfection in this—than one whose study of Torah and fulfillment of *mitzvos* is in a manner where he has a desired object of connection and *dveikus* in Hashem.

Based on this, one can explain the continuation of the words of Yehoshua and Kalev, who said (earlier) "The land is good, very, very". Even though through the mode of *avodah* in the desert, studying Torah in a manner where they were free in wisdom, they merit "to see the goodness of Hashem in the land of the living"—the World to Come, which is "a very great good and has no value in the goods of this world," which "that great good, which is beyond investigation and has no value or comparison, is what David said, 'How great is Your goodness which You have hidden for those who fear You, etc.'" (as the Rambam wrote in *Hilchos Teshuvah*)

—because through the entry into Eretz Yisrael and the fulfillment of the *mitzvos* there—meaning the mode of *avodah* of "Hashem desires us"—"does the truth because it is truth"—they merit an even greater good. Not only "very" once (which indicates a great good that is beyond the ability of a person to receive in this world, and it is the good of the World to Come) that comes through the *avodah* of Hashem in the category of "very," "a great, exceeding, very strong love, until his soul is bound in the love of Hashem and he is found to be obsessed with it always," but "very, very" twice. And as the Rambam wrote that "he does the truth because it is truth, and the good will ultimately come because of it," that there is a good that comes specifically through this lofty *avodah*, which even regarding a great, exceeding, very strong love is in the category of "very," for this indicates the total *bitul* (nullification) of the person before the Holy One, Blessed be He.

10. And here, from the fact that in the Torah, which is an eternal (instruction)—the story of the spies and the generation of the desert and the response of Yehoshua and Kalev to their claim is told, it is understood that it is within the power of every individual from Yisrael to conduct himself according to the demand of Yehoshua and Kalev, which is above the level of "on a very high level" of the *avodah* of the spies (and the generation of the desert).

Meaning: This level of fulfilling practical *mitzvos* in the land, through which they come to "the land is good, very, very" (twice), is not only for those whose entry into the land came after being in the desert (and forty years), but it exists in every individual from Yisrael. For when he fulfills *mitzvos* with the acceptance of the yoke because Hashem so commanded, there is in this acceptance of the yoke a superiority over the *dveikus* that comes from love.

And it can be said that this too is hinted at in the language of the Rambam:

In continuation of his words regarding "the level of Avraham Avinu, whom the Holy One, Blessed be He, called His lover, etc.," the Rambam writes, "And this is the level that the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded us through Moshe, as it is said, 'And you shall love Hashem your G-d.' And at the time when a person loves Hashem with the proper love, immediately he will perform all the *mitzvos* out of love" (and in the following Halacha he continues to explain "what is the proper love")—and seemingly it is not understood: after he wrote that "this level is a very great level and not every wise man merits it," how is it fitting to say "And this is the level that the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded us... through Moshe," meaning that all of Yisrael were commanded in it?

Rather, the Rambam comes to inform us that what he wrote, "and not every wise man merits it," refers to one's own strength and *avodah*, but (through and) by the power of Hashem's command (through Moshe), everyone can reach it.

11. However, seemingly one must still examine, for love is a trait of the heart, which one reaches through contemplation of the greatness of Hashem, as the Rambam elaborated at the end of the chapter (and also in *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah*). What is the meaning that every individual from Yisrael can reach the "proper love" according to the level of the love of Avraham Avinu?

This will be understood by prefacing what the Rambam wrote in Halacha 4: "The early Sages said, 'Lest you say, "I will study Torah so that I will be rich, so that I will be called Rabbi, so that I will receive a reward in the World to Come," the Torah says, "to love Hashem." Everything you do, do only out of love. And the Sages further said, etc., and so the great wise men would command their discerning students, etc., "Do not be like servants, etc."'"

And seemingly it is puzzling, why did the Rambam wait to reject the intention "so that I will be rich, etc." until after the explanation of the greatness of the level of service out of love (the level of the love of Avraham Avinu (in Halacha 2) and the description of the "proper love" (in Halacha 3)), and did not include it in Halacha 1 regarding one who serves out of fear, that "a person should not say, 'I will perform the *mitzvos* of the Torah, etc., so that I may receive all the blessings, etc.'"?

[For since this is a lower level of one who serves not for its own sake (as mentioned in section 6)—he should have rejected this level in "not for its own sake" before the elaboration on the greatness of the level of Avraham Avinu and the "proper love."]

It can be said that in this, the Rambam emphasizes that the service out of love "that the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded us through Moshe" is relevant to every individual from Yisrael, even to one who is still on a very low level, who of his own accord would be "studying Torah so that I will be rich, etc."

The explanation of the matter:
In "proper love" there are two levels:
(a) The feeling of love in the heart, and "this level is a very great level," and it is the level of Avraham specifically, as he elaborates in Halacha 2, "And what is the proper love? It is that one should love Hashem with a great, exceeding, very strong love, until his soul is bound in the love of Hashem, etc."

(b) Even one who has not reached the level of Avraham to love Hashem with the proper love (which brings a person to "do the truth because it is truth"), and on the contrary, of his own accord he is on the level where he says, "I will study Torah so that I will be rich, etc."—even to him the instruction of the Torah was given, "to love Hashem, everything you do, do only out of love." Meaning, even though he does not feel the proper love in his heart, nevertheless he can act upon himself to do it out of love, meaning because Hashem commanded it, in the likeness of this level of the "very great level" of "doing the truth because it is truth."

And this is what he continues in the following Halacha (after he explains the matter of service for its own sake and not for its own sake, "And the Sages said, 'A person should always occupy himself with Torah, even if not for its own sake, for from not for its own sake, one comes to for its own sake'")—"Therefore, when we teach children, women, and the general ignorant, we teach them only to serve out of fear and in order to receive

## Sicha 3

1. The author of the Shulchan Aruch writes in the Laws of Tzitzis : "The primary mitzvah of a tallis katan is to wear it over one's garments so that one will always see it and remember the mitzvos." The Magen Avraham there notes: "And in the 'Kesavim' (referring to the writings of the Arizal), [it is stated] that the tallis katan should specifically be worn under one's garments."

Simply put, this difference in rulings is the source for the variation in customs regarding the wearing of the tallis katan: those who follow the ruling of the Shulchan Aruch wear the tallis katan over their garments, while those who wear it under their garments follow the opinion of the Magen Avraham based on the writings of the Arizal.

Regarding this difference in custom concerning the garment (the tallis katan), there is a further distinction regarding the tzitzis themselves—many of those who wear the tallis katan under their garments follow the custom of having the tzitzis hang outside.

One must examine whether this custom of having (only) the tzitzis outside is also based on the writings of the Arizal—meaning that according to the Arizal, the tallis katan must be under the garments, but the tzitzis themselves must be outside—or whether this is done merely to be stringent in accordance with the ruling of the Shulchan Aruch, while according to the Arizal, the tzitzis should also be covered under the garments.

Now, from the continuation of the Magen Avraham’s language, "And it seems to me that the tzitzis must be outside, etc.," one cannot seemingly infer that even according to the "Kesavim" they must be outside—because [aside from the fact that one could say the Magen Avraham’s intention is also to be stringent to satisfy the opinion of the Shulchan Aruch, it is possible that] the primary intention of the Magen Avraham in writing "that the tzitzis should be outside" is not that they be outside the garments (in a way that one can always see them), but rather that they be outside the corners, as he continues there: "and not like those who tuck them into the corners, similar to what is stated in the Gemara, 'there is no tzitzis unless it hangs out, etc.'" (And this is also implied in the Shulchan Aruch of the Alter Rebbe, who did not cite what is written in the "Kesavim" (as the Magen Avraham did), yet he copied the words of the Magen Avraham in a separate paragraph: "The tzitzis must be outside, as it is said, 'And you shall see it,' and not like those who tuck it into the corners").

## 96

Furthermore, it seems one cannot decide the matter based on what the Alter Rebbe brought (from the Levush): "And since the reason for wearing tzitzis is to remember the mitzvos, therefore one who wears a tallis katan should be careful to wear it over his garments so that he will always see it and remember the mitzvos, and those who wear it under their garments should be careful to wear it in a way that they will always see the tzitzis, for through seeing them they will always remember the mitzvos"—because even here, it is not clear if this is because the primary requirement in the writings of the Arizal applies only to the tallis katan and not to the tzitzis, or if this is only to be stringent in accordance with the halachic ruling (the words of the Shulchan Aruch) that the tzitzis must be outside the garments.

2. Seemingly, this doubt can be resolved based on what is written in the source of these matters in the writings of the Arizal, that "my teacher (the Ari) zalleh would behave as follows when he wore tzitzis: The tallis gadol is over all the garments, and the tallis katan is under all the garments, and not like the opinion of some Kabbalists who place it over all the garments, which is an error ; however, he would place it over the undershirt." And the reason for this is explained there, that "the matter of the tzitzis of the tallis katan is that it is the aspect of the *penimiyus* (inner dimension), etc., and the tallis gadol is, etc.."

It is understood that the aforementioned reason regarding the tallis katan applies (also and primarily) to the tzitzis, "which is the aspect of the *penimiyus*" (and in particular, since the garment itself has no inherent holiness, etc.). According to this, the tzitzis of the tallis katan should also be covered —

## 97

under the garments and not outside.

[And this is also proven from what is written in the Zohar that a certain "bar nash" encountered Rabbi Chiya and Rabbi Yosi and did not see them wearing tzitzis, meaning that their tzitzis were not visible on the outside. To such an extent that even when the "bar nash" suspected them that their conduct was not as it should be, they did not explain to him (even after he saw that they were "zaka'in" - righteous) why they behaved this way, because according to their view, it was simple that the tzitzis must also be covered.]

According to this, those who follow the custom of revealing the tzitzis are doing so only to satisfy the opinion of the Shulchan Aruch as well, but according to the writings of the Arizal, there is no need for this. On the contrary, this is the reason for those who follow the custom of wearing the tallis katan under the garments in such a way that the tzitzis are also covered.

3. One must still examine the conduct of many who walk in the light of the teachings of the Alter Rebbe in his Shulchan Aruch, including some Chassidei Chabad—that their tzitzis were not exposed on the outside. Seemingly, this is difficult to understand, for the words of the Alter Rebbe in his Shulchan Aruch are clear: "those who wear it under the garments should be careful to wear it in a way that they will always see the tzitzis, etc."

[And one cannot answer this in the way we find regarding "cutting the hair in the style of the gentiles" (*mesaper komi*), which is a "way of the Amorites," that "Avtolmus bar Reuven was permitted to cut his hair in that style because he was close to the government"—for we have seen many of the aforementioned individuals whose tzitzis were not visible even though they were not "close to the government" (and even those who had to intercede for the Jewish people with the government did so even at times when they were not engaged in that work).

It is also understood that the reason for this is not because at that time they were walking among the gentiles, etc., because we are speaking of Chassidim who kept all the customs of Israel, and it goes without saying—the halachic ruling of the Alter Rebbe in his Shulchan Aruch!]

Perhaps this can be explained based on what is recounted : "From the mouth of the famous Gaon and Chassid, R' Hillel Paritcher, that once they asked the Alter Rebbe how to act in a matter where the Kabbalists disagree with the Poskim, and the Alter Rebbe replied: 'Presumably, one should act according to the Kabbalists.' They asked him: 'But you yourself wrote in your Shulchan Aruch that one should follow the Poskim?' He replied: 'That is what the Poskim write (and in the Shulchan Aruch, I followed the Poskim), but the Kabbalists write to follow the Kabbalists against the Poskim (who ruled against the Kabbalists; and it is understood that one cannot say that the Kabbalists would be against the Talmud (or the Poskim who learned their words from the Gemara, in which all of Israel is equal), as explained in the Shulchan Aruch, Siman 68).'"

Based on this, the conduct of those who wore the tallis katan and the tzitzis inside, which is based on what is written in the writings of the Arizal (as mentioned above), is in accordance with the instruction of the Alter Rebbe himself: to act according to the Kabbalists (the Arizal, etc.) in a place where it is not in accordance with the Poskim.

However, there is still room for study: according to the above, the custom of all those who walk in the light of the teachings of the Alter Rebbe should have been that the tzitzis be covered together with the tallis katan. In practice, we see that many follow the ruling of the Alter Rebbe in his Shulchan Aruch to wear the tallis katan under the garments in a way that the tzitzis are visible—which is not in accordance with the aforementioned writings of the Arizal.

4. Perhaps this can be explained by prefacing an explanation of the essence of the matter of wearing tzitzis under the garments. Seemingly, one should wonder: the reason why, according to the revealed Torah, the tzitzis must be exposed is based on the explicit verse (which is the content of the mitzvah of tzitzis): "And you shall see it..."

## 100

It is not fitting for a Chassid to exempt himself from this mitzvah; rather, one should always strive to be wrapped in a garment that is obligated in tzitzis so that he may fulfill this mitzvah, etc." And in the following halachah : "A person should always be careful in the mitzvah of tzitzis, for the verse equates it and hangs all the mitzvos upon it, as it is said, 'And you shall see it and remember all the mitzvos of Hashem.'"

The Tur also brought in his book the merit of being careful in the mitzvah of tzitzis, and his words are: "Even though a person is not obligated to buy a tallis... in order to become obligated in tzitzis, nevertheless, it is good and proper for every person to be careful and diligent in the mitzvah of tzitzis, that he should have a small garment with tzitzis that he wears all day, for the essence of its mitzvah is the remembrance of the mitzvos, and at every hour and every moment one needs this, etc."

We see that the Rambam divided the striving and the care in the mitzvah of tzitzis into two details in two halachos. First, he speaks about the mitzvah of tzitzis itself, that "it is not fitting, etc., to exempt himself from this mitzvah, etc.; one should always strive, etc., in order to fulfill this mitzvah." And in a separate halachah, he explains the care that comes from this, that the tzitzis leads to the remembrance of all the mitzvos—"for the verse equates it and hangs all the mitzvos upon it."

The Tur, however, included them together as one subject and one sequence, and did not mention the striving to fulfill the mitzvah of tzitzis as a separate matter, but rather the care in the mitzvah of tzitzis from the perspective of the remembrance of all the mitzvos that is in it, and he emphasizes that this matter is the "essence of its mitzvah."

According to the above, it is understood that for the Rambam, "And you shall see it and remember all the mitzvos of Hashem, etc." (that "the verse equates it and hangs all the mitzvos upon it") is an additional matter to the essence of the mitzvah of tzitzis [ftnref_847_*46, 848_47], which is why he divided them into two details in two halachos, and emphasizes their difference: the obligation of striving in the mitzvah of tzitzis itself is "the fulfillment of this mitzvah"; the care in the mitzvah of tzitzis is because there is another matter in it (aside from the essence of the mitzvah)—"for

## 101

the verse equates it and hangs all the mitzvos upon it."

The Tur, however, holds that "And you shall see it and remember all the mitzvos of Hashem" is of the essence of the mitzvah of tzitzis [ftnref_850_49, 851_50].

[And as the Bach explained the words of the Tur in this, that he precedes wrapping himself in tzitzis before putting on tefillin, "because tzitzis come in order to remember all the mitzvos, but tefillin is a unique mitzvah, etc."

And it is worth noting the words of the Bach regarding the reason the Tur brought the intention in the mitzvah of tzitzis: "And he should intend when he wraps himself that the Place [Hashem] commanded us to wrap ourselves so that we will remember all His mitzvos to do them" (even though "it is not his way in this work of his to explain an intention for any verse in the Torah"), "for since it is written in the parsha of tzitzis, 'in order that you remember, etc.,' it shows that the essence of the mitzvah and its fulfillment depend on its intention, that he should intend at the time of the fulfillment of the mitzvah," "that he did not fulfill the mitzvah as it should be if he does not intend that intention [ftnref_855_54, 859_57]."]

Therefore, one should not divide them into two matters, as is explicit in his language: "that every person should be careful and diligent in the mitzvah of tzitzis... for the essence of its mitzvah is the remembrance of the mitzvos."

7.
