Now, all of this is regarding the fulfillment of the mitzvah of tzitzis according to the essential law, meaning in a tallis gadol, where even according to the opinion that "And you shall see it" is not of the essence of the mitzvah, it is a detail of the mitzvah. Consequently, it is simple that the mitzvah of tallis gadol is to wear it over the

## 102

garments in a way that it contains "And you shall see it and remember, etc."

But regarding the tallis katan, the Poskim disagree—whether they ordained that a person should fulfill the mitzvah of tzitzis all the time as a private mitzvah (in addition to the fulfillment of the mitzvah of tzitzis in a tallis gadol, in which he fulfills (also) "And you shall see it"), or whether the entire ordinance of the tallis katan was in order to remember all the mitzvos at all times.

According to the opinion of the Shulchan Aruch, "the essence of the mitzvah of tallis katan is to wear it over his garments so that he will always see it and remember the mitzvos," because he follows the opinion of the Tur mentioned above, and as is explicit in his work, the Beis Yosef, that the aforementioned law is—"from what he (the Tur) said in Siman 24, that the essence of its mitzvah is on the remembrance of the mitzvos, and at every hour and every moment one needs to remember, it appears that the essence of the mitzvah of tallis katan is to wear it on top so that he will always see it and remember the mitzvos."

And in this approach stands the Alter Rebbe in his Shulchan Aruch, who at the beginning writes : "When he wraps himself in tzitzis, he should intend that the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded us to wrap ourselves in tzitzis so that we will remember all His mitzvos to do them, as it is said, 'And you shall see it and remember all the mitzvos of Hashem.'" And afterwards he continues: "And since the reason for wearing tzitzis is to remember the mitzvos, therefore one who wears a tallis katan should be careful to wear it over his garments so that he will always see it and remember the mitzvos," and he continues: "And those who wear it under the garments should be careful to wear it in a way that they will always see the tzitzis, for through seeing them they will always remember the mitzvos," emphasizing "should be careful," "should be careful," because without this, it is lacking in the essence of the mitzvah of tzitzis.

But according to what is written in the writings of the Arizal, the matter of the tallis katan is only for the fulfillment of the mitzvah of tzitzis alone, and for this reason he would place them underneath, under all the garments, because in the tallis katan, the matter of "And you shall see it, etc." does not apply (and is not relevant).

[And this is also emphasized in the distinction regarding tzitzis at night: according to the ruling in the Shulchan Aruch, "Night is not the time for tzitzis, for it is excluded from 'And you shall see it'"—everything that one wears at night or a night garment, etc., is excluded; whereas in Pri Etz Chaim, he distinguishes in this: "There is no tallis and tzitzis at night... but the tallis katan, one must sleep with it even in bed... it is never nullified... even at night it is not interrupted, only that of greatness alone is nullified, but the tzitzis (of the tallis katan)... are never nullified even at night, therefore it is called tallis gadol and tallis katan, and therefore it is proper to be careful not to remove the tallis katan from upon him

## 104

the fulfillment of all the mitzvos, and this is through "And you shall see it (and then) you will remember all the mitzvos of Hashem and do them."

And only for some of them—is there a form of the conduct of the Arizal—that the tallis katan and also the tzitzis will be covered, because they do not need (as much) the boundary of "And you shall see it" literally (throughout the entire day and every moment) for the sake of care and fulfillment of the mitzvah literally, and for them, the matter of seeing in the inner dimension shines more.

And for this reason, even in the generation before us, we did not see this conduct of wearing the tallis katan under all the garments in such a way that the tzitzis would be covered among all the Jewish people, but only among some of them, and it is possible to say that this is because it is "the edge and a trace" of the exalted avodah of the Arizal.

9. May it be the will that the care in the mitzvah of tzitzis—according to the variation of customs in this —those who bring the tzitzis outside the garments, who "carry the seal of their Master fixed on their garment," and our brothers, the Jewish people, who do not walk with tzitzis in the open, according to the conduct of the Arizal (which is then conduct (that is proper) according to Torah)—

shall hasten the fulfillment of the mitzvah of tzitzis of the Holy One, Blessed be He—in the language of our prayer, "And bring us in peace from the four corners of the earth... to our land," with the coming of Mashiach Tzidkeinu, immediately.


## קרח

## Sicha 2

113

### Sicha 2

A. Regarding the obligation of separating *terumah*, the Sages learn from the verse, "So shall you also set aside a *terumah* for Hashem," that "also you" implies: "Just as you are *bnei bris* (members of the covenant), so too your agent must be a *ben bris*," which excludes a non-Jew from being an agent.

We find in the *Gemara* a discussion regarding the parameters of this law: Rav Ashi said that when we say there is no agency for a non-Jew, this applies specifically to *terumah*, but for the entire Torah, there is agency for a non-Jew. The *Gemara* concludes that this statement of Rav Ashi is a *baruta* (a weak or erroneous opinion). Why is *terumah* different? Because the verse states "you" and "also you"—just as you are *bnei bris*, your agent must be a *ben bris*. The agency for the entire Torah is also derived from *terumah*.

Simply put, it appears that Rav Ashi’s reason for saying the law of "no agency for a non-Jew" applies only to *terumah* and not the entire Torah is because he holds that we do not derive the agency for the entire Torah from *terumah*.

However, on the face of it, it is difficult to say that the dispute here is whether we derive the agency for the entire Torah from *terumah*. It is more logical to say that the difference between Rav Ashi and the conclusion of the *Gemara* lies in the underlying *sevara* (reasoning):

Rav Ashi holds that the law "just as you are *bnei bris*..." is a *gezeiras hakasuv* (a scriptural decree). Logically, we would have said that a non-Jew can also be an agent for a Jew; therefore, you have nothing more than the specific innovation of the text, which applies only to *terumah* and not the entire Torah.

In contrast, according to the conclusion of the *Gemara*, this is a matter of *sevara*: the agent must be similar to the principal—"just as you are..., so too your agent must be..." This means that the phrase "also you" regarding *terumah* is merely a *gilui milsa* (a revelation of the matter), indicating that the definition of agency requires the agent to be similar to the principal—and therefore, there is no law of agency for a non-Jew (even) in the entire Torah.

B. It appears they disagree on the fundamental definition of agency. To understand this, we must preface it with the well-known inquiry regarding

114

agency—that a person’s agent is like himself—which can be explained in several ways:

1) "Like himself" literally—regarding the one performing the act, meaning the body of the agent is like the body of the principal (and consequently, the agent's action is attributed to the principal).

2) "Like himself"—(only) regarding the connection to the agent’s power of action or the act itself, meaning the agent’s action is like the principal’s action.

3) "Like himself"—only regarding the results of the act. In other words, regarding the *cheftza* (object) upon which the action is performed—that even the agent’s action is not the principal’s, but the agent’s action on the *cheftza* (accomplishes the same thing that the principal’s action would accomplish).

Based on this, one could say that Rav Ashi’s opinion follows the third approach: that even the act of agency is not attributed to the principal but to the agent. Since the agent does not stand in the place of the principal (not even to the extent that his act is considered the principal’s act), then if he is merely a human being who belongs to this action, he can be an agent for one who requests it and connects him to this action, similar to how one connects him with a rope and he does not resist.

Therefore, he holds that for the entire Torah there is agency for a non-Jew, because if the agency is of such a nature that even a non-Jew belongs to its performance, he can be an agent for a Jew.

However, the *Gemara’s* conclusion is that "this statement of Rav Ashi is a *baruta*," meaning this position is refuted, and it is necessary that the agent be similar to the principal ("just as you are..., so too your agent..."). This means that even if you say the matter of agency is like the third approach, where the action is the agent’s act—nevertheless, the matter of agency is impossible except for one who is "like the principal," and this is according to all the ways one explains the matter of agency.

Explanation of the matter:
In agency, there are two matters: (a) The actual fulfillment of the agency. (b) The application of the status of "agent."

All three approaches mentioned above regarding the definition of agency only concern the fulfillment of the agency: when discussing how the act of this person (the agent) is effective for another person (the principal)—then it is possible to suggest the reason for this in three ways: whether because the Torah innovated in agency that the agent’s act is effective as the principal’s act, or because this action is considered the principal’s action, or even more, that the agent’s body is considered the principal’s body and consequently his act is like the principal’s act.

But prior to this, there must be the application of the status of agency upon the agent, meaning the appointment that creates the status of "agent." And for the status of agent to apply to him, there must be a connection between the agent and the principal, for it is impossible to be the principal’s agent (in any way) without being "like the principal," as "the agent must be related in some value" to the principal.

Therefore, there is no agency for a non-Jew in the entire Torah, because he cannot be "like the principal" (the Jew).

C. Based on the above explanation of Rav Ashi’s words, we can explain another statement of Rav Ashi regarding agency, which we also find was refuted in *halacha*—the law of an agent appointing an agent, who can appoint many agents ("even a hundred"). Rav Ashi held that "if the first agent dies, all are nullified." Mar bar Rav Ashi said, "This statement of my father is a *katnut* (a youthful/immature statement): if the owner dies, is there any substance in them? They all come from the power of the owner. If the owner is present, they all exist; if the owner is not present, none of them exist."

Now, the *Acharonim* have written that the dispute between Rav Ashi and Mar bar Rav Ashi (whether they come from the power of the first agent or from the power of the owner) is that Mar bar Rav Ashi holds that the agent is like the principal himself, or at least that his action is attributed to the principal, and thus the first agent (and like him, all subsequent agents) comes from the power of the owner, the principal, for he stands in his place. In contrast, Rav Ashi holds that the agent is not like the principal himself, and his action is not attributed to the principal, but only is effective for the principal. If so, the second agent is not made an agent by the power of the principal, but by the power of the first agent.

But this requires study, for from Mar bar Rav Ashi’s language, "This statement of my father is a *katnut*," it is understood that Rav Ashi’s reasoning is refuted in *halacha* (and it is indeed ruled in *halacha* according to Mar bar Rav Ashi). Yet, the *Acharonim* brought several proofs (also) for this side of agency, that the act is considered only as the agent’s act.

Therefore, it seems that here too, Rav Ashi’s position is not only because the agent’s act is not considered the principal’s act (and only that his action is effective for the principal), but because he holds even more: that since the entire matter of agency is only regarding the results of the act on the *cheftza* (as above), there is no need for any value between the agent and the principal. That is to say: after the principal makes him an agent, the agent no longer comes from the power of the principal, but the "power" of agency is found with him, as if he were the owner of this matter. For this reason, he holds that "if the first agent dies, all are nullified," because the agents do not come from the power of the principal but from the power of the first agent.

And this is what Mar bar Rav Ashi said, that this reasoning is completely refuted—"this statement of my father is a *katnut*"—because "they all come from whose power? They come from the power of the owner." That is to say: the definition of agency is that which "comes from the power" of the principal (for the very title "agent" means that he is the power of the principal), a person’s agent is like himself, and therefore it is impossible to attribute the matter of agency to someone (the first agent, etc.) without him coming and being drawn from

116

the owner—the principal. And even if you say that the act of agency (and even the action of appointing the agent) is considered as if the agent did it (like the third approach above)—nevertheless, he is not the owner at all of the matter of "all of them... (only) come from the power of the owner," as explained at length above.

D. Based on the above, it will be understood why the teaching to exclude non-Jews from the law of agency—"just as you are *bnei bris*..."—is stated regarding *terumah*, and not regarding divorce and the like [in which the law of agency is also stated]:

There is a fundamental difference between the matter of agency in separating *terumah* and agency in other matters—divorce, etc.:

In separating *terumah*, the main thing is what is accomplished, that the *terumah* is separated from the grain. Therefore, logic dictates that the matter of agency in *terumah* does not need to be in a way that (it would be considered as if) the principal (the owner of the grain) performed the act of separation, but rather, since he is the owner of the grain, the separation must be from his intent—even though it is the agent’s action.

In contrast, regarding divorce, it is simple that the one divorcing must be the husband specifically, because divorce is (not just the action of writing and giving the *get*, but primarily) that the husband divorces his wife. It is understood that agency in a *get* means that through the agent’s action, it is considered that the husband divorced his wife.

According to this, if the Torah had written the law of "just as you are *bnei bris*..." regarding divorce and the like, one might have thought to say that it applies only to matters that require the owner’s action, and consequently the agent must be similar to the principal (the owner), as he is then fit to stand in the owner’s place.

Therefore, this law is stated specifically regarding *terumah*, to teach us that any matter of agency (in whatever matter it may be)—even in *terumah*, where we are discussing only the performance and the action that will be effective for the Jew—it is impossible for the status of "agent" to apply if the agent is not similar to the principal ("just as you are..., so too your agent..."), because the essential matter of agency is that the agent is "like the principal."

E. One can bring proof to this matter from the words of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, who writes regarding the matter of agency that "a person’s agent is like himself, and therefore there is no agency for a deaf-mute, an imbecile, or a minor, who are not like the principal."

On the face of it, this requires study, for it is explicit in the *Gemara*

117

that the reason they are not capable of agency is because "they are not people of *deiah* (intellect/consciousness)"?

And even though one can say that this is the interpretation of the *Gemara’s* words, that since they are not people of *deiah*, they are not "like the principal"—nevertheless, one needs an explanation of what the necessity is for this interpretation?

Based on the above, one can say:
It is a known rule that a minor has no *kavanah* (intent), but he has action. Based on this, if you say that the matter of agency is not that the agent stands in the place of the principal, but only that his action is effective for the principal, then for matters that do not require *kavanah* but only action, there would seemingly be no reason why there should not be a law of agency for a minor?

This is what the Alter Rebbe emphasized by saying that the reason *cheresh, shoteh,* and *katan* are not capable of agency is because they are not "like the principal"—and therefore they are fundamentally excluded from the definition of agency. That is to say: even though regarding the act of agency there might have been room to say they are capable of agency, that their action would be effective for the principal, nevertheless, they are not within the category of agency, for it is impossible for the status of "agent" to apply to them, since they are not "like the principal."

F. The law of agency in simple terms, between one person and another, is drawn and descends from the matter of agency between man and the Omnipresent, between a Jew and their Father in Heaven:

Every one of the Jewish people is an agent of the Holy One, blessed be He, an agent of the "Person"—the Supreme One—like Himself, to complete the intent of creation through the *avodah* of fulfilling the *Torah* and *mitzvos*.

And just as there are three ways in the aforementioned law of agency, so too it is in the *avodah* of man, where there are three levels in the manner of the agent’s (man’s) surrender and *bitul* to the principal (the Holy One, blessed be He):

There is a level in *avodas Hashem* where the person working—in his feeling, he is an independent existence in all his matters, but he forces himself to fulfill the will of the Almighty—in his actions, he fulfills the agency of the Holy One, blessed be He;

And there is one who acts within himself with internal *bitul*, until his actions are attributed to the principal, that is to say, his deeds and matters (in the matter of agency) are surrendered and given to Hashem until these actions are not an independent existence and are nothing but the power of the principal;

And higher than this is one who is completely *batel* to the Holy One, blessed be He, until his entire existence (and consequently all his actions) is nothing but the existence of the principal.

However, all these levels are only regarding the fulfillment of the agency, but the fact that every Jew has the ability to fulfill this agency (in whatever way), is because the status of "agent" applies to him; and the application of this status can only be upon the Jewish people (and not the nations of the world, to distinguish) because only among the Jewish people is it possible to say that they are "literally like the principal"—and this is because the souls of the Jewish people are "the portion of Hashem is His people," a portion of *Elokus*

118

from above literally, and therefore only they have a relationship of "some value" regarding the Holy One, blessed be He, the Principal, to the point that they are "literally like the principal."

[And this is also hinted at in the words of our Sages, "Just as you are *bnei bris*..."—that all the Jewish people are "members of the covenant" with the Holy One, blessed be He, which is the very existence of a Jewish person (equal in every Jew) that he is included in "members of the covenant," which is the connection and the covenant between the Jewish people and their Father in Heaven].

And from all this we learn a practical lesson in *avodas Hashem*:
Even one whose *avodah* is still at the lowest level, that he is an independent existence and only his action is for Hashem, nevertheless, since his very existence is "literally like the principal," he has the ability—and therefore he must—infuse even into this *avodah* of his, vitality from the very essence of his soul.

G. Now, even though the words of Rav Ashi (that there is agency for a non-Jew, and as explained above, the reason for this is that he holds the agent does not need to be "like the principal") were rejected in *halacha* to the point that they said "it is a *baruta*," nevertheless, from the very fact that his words were also included in the Torah of truth, it must be that they also have a place.

One can say the explanation for this—in the *avodah* of man:
In man’s *avodah* to his Creator, there is an elevation when the agent is an independent existence (separate from the principal)—for then there is in his *avodah* the revealed quality of "his own *kav*," and our Sages said that by the nature of his creation, "a person desires his own *kav* more than nine *kavs* of his fellow," and as we see tangibly, that when a person’s feeling regarding a certain matter is that it is a matter that concerns him, this arouses in him greater exertion, and he also has more *taanug* in it, etc.

And likewise in spiritual *avodah*, when his *avodah* is in a way that he does not feel his existence and thinks only about the principal [or even at the lower level of agency, that it is considered the agent’s action but his action is for the principal], this can cause weakness in the matter of exertion.

For example: when he has exerted himself with all his strength, and nevertheless the matter is not yet completed, then it is possible that it will occur to him that since he did everything in his power, and the matter is the "principal’s" (the Holy One, blessed be He), he has done his part, and the rest—the principal will complete.

Therefore, there is a quality in the feeling that there is an existence of the agent, to whom the matter of agency is surrendered and it is his matter, and consequently, the matter concerns him especially.

[And as is known the parable for this—the difference between the dedication of an official or an employee who does a matter faithfully, to the dedication of the owner of the house in the same matter: the official, since he does his work faithfully, works with all his strength in his revealed powers, and then goes to his affairs in peace, since he truly did all that was incumbent upon him; in contrast to the owner—who cannot sleep in peace, even if he did all in his power,

119

and consequently, hidden powers are revealed in him, etc.].

H. However, the conclusion of the *Gemara* is that "this statement of Rav Ashi is a *baruta*," because even though there is a quality in the feeling of the agent’s existence, this does not contradict, Heaven forbid, the matter of "a person’s agent is like himself," that the agent must be *batel* to the principal.

Explanation of the matter:
This very fact that there must be an existence of an agent (separate from the principal), this itself is only because this is the will and *taanug* of the principal—for specifically through this, "there is *nachas ruach* before Me that I said and My will was done."

From this it is understood that in the depth of the matter, the existence of the agent (is not an independent existence, but rather) unites with the existence of the principal to be one existence.

[And on the contrary—this itself is the innovation in the matter of agency, that the agent must be a person of intellect in his own right (and not a minor), to whom the power and strength of the principal is surrendered, and nevertheless all his power (in the matter of agency) comes from the power of the principal].

And it turns out that there is no level and manner in agency where the agent remains (truly) an independent existence, but this matter (that the agent must be an independent existence) is an additional detail in the matter of agency (that a person’s agent is like himself), that in order for the principal’s will to be completed as it should be, the act of agency must be performed by the agent’s existence (to the point that he feels himself as the owner of the matter).

And this is also the hint in the language of our Sages (above) "a person desires his own *kav* more than nine *kavs* of his fellow":
"Person" and "his fellow" refer to the Jewish people and the Holy One, blessed be He ("your friend... this is the Holy One, blessed be He"), that through both of them together there are ten *kavs*, which indicates perfection (for ten is the number of perfection).

And to reach this perfection there must be the connection of the "his own *kav*" of the person and the "nine *kavs* of his fellow"—that is to say, on one hand there must be the person’s exertion, in which he feels that this is his ("his own *kav*"), and together with this he must recognize that all his *avodah* (including the fact that there is "his own *kav*") is for the sake of "his fellow," and through the combination of both of them together—there are all ten *kavs*.

(From the *Sichos* of 20 Menachem Av 5745, 21 Menachem Av 5744)


## חוקת

## Sicha 1

### 127

A. "This is the statute of the Torah... that they shall take to you a red heifer...". Our Sages of blessed memory explain the precision of the phrase "take to you" (specifically to Moshe): "All other heifers are null, but yours remains." This applies (not only to the heifer of Moshe, but) to all red heifers of all generations, for in all of them, the Kohanim who prepared the heifer were sanctified using the ashes of Moshe’s heifer. Therefore, all heifers are named specifically after Moshe.

The unique connection between the red heifer and Moshe Rabbeinu is also emphasized regarding the reason for this mitzvah. Even though King Shlomo—who "stood upon all these" (all the statutes of the Torah) —said of this mitzvah, "and it is far from me", nevertheless, the reason for the heifer was revealed to Moshe Rabbeinu, as our Sages say regarding the words "take to you": "To you I reveal the reason for the heifer."

One must understand: how is this mitzvah different from all other mitzvos of the Torah, such that its connection to Moshe Rabbeinu is emphasized both in the manner of its fulfillment—since the fulfillment of this mitzvah (even for future generations) is through the ashes of Moshe’s heifer—and in the manner of its study, "To you I reveal the reason for the heifer" ?

B. This can be explained by prefacing what our Sages said (regarding why the red heifer is female, unlike all other communal offerings which are male): "The Holy One, blessed be He, said: Let the heifer come and atone for the deed of the Golden Calf" (and in Rashi on the Torah, based on the teachings of R. Moshe Hadarshan, are the specific allusions in the mitzvah of the red heifer to the atonement for the sin of the Calf).

The commentators explain the connection between the atonement for the sin of the Calf and the purification from the impurity of death (the literal purpose of the red heifer) based on the words of our Sages : that were it not for the sin of the Calf, there would be no concept of death among Bnei Yisrael, for they would have been "free from the Angel of Death" (just as before the sin of the Tree of Knowledge, for at the time of the Giving of the Torah, their filth ceased, but through the sin of the Calf, their filth returned). It follows that the sin of the Calf was the cause (for the matter of 

### 128

death, and consequently, also) for the impurity of death. This is the link between the two matters of atonement for the sin of the Calf and purification from the impurity of death—they are interdependent; through the atonement for the sin of the Calf, the matter of the impurity of death is automatically nullified (since the cause for the impurity of death—death itself—is nullified).

With this, one can explain (by way of *drush*) the precision of the Rambam’s language : "Nine red heifers were prepared from the time they were commanded in this mitzvah until the Temple was destroyed the second time; the first was prepared by Moshe, etc., and the tenth will be prepared by Melech HaMashiach." Seemingly, this is difficult to understand: why did the Rambam need to write the number of heifers prepared throughout all the generations?

Furthermore, it is a question: the Rambam was not satisfied with bringing the number of heifers mentioned in the Mishnah, but adds, "and the tenth will be prepared by Melech HaMashiach." The Rambam brought into his book *hilchata l’meshicha* (laws pertaining to the future), that then "all the laws will return... as they were before, they will offer sacrifices, etc.". But why did the Rambam single out the tenth heifer from all the matters and sacrifices of the "Prince" in the prophecy of Yechezkel, and write that Melech HaMashiach will prepare the tenth heifer?

Based on the above, it can be said that the Rambam is hinting that the perfection of purification through the red heifer in general will only occur with the tenth heifer, which will be prepared by Melech HaMashiach. For there is no complete atonement for the sin of the Calf (as it is written, "and on the day I visit, I will visit...") until the time to come. Only then, when the atonement for the deed of the Calf is completed, will the perfection of purification from the impurity of death come, because all matters of death will be nullified (as it is written, "He will swallow up death forever"), which is the cause of the impurity of death, as mentioned above.

This is the hint in the fact that "the tenth will be prepared by Melech HaMashiach"—because ten is the number of perfection (as is known), and the perfection of the (purification of the) red heifer will only be in the time to come, with the heifer that Melech HaMashiach will prepare.

C. According to this, one can also explain the connection of the red heifer "to you" to Moshe Rabbeinu:

The foundation of the purification of the red heifer is not merely the nullification of impurity in practice, but the nullification of the cause of impurity from the outset—the matter of death. Therefore, one requires for this the power of Moshe —for one of the unique attributes of Moshe is the matter of eternity, as explained by our Sages that the works of Moshe’s hands are eternal (as we find in this case, the red heifer itself, for the ash of his heifer is from the beginning of the matter, and also—it exists forever). Likewise regarding Moshe himself, even though it is said, "And Moshe died there", they said of him, "Moshe did not die... just as he stands and serves there, so too here, etc." Therefore, the purification of every single heifer depends on the ashes of Moshe’s heifer—because the power of purification of the red heifer is connected with the matter of the eternity of Moshe Rabbeinu.

It should be noted that even though the matter of purification from the impurity of death in its perfection will not be achieved until the time to come, when death will be swallowed up forever (and until then, the heifer purifies from impurity, but it does not have the power to nullify the cause of impurity)—this is only regarding the individual. But regarding the public—through the Torah, which Moshe received from Sinai and transmitted, etc., a communal reality is created, where "the public does not die", for in the public of Bnei Yisrael, the matter of death does not apply.

D. Now, "the Torah is eternal" at all times and in all places. From this it is understood that even though we do not have the ashes of a heifer now, nevertheless, the content of the matter of the purification of the red heifer, which includes the ashes of Moshe’s heifer, exists in the spiritual *avodah* of every Jew.

Explaining the matter:
The transmission of Moshe’s eternity was because he was in a state of absolute *bitul* to the Holy One, blessed be He ("a faithful servant"), a matter of *mesiras nefesh*, that he was prepared to surrender his entire existence for Him, may He be blessed. It can be said that this is why the works of Moshe’s hands are eternal—because *mesiras nefesh* is above changes, for the entire matter of *mesiras nefesh* is the strength to do His will, may He be blessed, in such a way that nothing in the world can change it.

Therefore, the purification from the impurity of death is through the power of the ashes of Moshe’s heifer: impurity of death indicates separation from holiness, for when "you who cleave to Hashem your G-d are all alive today" (death does not apply, and consequently, neither does the impurity of death). To nullify completely the "death" and the separation from holiness, so that there will be ultimate *dveikus* to Hashem your G-d—this is achieved by the person awakening within himself his *mesiras nefesh* to Hashem.

Just as the eternity in the ashes of Moshe’s heifer comes in two aspects—(1) that it itself exists forever, (2) that all heifers needed the ashes of Moshe’s heifer —likewise it is in spiritual *avodah*, where in the awakening of the power of *mesiras nefesh* there are two aspects: (1) the awakening of the essence of the power of *mesiras nefesh*, (2) that the power of *mesiras nefesh* gives strength and fortitude to all matters of *avodas Hashem* so that they will be as they should, as explained in Tanya : "that the fulfillment of the Torah and its *mitzvos* depends on this, that he should always remember the matter of his *mesiras nefesh* to Hashem... that it should be fixed in his heart truly always, day and night, and it shall not depart from his memory, for with this he will be able to stand against his *yetzer* and defeat it always, at every time and every hour."

And in the *avodah* of a person day by day—this is the distinction between the *avodah* of *tefillah* and other matters of *avodas Hashem* throughout the entire day:

At the time of *tefillah*, a person must awaken his love for Hashem that is hidden in his heart, the essence of which love (as explained at length in the Tanya) is the desire and longing to cleave to the One Hashem even to the point of *mesiras nefesh* [and this love is planted in the heart of every Jew, for his soul is "truly a part of G-d above", and the "One Hashem" shines in the soul in a revealed manner; however, it is hidden in his heart, and the *avodah* of the person is to awaken this love and bring it to revelation].

And afterwards, throughout the day, when the person is not occupied with the *avodah* of *mesiras nefesh* (as such), but with "the fulfillment of the Torah and *mitzvos*," nevertheless, even then there must be a remembrance of "the matter of his *mesiras nefesh* to Hashem," through which "he will be able to stand against his *yetzer* and defeat it always, at every time and every hour"—a constant and eternal *dveikus* to Hashem, and then—"you are all alive today."

(From a *sicha* of Shabbos Parshas Chukas, 5723)

## Chukas

### Sicha 2

**1.** In his commentary on the verse, "And Yisrael sent messengers to Sichon, king of the Emori," Rashi quotes, "And Yisrael sent messengers," and explains: "In another place, the mission is attributed to Moshe, as it is stated, 'And I sent messengers from the wilderness of Kedemos,' and similarly, 'And Moshe sent messengers from Kadesh to the land of Edom.' And regarding Yiftach, it says, 'And Yisrael sent messengers to the king of Edom, etc.' These verses require one another; one locks and one opens. For Moshe is Yisrael, and Yisrael is Moshe—to tell you that the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation, for the Nasi is everything."

Now, although on the surface it appears that Rashi’s intention in this commentary is to reconcile contradictions between the verses (whether the mission to Sichon or Edom was carried out by Moshe or by Yisrael), it has already been explained many times at length that it is not Rashi’s way to resolve and reconcile contradictions and misunderstandings in the verses, but rather to explain them after the fact, in the place where the contradiction or similar issue arises. Accordingly, we must say that Rashi’s intention here is to explain a difficulty that arises in the current verse, but for this purpose, he must explain all the verses he brings, resolving them in the process.

It can be said that the difficulty in the text here is the change between this verse regarding the mission to Sichon (where it says, "And Yisrael sent messengers") and the preceding verse (cited by Rashi here) regarding the mission to the king of Edom, "And Moshe sent messengers." To explain this change, Rashi explains, "In another place, etc.," through which this change (also) is resolved.

**2.** However, on the face of it, one must understand the details of Rashi’s language: "These verses require one another; one locks and one opens. For Moshe is Yisrael, and Yisrael is Moshe—to tell you that the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation, for the Nasi is everything."

a) What is the content of the introduction, "These verses require one another; one locks and one opens"? [Even though Rashi’s language originates in the Midrash, nonetheless: 1) It has already been explained many times that Rashi’s purpose is to explain the *peshuto shel mikra* (the plain meaning of the text) and not to copy and announce the language of the Midrash. Where he does copy the words of our Sages in the Midrash or the like, it is because this language is necessary for understanding the plain meaning of the verses. This is true even when he writes explicitly, "Our Rabbis expounded," and certainly when he explains the verse without stating that the source of the explanation is the words of Chazal. 2) Most importantly: In the Midrash, the exposition begins, "All the words of Torah require one another, for what one locks, the other opens," meaning that the content of the Midrashic statement is a general explanation in the manner of "all words of Torah." But in Rashi, whose intention is only to explain the details of these specific verses, what is the practical difference of the introduction that "these verses require one another; one locks and one opens"?]

b) What is the duplication of language (and concept) in "For Moshe is Yisrael, and Yisrael is Moshe (to tell you that the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation)"?

c) Just as in the details of the matter he writes both sides, "Moshe is Yisrael and Yisrael is Moshe," he should seemingly have specified in the conclusion of his commentary not only that "the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation," but also "and the entire generation is like the Nasi," and the like?

d) What is the content of the reason, "For the Nasi is everything," and what is the addition in this compared to what he said previously, "that the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation"? Furthermore, it seemingly contradicts itself: at first, it implies that the Nasi of the generation is only *like* the entire generation (and not literally "the entire generation"), and subsequently, he writes, "For the Nasi is everything," which is not just "like the entire generation" with the *kaf* of comparison, but "everything" literally.

**3.** We must preface this by explaining the precision of Rashi’s language, "In another place, he attributes the mission to Moshe, as it is stated, 'And I sent,' etc." Seemingly, he should have been brief and said, "In another place it is stated, 'And I sent,'" or the like.

It can be said that this language emphasizes the difficulty in the text here according to the *peshuto shel mikra*, which Rashi comes to resolve in his commentary.

The explanation is as follows: The fact that in the current verse (regarding the mission to Sichon) it is written, "And Yisrael sent messengers," and in the previous verse (regarding the mission to the king of Edom) it is said, "And Moshe sent messengers," cannot be interpreted to mean that the sender to the king of Edom was Moshe, while the sender to Sichon was the people of Yisrael. For it is obvious that the sending of messengers to the king of Edom was a mission of the people of Yisrael, as the entire passage there continues: "Thus said your brother Yisrael... and our fathers went down to Egypt... and we cried out to Hashem and He heard our voice." The entire content of the speech concerns the people of Yisrael. And what is said, "And Moshe sent," is understood simply, because all the actions of the people of Yisrael were done through Moshe, and he is the one who actually sent the messengers.

Conversely, even when it is said, "And Yisrael sent," it is obvious that the actual sending was done by Moshe. The reason it says, "And Yisrael sent," is because the mission was on behalf of and from the entire people of Yisrael, and was performed in practice by Moshe—as with all matters concerning the collective of Yisrael that were done by Moshe.

Accordingly, there is no difference between the mission to Sichon and the mission to the king of Edom, only a change in the language of the verses.

This is what Rashi emphasizes: "In another place, he attributes the mission, etc." His intention is that the question regarding the change in the language of the verses here does not concern what actually happened (whether Moshe sent the messengers or Yisrael), but only to whom the text attributes the mission. That is to say, what is the reason for the change in the language of the verse—that regarding Edom, the text attributes the mission to Moshe, and here, the text attributes the mission to Yisrael?

Especially since, on the face of it, the opposite would seem more logical: if in the mission to the king of Edom, where the verses emphasize that the sender is the collective of Yisrael ("Thus said your brother Yisrael"), it says there, "And Moshe sent," then all the more so it should have said so in the mission to Sichon (where there is no such emphasis).

**4.** Now, seemingly, there would be room to explain that attributing the mission to Moshe or to Yisrael is not just a secondary matter (of the language of the verse), but rather it explains who initiated the mission (since the mission comes from his initiative, the mission is dependent on him).

This is the reason for the difference: regarding Edom, the text attributes the mission to Moshe, and here—to Yisrael. For the mission to the king of Edom is different from the mission to the king of Sichon:

Regarding Edom, the mission was by the command of Hashem, and therefore the text attributes the mission to Moshe, who is the servant and messenger of Hashem who hears the instruction of the Holy One, Blessed be He, and fulfills them in practice (to the point that the text does not need to write that Hashem commanded him regarding this, because it is self-understood that this is so, as we find in several places where the command of Hashem to Moshe is not stated in the Torah).

Not so regarding Sichon, for it is explicit in Rashi here that the Holy One, Blessed be He, did not command to open with peace. If so, there is room to say that for this reason it says, "And Yisrael sent," to teach us that the Children of Yisrael were the ones who demanded this mission, and Moshe agreed to them, and therefore it says, "And Yisrael sent."

To negate this interpretation, Rashi begins his commentary: "In another place, he attributes the mission to Moshe, as it is stated, 'And I sent messengers from the wilderness of Kedemos,'" meaning that it is explicit that (even) this mission is Moshe’s, and it should not be attributed and dependent (only) on Yisrael. Afterward, Rashi continues, "And similarly, 'And Moshe sent messengers from Kadesh to the king of Edom,' and regarding Yiftach it says, 'And Yisrael sent messengers to the king of Edom, etc.'"—meaning, even regarding the mission to the king of Edom, the intention of the text is not to attribute the mission only to Moshe (and not to the Children of Yisrael), for it is explicitly stated, "And Yisrael sent."

This is what Rashi continues: "These verses require one another; one locks and one opens. For Moshe is Yisrael, and Yisrael is Moshe." If in the mission to Edom it were written only, "And Moshe sent," it would "lock"—that is, it would lock (against the interpretation) and negate that the mission to Edom was (also) of the Children of Yisrael, but rather that Moshe did this as the messenger of Hashem and the mission should not be attributed to Yisrael (even though the content of the mission is for their sake). Similarly, conversely—if in the mission to Sichon it were written only, "And Yisrael sent," it would "lock" the interpretation of Moshe’s mission in this, for the mission to Sichon should be attributed only to Yisrael and not to Moshe.

Therefore, the second verse comes and "opens" and reveals that "Moshe is Yisrael and Yisrael is Moshe," for what is said, "And Moshe sent," is as if it were said, "And Yisrael sent" (because "Moshe is Yisrael"), and what is said, "And Yisrael sent," is as if it were said, "And Moshe sent" (because "Yisrael is Moshe").

**5.** However, it requires explanation: how is it possible to say that "Moshe is Yisrael and Yisrael is Moshe"? Rashi explains, "To tell you that the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation, for the Nasi is everything."

It can be said that the two matters in Rashi’s commentary, "The Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation" and "For the Nasi is everything," correspond to the two matters mentioned previously: "Moshe is Yisrael and Yisrael is Moshe."

The fact that "Moshe is Yisrael" is according to "the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation." That is to say, the meaning is not that when Moshe is occupied with a matter pertaining to the needs of the collective of Yisrael, he is not a private individual in this, but rather he does it for the collective, and consequently, it is possible to write "Moshe" (who does it in practice) or "Yisrael" (for whom it is done). Rather, the very existence of Moshe, by virtue of being the "Nasi of the generation," is "like the entire generation." This means that the Nasi of the generation is a public figure in his very essence, and consequently, what he does is the action of Yisrael.

Conversely, the fact that "Yisrael is Moshe" is according to "for the Nasi is everything." Here, Rashi did not write "the entire generation," for his intention here is not that the essence of the Nasi of the generation is all the people of the generation, but rather that everything—all the needs of the collective—are (his needs and come through) the Nasi. For everything that the Children of Yisrael have comes from (the power of) the Nasi [as we find regarding the Children of Yisrael in the wilderness, that all their physical needs (food (manna and meat) and drink) they received through Moshe, and likewise, the entire Torah was given to them (and many commandments and the like) through Moshe]. And since all the affairs and needs of the Children of Yisrael come from Moshe (the Nasi), it turns out that "Yisrael is Moshe."

This is what "these verses"—which attribute the mission sometimes to Moshe and sometimes to Yisrael—come "to tell you": that a mission which on the face of it should be attributed and dependent only on Moshe (as the messenger of Hashem) actually belongs to all of Yisrael. And likewise, conversely, that a mission which on the face of it is of Yisrael, the text attributes to Moshe—because "the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation... the Nasi is everything."

**6.** Among the "wondrous matters" in Rashi’s commentary:
In the Midrash (the source of Rashi’s commentary), the language is "that the head of the generation is the entire generation," and Rashi changes it and writes, "the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation."

It can be said that this is in accordance with Rashi’s innovation (mentioned above): "Head of the generation" means (like a head in the literal sense) that he leads the entire generation. Not so "Nasi," which is a term of *nesi'us* (leadership/presidency) and exaltation, meaning that it is a title for a person who is exalted and elevated.

Therefore: In the language of the Midrash, "that the head of the generation is the entire generation," it is possible to interpret the intention that since he is the head who leads the entire generation, and through one single, unified leadership of the head, all the people of the generation become one reality—the reality of the head—therefore, "the head of the generation is the entire generation." [Note the Responsa of the Rashba regarding a king: "The king is like the collective, for the collective and all of Yisrael are dependent on him," where the reason the king is like the collective is because the collective and all of Yisrael are dependent on him.] Accordingly, it turns out that the fact that "the head of the generation is the entire generation" is (only) in his public matters, meaning in matters he does for all of Yisrael and the like, through which all of Yisrael are united as one reality.

But in Rashi’s language, "that the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation, for the Nasi is everything," it can be said that this emphasizes that the "Nasi" of the generation, even as he is exalted and elevated (in himself), is like the entire generation.

The innovation in this is at both extremes:
On one hand, since his affairs are part of his reality as Nasi, it is understood that his actions and deeds—which on the face of it one might have said are "equal," or at least "comparable" to the deeds of the rest of the Children of Yisrael—are also different and elevated regarding the entire people.
On the other hand, since "the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation," then even the most sublime affairs of the Nasi, which on the face of it are in the category of "from his shoulders and upward he was taller than all the people" and are not comparable to the people of the generation, since they are also part of the reality of the Nasi (the Nasi is everything), they act upon and are drawn down into the people of the generation.

**7.** The parsha of Chukas falls in some years between the 3rd of Tammuz (the day on which "freedom was granted to him," the beginning of the Redemption) and the 12th-13th of Tammuz—the day of the redemption of my sainted father-in-law, the Rebbe, the Nasi of the generation, from his imprisonment for the dissemination of Torah and the strengthening of the faith.

One can find a hint to his service and the manner of his leadership in the aforementioned Rashi—for with him it was seen openly, and especially in his aforementioned service, that "the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation... the Nasi is everything."

The "great work" that my sainted father-in-law, the Rebbe, performed in "the dissemination of Torah and the strengthening of the faith" in that country (which, by the way of nature, was what caused the imprisonment)—and likewise in every place of his residence after he left that country—was not limited to a specific area of Judaism, but rather he dealt with all areas: both in everything pertaining to the strengthening of the faith: sending rabbis and *shochtim* to places that needed them, building *mikvaos*, establishing yeshivos and *chadorim* for young children, to the point of disseminating Torah to all of Yisrael, and also the dissemination of the secrets of the secrets of the Torah; and also the saving of lives in the literal sense, in the redemption of captives, and material help and assistance to the poor and all who needed it (as is known regarding the story of those days, and what is known is but a small fraction, for understandable reasons).

And in all these works, which are as far apart as the ends of the earth, his involvement was with absolute self-sacrifice, to the point of actual *mesiras nefesh*.

And thus, they saw tangibly in him the concept that "the Nasi of the generation is like the entire generation"—and consequently—"the Nasi is everything," that all the affairs and needs of the Children of Yisrael, both spiritual and physical, come and are drawn down from him.

(From the sicha of Shabbos Parshas Chukas, 5741)


## י"ב ­י"ג תמוז

## Sicha 1

### 138

A. The text of the *bracha* of *Hagomel* (according to *halacha*) is: "Blessed are You... who bestows kindness upon the guilty, for He has bestowed goodness upon me." The text of the *bracha* of *Haness* (recited by an individual) is: "Blessed are You... who performed a miracle for me in this place." The Rebbe (in his *maamar* delivered on Shabbos, 16 Tammuz 5687, following his liberation from imprisonment in that country) notes the following:

"One must understand why the text of this *bracha* differs from the *bracha* of *Haness*. In the *bracha* of *Haness*, one says 'Blessed... who performed a miracle for me,' and one does not mention any guilt on one's own part. This implies that one finds no guilt in oneself for which one was, Heaven forbid, in danger; rather, one acknowledges that one was not at fault for the situation of danger, Heaven forbid, but that Hashem performed a miracle for him. This is in contrast to this *bracha* (*Hagomel*), where one says 'who bestows kindness upon the guilty,' mentioning guilt on one's own part. One acknowledges that one is the guilty party, but that it is due to the mercies of *Havayah* and the ways of *Havayah* that He, may He be blessed, bestows kindness even upon the guilty."

Now, in the continuation of the *maamar*, the answer to this question ("Why is the text of this *bracha* different from the *bracha* of *Haness*?") is not explicitly stated. Only after explaining the concept of the "four who must give thanks" in the *avodah* of man—which are four types of obstacles and hindrances to the *avodah* of Hashem (which create a state of spiritual danger for a Jew)—and that Hashem stands at the right hand of every person and gives him the strength to overcome these obstacles and hindrances, does he conclude the *maamar* with a brief explanation of the content of saying "who bestows kindness upon the guilty":

"And this is the text of this *bracha*: 'who bestows kindness upon the guilty.' For all of Israel are obligated in the study of Torah and the fulfillment of *mitzvos*... And if, for some reason, one did not fulfill this, surely he is the one to blame... All the obstacles and hindrances during the time of *galus* are merely tests... Therefore, in this *bracha*, one mentions guilt on one's own part."

B. It seems we can explain the answer to the aforementioned question with the following introduction:

The *Beis Yosef* explains the text of the *bracha* "who bestows kindness upon the guilty" as follows: "Meaning, even to those who are guilty—that is, those who are wicked... nevertheless, He bestows goodness upon them. And I, too, am one of them, for even though I am not worthy, He has bestowed all goodness upon me."

## 139

The implication of his explanation is that the term "guilty" here is akin to "unworthy" of receiving the kindness of Hashem. That is, the one reciting the *bracha* acknowledges and praises Hashem for performing this kindness for him, even though he is not worthy of it ("even though I am not worthy").

However, in the aforementioned *maamar* of the Rebbe, it is explicitly stated that the mention of "guilt on one's own part" means "that one finds guilt in oneself for which one was, Heaven forbid, in danger, and one acknowledges that one was the guilty party regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid."

It can be said that this difference from what the *Beis Yosef* writes is relevant to understanding the resolution of the question, "Why is the text of this *bracha* (*Hagomel*) different from the *bracha* of *Haness*?"

If you were to say that the intent of saying "who bestows kindness upon the guilty" is merely to thank Hashem for the goodness performed for him even though he is unworthy—it is puzzling: why was this phrasing not also instituted for the *bracha* of *Haness*?

On the face of it, the opposite is true: the obligation of *Hagomel* is not specifically for a rescue that came about through a miracle. On the contrary, the four who must give thanks are in situations from which they could naturally emerge safely (which is why they are not obligated in the *bracha* of *Haness* unless they were saved by a specific miracle). In contrast, the obligation of the *bracha* of *Haness* is for a miracle that deviates from the natural order of the world.

If so, if in a natural rescue one must thank Hashem for bestowing this goodness upon him "even though I am not worthy," then *a fortiori*—all the more so—when thanking Hashem for the suspension of the laws of nature, one should have added to the thanksgiving the emphasis that the miracle was performed "even though I am not worthy."

C. Seemingly, there is room to distinguish between the *bracha* of *Hagomel* and the *bracha* of *Haness*:

The *bracha* of *Hagomel* is one of the *brachos* of thanksgiving, where one acknowledges and praises Hashem for the goodness that reached him. Therefore, there is room to add "who bestows kindness upon the guilty," since this makes the kindness performed for him more wondrous (that despite not being worthy, this kindness was performed for him).

In contrast, the *bracha* of *Haness* is one of the *brachos* of sight—one makes the *bracha* upon seeing the place where the miracle occurred. That is, the *bracha* is on the very act of the miracle in that place (which demonstrates His greatness, may He be blessed, that it is within His power to suspend the laws of nature). Consequently, there is no place to add the detail of "who bestows kindness upon the guilty," which pertains to the person to whom this miracle occurred, rather than to the act of the miracle itself.

However, [aside from the fact that when a miracle is performed for someone unworthy, it could be said that it is a greater miracle,] this distinction is not definitive, because from the wording of

## 140

the *bracha* of *Haness* (for an individual), "Blessed... who performed a miracle for me in this place," it is proven that the thanksgiving and praise are (not only for the miracle itself, but) for the fact that he is the "owner of the miracle" [which is why only "all the descendants of that person... they and their children until the end of all generations are obligated to bless... for all are partners in that miracle"].

It can be said that in order to resolve this question, the Rebbe adds that the interpretation of "who bestows kindness upon the guilty" is that "one acknowledges that one was the guilty party regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid." This applies only to the *bracha* of *Hagomel*, which is a thanksgiving for a rescue—that is, when previously there was a state of danger and subsequently one was saved from it. Therefore, it is appropriate to say "who bestows kindness upon the guilty," in that "one acknowledges that one was the guilty party regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid" (meaning that it was through his own fault that the situation arose which required rescue from Above).

In contrast, the *bracha* of *Haness* is not necessarily for a miracle that is a rescue from a state of danger. Similar to "one who sees... the crossings of the Yarden" (the place where Israel crossed in the days of Yehoshua), this miracle (the splitting of the Yarden) was not in the category of a rescue from a place of danger, but merely to facilitate their crossing of the Yarden. From this, it is understood that even when one makes a *bracha* on a miracle of rescue, the thanksgiving is not for the nullification of a state of danger, but for the kindness itself—that Hashem performed a miracle for him.

Therefore, the concept of "who bestows kindness upon the guilty" is not appropriate in the *bracha* of *Haness, because there is no necessity that the "owner of the miracle" was in an undesirable state (danger, etc.) "for which he was the guilty party regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid" (and even if he was in such a state, the intent of this *bracha* is not to give thanks for the nullification of that state, as mentioned above).

[And similarly, this is the distinction between the *bracha* of *Hagomel* and the *bracha* of *Haness* in their inner content:

The *bracha* of *Hagomel*—whose purpose is thanksgiving for a rescue from an undesirable state (danger, etc.)—pertains to the *avodah* of the *neshamah* in the body, which is a great descent (as explained at length in the continuation of the

## 141

*maamar* of the Rebbe). This descent provides the opportunity for a person to place himself in a state of danger, Heaven forbid (in the four ways of the "four who must give thanks"). However, even for this person who brought himself into this state, Hashem gives him the strength to be saved from the danger, by "bestowing kindness upon the guilty, for He has bestowed goodness upon me"—("there is no good but Torah, which is the true good").

Therefore, one says in this *bracha* "who bestows kindness upon the guilty," because it is connected with a state of being "guilty"—a spiritual danger—and the person thanks Hashem for saving him even though "he was the guilty party regarding the matter of the danger," as mentioned above.

In contrast, the *bracha* of *Haness* pertains to the thanksgiving and praise to Hashem for the Divine revelation in the act of miracles (which are above—from the word *arim nissi* (I will raise my banner)—the nature of the world). Therefore, it is not appropriate to say "who bestows kindness upon the guilty," for there is no state of "guilty" here; rather, on the contrary, it is a revelation of Hashem.]

D. However, on the face of it, the very interpretation mentioned above requires study:

The *bracha* of *Hagomel* is in a text that is the same for all of Israel, including perfectly righteous individuals (*tzaddikim gemurim*). Seemingly, it is extremely puzzling to say that even a *tzaddik gamur* "was the guilty party regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid"?

It is understandable according to the *Beis Yosef*'s interpretation that the intent of "guilty" is regarding the reception of goodness (that the person acknowledges he is not worthy or deserving of this goodness). One could say that this applies even to *tzaddikim*, who, due to their great humility, think they are not worthy of Hashem's kindness. This is similar to what we find regarding Yaakov, who "apologized for his fear of Esav, and was not satisfied with the promise... because Yaakov was very, very small in his own eyes... and he was not worthy or deserving at all to be saved... and it seemed to him that he had sinned."

But how can it be said that even a *tzaddik gamur* must think (and truly so) that he sinned to such an extent that he would be "the guilty party regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid"?

Furthermore, it is even less understandable: this interpretation was stated

## 142

by the Rebbe in connection with his liberation from imprisonment, where the natural cause of his imprisonment ("the matter of the danger") was "the great work" he performed "in spreading Torah and strengthening the religion", in which he worked to the point of actual *mesiras nefesh*. This is a state of merit in the ultimate perfection and elevation—so how is it possible to say that he "was the guilty party regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid"?!

E. This can be understood based on what is explained in the *iggeres* of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe (which he wrote "after his arrival from Petersburg") regarding the interpretation of the verse "I am small from all the kindnesses," as follows:

"For every single kindness that Hashem performs for a person, one must be very humble of spirit, because kindness... is a state of actual closeness to Hashem... And the closer one is to Hashem to a greater degree... the more humble of spirit one must be... The more one is before Him, the more one is as nothing, naught, and void. And this is also the trait of Yaakov... that Yaakov was very, very small in his own eyes due to the abundance of kindnesses... and he was not worthy or deserving at all to be saved... and as our Sages say, 'lest sin cause,' that it seemed to him that he had sinned."

From the continuation of the language, it is understood that even the fact that it "seemed to him that he had sinned" is a result of the "abundance of kindnesses." That is, the "closeness to Hashem" (which comes through the abundance of kindnesses) worked within Yaakov not only a feeling of smallness and humility (that he should be very, very small in his own eyes), but that it should "seem to him that he had sinned."

One can explain this matter:

The fact that Yaakov feared "lest sin cause, that it seemed to him that he had sinned" was not, Heaven forbid, a false humility, where Yaakov erred about himself in thinking that he had transgressed His will, may He be blessed. Rather, this fear was generated by the fact that he became "closer to Hashem to a greater degree."

Meaning: before he merited this "closeness to Hashem," his *avodah* was in the ultimate perfection without any deficiency at all; and only after he was elevated to be "closer to Hashem to a greater degree," then (he thought) his previous *avodah* would be considered as a sin (even though before that there was no deficiency in his *avodah*).

## 143

An example of this is what our Sages say that one who is "shown a sign before the King" is considered to be rebelling against the King, because when standing before the King, one must be in total *bitul*, without any movement whatsoever. This is only when standing before the King; however, when not in the presence of the King, not only is it sufficient that his level of *bitul* to the King be at a lower degree (that he accepts upon himself to fulfill the King's laws, etc.), but furthermore, it is the King's will that the person not be in such total *bitul*, for in such a state of *bitul* he would not be capable of fulfilling the King's commands in settling the country, etc.

Thus, while being before the King, his regular *avodah* while occupied with the country and its settlement (even though it is in the ultimate perfection) is considered "as a sin," because before the King he has nothing but his *bitul* to the King.

[And as explained in the *sefarim*, this is also the reason for the difference between the weekdays, in which we were commanded "Six days you shall work and do all your labor" (and our Sages say "this is a positive commandment"), while on the day of Shabbos, the performance of labor was forbidden:

On the day of Shabbos, His holiness, may He be blessed, shines in the world in a revealed manner, similar to standing before the King, and therefore we are forbidden from labor. In contrast, on the weekdays, His holiness, may He be blessed, is hidden in the world; this is like the citizens who are outside the King's palace—in which case it is the King's will that they occupy themselves, do labor in the settlement of the world, etc.]

This is the interpretation of the fact that it "seemed to Yaakov that he had sinned," because "due to the abundance of kindnesses" that raised Yaakov to be "closer to Hashem to a greater degree," his level, his smallness in his own eyes in his previous *avodah* of Hashem, is considered as a sin.

And similarly, one can explain the text of the *bracha* "who bestows kindness upon the guilty" for *tzaddikim gemurim*—that by the very fact that kindness and goodness were bestowed upon them, this causes them to feel themselves as "guilty," for relative to the greatness of their closeness to Hashem (through this kindness), their previous state is in the category of "guilty" (even though before this "closeness to Hashem," their *avodah* was in the ultimate perfection), as mentioned above.

F. However, the matter is still not fully resolved, because the aforementioned explanation is only sufficient to explain how it is possible that a *tzaddik gamur* would consider himself (truly)

## 144

as "guilty," but in the *maamar* it explains (as mentioned above) "guilty"—that "one acknowledges that one was the guilty party regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid." And the puzzle remains: it is true that relative to his current state, his previous *avodah* is not in perfection, but how is it possible to say "that he was the guilty party (prior to this) regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid"?

[And it is a great strain to say that this interpretation of "who bestows kindness upon the guilty" is only for those who are not *tzaddikim*, for whom it is appropriate to say that they are to blame "regarding the matter of the danger"].

The explanation for this is as follows:

The fact that Hashem places the *tzaddik* in a state of danger, etc., is only in order to elevate him to a higher level. For it is specifically through *avodah* in a state of "straits" (*meitzar*) that one reaches the true "expanse" (*merchav*) ("From the straits I called to Hashem; He answered me with an expanse").

[Similar to what is explained in the *maamar* regarding the descent of the *neshamah* below (that the thanksgiving in the *bracha* of *Hagomel* in the *avodah* of man is for the exit from the spiritual danger that is in this descent)—that the intent of this descent is for the sake of an ascent, that through the *avodah* of the *neshamah* in clarifying and refining the physical body (and drawing holiness into physical things), the *neshamah* is elevated to a higher level than it was before its descent into the body.

And this matter is also true for *tzaddikim gemurim*, that the fact that a servant of Hashem works in this world is a descent, for "even... a *tzaddik gamur* who serves Hashem with fear and great love with delights will not reach the levels of his *dveikus* in Hashem... before his descent into the material world, not in part nor in whole, and there is no value or comparison between them at all"—but this descent is for the sake of an ascent, that through their *avodah* while they are *neshamos* in bodies specifically, they are elevated from level to level, etc.]

Therefore, even a *tzaddik gamur* recites "who bestows kindness upon the guilty"—even though it is not appropriate to say that he is "to blame" "regarding the matter of the danger, Heaven forbid"—because his previous state, which is in the category of "guilty" relative to the "closeness to Hashem" that he merited through the bestowing of the goodness, is what caused him to be placed in these straits, in order that he should reach this ascent (which comes through these straits).

And all these things are seen tangibly in the liberation of the Rebbe—that specifically after he underwent the sufferings of imprisonment, a new period began in his *avodah* in spreading Torah and strengthening the religion in general, that this *avodah* of his expanded—it went out to an expanse—even to spreading to all the ends of the earth.

And it was revealed to all that the intent of the imprisonment was for the sake of this ascent, to provide the possibility for the expansion of the *avodah*, to spread Torah and Judaism—including the spreading of the wellsprings of the inner dimension of Torah—throughout all the expanses (to all the ends) of the earth.

(From the *sichos* of 12 Tammuz 5721, *Shabbos Parshas Balak* 5737)

## 12-13 Tammuz

### Sicha 2

A. Regarding the day of 12 Tammuz—the day on which the Rebbe was redeemed from his imprisonment, for which he was imprisoned due to his "great work" of "disseminating Torah and strengthening the faith"—the Rebbe wrote in his letter for the first 12 Tammuz celebration (in the year 5688) as follows: "It is fitting to establish it as a day of *farbrengen* and *hisorerus* for the strengthening of Torah and Yiddishkeit in every place, according to its specific circumstances."

It has been explained many times that all the words of our Rebbes are precise, even regarding their "conversations" and even regarding their casual talk—which require study. How much more so is this true regarding their Torah teachings, and with even greater emphasis regarding their instructions (*Torah* comes from the word *hora'ah*—instruction).

In our case, we must be precise regarding the Rebbe’s phrasing, "strengthening of Torah and Yiddishkeit." Seemingly, "Yiddishkeit" is a general term that includes everything pertaining to the Jewish faith—both the study of Torah and the observance of *mitzvos* (whether *d'oraisa*, *d'rabanan*, or even Jewish customs, etc.). If so, it would have been sufficient to say "strengthening of Yiddishkeit." What, then, is added by the words "strengthening of Torah"?

Simply put, the intention here is to emphasize the matter and the activities of "strengthening Torah," as he writes several times in that same letter regarding his work of "disseminating Torah" (the simple meaning being the founding of *chadarim*, *yeshivos*, and public Torah *shiurim* for business people, etc.). However, he should have (seemingly) placed the general term, "Yiddishkeit," first, and then added the emphasis on the specific, "Torah."

One must also note and explain his phrasing "strengthening of Torah," rather than his phrasing used several times in that same letter, "disseminating Torah."

It seems that one of the Rebbe’s intentions here is not to arouse us regarding two separate matters—strengthening the work of disseminating Torah and strengthening the work of upholding Yiddishkeit (observance of *mitzvos*, etc.)—but rather, the intention is one matter: "strengthening Yiddishkeit." However, it is impossible to strengthen Yiddishkeit in the desired manner except by prioritizing and beginning with the strengthening of Torah.

B. The explanation of this matter:

The concept of "Yiddishkeit" in its simple sense means the totality of Jewish conduct as Jews. Therefore, it is necessary to emphasize and establish that "Yiddishkeit" is not merely the conduct of human beings—the way Jewish people have behaved throughout the generations, a human creation, *chas v'shalom*—but rather, its entire essence is the fulfillment of the instructions of the Torah, which was given at Sinai from the mouth of the Almighty.

This is the content of the emphasis "strengthening of Torah and Yiddishkeit": before strengthening Yiddishkeit, one must strengthen the *emunah* and the recognition that only the Torah determines what "Yiddishkeit" is. The matter is not left to human beings to decide through their own intellect which specific matters in Yiddishkeit are mandatory and which are not. Rather, every single detail in the Torah—even the innovation of a veteran student, and even Jewish customs that were introduced in later generations (and accepted by the majority of the Jewish diaspora)—is a part of our holy Torah, which is the Torah of Hashem, Torah from Heaven, given to Moshe at Sinai (for even a Jewish custom is Torah).

From this it is understood that one who is lenient regarding the stringencies and Jewish customs that were established and practiced in later generations, thinking that they are "human creations" and that therefore he is not obligated to accept them upon himself, is not merely opposing some specific details of "Yiddishkeit" or lacking a few deeds. Rather, this is in direct opposition to the fundamentals of Yiddishkeit, which is not a human creation at all. Every detail within it is a law that Hashem revealed through His servants—the Sages of Israel in every generation.

[Furthermore, this "approach"—to distinguish between matters of the Oral Torah based on the Written Torah (i.e., matters the Sages derived through the thirteen hermeneutical principles) or those that are *Halacha l'Moshe miSinai*, versus the "decrees, enactments, and customs" that were introduced by the Sages of Israel in every generation—is a gateway to heresy, *r'l*, regarding the fundamental principle that the entire Oral Torah is from Heaven.]

C. It can be said that the need for this emphasis, "strengthening of Torah" before strengthening Yiddishkeit, is due to the continuation of the instruction: "(strengthening of Torah and Yiddishkeit) in every place, according to its specific circumstances."

If the strengthening of Torah and Yiddishkeit were only in a "city full of Sages" or the like, there would be no need for the aforementioned emphasis, as it is obvious to everyone that the entire essence of Yiddishkeit is its being the Torah of Hashem. But since we are speaking of strengthening Yiddishkeit "in every place, according to its specific circumstances," it is necessary to negate the error that Yiddishkeit is subject to change, *chas v'shalom*, according to the conditions of time and place. Furthermore, there are "places" where the people are very far from Yiddishkeit, and it is impossible to influence them to accept the yoke of observing the entire Torah in all its details. The "circumstances" of such a place might require settling (for the time being) for efforts to ensure they observe (only) a few *mitzvos* (and the like).

Therefore, when engaging in the strengthening of Yiddishkeit "in every place, according to its specific circumstances," one must always remember the premise: that before strengthening Yiddishkeit comes "strengthening of Torah," for "Yiddishkeit" is established and built solely upon the foundation of Torah, which was not given to change due to shifts in time and place.

As has been explained several times regarding the precise phrasing of the *Mishnah*: "Be of the disciples of Aharon... love the creatures and bring them close to Torah"—one must bring the creatures close to Torah, and not the opposite: "bringing" and lowering the Torah, *chas v'shalom*, to the level of the creatures through concessions, *chas v'shalom*, compromises, and diminutions of the laws of the Torah. This applies even if one thinks that this compromise might specifically help (in his opinion) to bring the other person closer to Torah and *mitzvos*, at least to the observance of a few *mitzvos*. For, as mentioned, our holy Torah is a Torah of truth and was not given to compromises. Every single Jew, regardless of his situation or location, has the obligation to accept the entire Torah to keep and observe it all.

This does not contradict the fact that the effort to strengthen Yiddishkeit is, as stated, "in every place, according to its specific circumstances," to the point that sometimes the effort must be done slowly—and even someone who is in the category of a *tinok shenishba* (a child captured among non-Jews) should begin with the observance of one *mitzvah* or the like (as mentioned above). This does not mean that we make compromises in Torah and Yiddishkeit. Rather, it is like what we find regarding Torah study: with a child, we begin by teaching the *Aleph-Beis*, and then he goes from strength to strength: learning to combine letters, then words, until he merits to learn the entire Torah. It is obvious that teaching the *Aleph-Beis* to a child is not a "compromise" at all, and there is no concession or diminution in it whatsoever; rather, it is the order of study, from the beginning to the continuation, etc., precisely.
