It is the same regarding the observance of *mitzvos*, "strengthening Yiddishkeit": when dealing with a "child" (spiritually) who has no knowledge of the matters of Yiddishkeit, one must first teach him the *Aleph-Beis* of "Yiddishkeit." But immediately at this beginning, the "child" knows that the *Aleph-Beis* (with which he began) is not the entire Torah, but rather that in his current state (as a "child," etc.), he is beginning with the observance of one *mitzvah*, and in time he will go from strength to strength, and "one *mitzvah* leads to another *mitzvah*".

D. This is the practical instruction that must serve as the foundation for the *farbrengens* of the celebration of the *Chag HaGeulah* of 12 Tammuz: that the work of strengthening Yiddishkeit must be based on these two principles, at both extremes:

The first principle is "strengthening of Torah and Yiddishkeit," which is the pure *emunah* that every matter of Yiddishkeit is determined solely by the Torah that is from Heaven (and is not a human creation at all, *chas v'shalom*). Consequently, it is impossible for there to be any compromise, concession, or diminution in Torah and its *mitzvos*, for just as "I, Hashem, have not changed", so too there can be no change in "Yiddishkeit," which is the word of Hashem.

Together with this, one must strive to strengthen Yiddishkeit "in every place, according to its specific circumstances." Even if one is speaking of a "place" that is currently far from Yiddishkeit, and certainly from observing the entire Torah, and the effort there is "according to its specific circumstances" (as discussed at length above)—nevertheless, truly, and precisely through truth, one takes hold of and conquers (*nemt men on*) the person.

A person is forbidden to stand from afar [like those who say that one should only engage with G-d-fearing and complete Jews to strengthen their hands in the observance of Torah and *mitzvos*, but not with those who are lacking in this, and certainly not with those who are far from the Torah of Hashem and His service].

Rather, one is obligated to strive with all his might to bring them close to Torah and *mitzvos*, as per the instruction of the Baal Shem Tov regarding the greatness of the *mitzvah* of *ahavas Yisrael* for every single Jew, even for one "whom he has never seen." As explained elsewhere, the intention here is also regarding spiritual matters: that even a Jew who is so far from Yiddishkeit that he seemingly has no connection to him in Yiddishkeit, one must love him to the point of striving to bring him close to Torah and Yiddishkeit—the most precious things in the entire world.

As explained in *Tanya*, regarding "one who is not your fellow (in Torah and *mitzvos*) and is not close to you," it is forbidden to hate him, *chas v'shalom* (even if one saw him sin). Rather, "regarding this, Hillel the Elder said: 'Be of the disciples of Aharon, loving peace... loving the creatures and bringing them close to Torah.' Meaning... one must draw them with cords of love, and perhaps, just perhaps, one will be able to bring them close to the Torah and service of Hashem."

Through the effort of *ahavas Yisrael* for every single Jew, one merits the blessing of Hashem, "Bless us, our Father, (when) we are all as one"—specifically so.

This leads to the ultimate and inner blessing—the true and complete *Geulah* through *Moshiach Tzidkeinu*, speedily in our days, literally.

(From the *sicha* of 13 Tammuz 5729)


## י"ז תמוז

## Sicha 1

A. The Rebbe Rayatz related that in the year 5640, on the Shabbos of the 17th of Tammuz, his grandfather, the Rebbe Maharash, was in a state of great joy. He mentioned several times that this Shabbos is called "postponed" (*nidcheh*), because the fast of the 17th of Tammuz is postponed due to Shabbos, and he concluded, "If only it would be postponed in truth."

On the surface, the words of the Rebbe Maharash are in the nature of a blessing and a prayer (as the Rebbe Rayatz concluded: "A blessing of a Tzaddik is surely fulfilled; if only it would be so, and it should be postponed in truth and transformed into joy and gladness"). However, (in addition to the fact that even the mundane conversation of a Torah scholar requires study, and all the more so their prayers and blessings), according to the well-known precision in the words of our Rebbes, it must be said that the content of a "postponed fast" is hidden and alluded to in the exactness of his language, both according to Halacha and according to the inner dimension of Torah.

B. At first glance, the connection between the postponement of a fast (the 17th of Tammuz) by the day of Shabbos and its total postponement in the time to come is simple: it is not merely a postponement from its set time to another time, but the postponement also effects a reduction in the severity of the fast. For a fast that is postponed (to Sunday) is lighter than when it occurs at its proper time [as is explicit in Halacha regarding *bris milah*, that when the fast is at its proper time, the *baal habris* must complete the fast, but when it falls on Shabbos and is postponed to Sunday, the *baal habris* does not complete his fast because it is his *yom tov*]. Since the severity of the fast is reduced, it can be said that this is a beginning of its total nullification.

The basis for this (that the postponement of the fast reduces its severity, to the point that it gives room for its total postponement) is in the words of Rabbi regarding "Tisha B'Av that falls on Shabbos... and we postponed it until after Shabbos, and Rabbi said: 'Since it is postponed, it is postponed,'" meaning that he holds that since it is impossible to fast at its proper time, the obligation of the fast is nullified entirely in such a year.

Based on this, we can explain the words of the Rebbe Maharash, preceded by an explanation and clarification of the aforementioned words of Rabbi, "Since it is postponed, it is postponed."

C. In the Gemara there, the Sages disagree with the words of Rabbi, and seemingly it can be said that the content of the dispute is whether the postponement from Shabbos to Sunday makes Sunday the "proper time" of the fast (and this is the opinion of the Sages).

[Based on this, it can be said that Rabbi and the Sages follow their respective principles.] It is explicit (as mentioned above) that specifically "Tisha B'Av that falls on Shabbos and is postponed to Sunday, the *baal habris* prays Mincha while the day is still long, and bathes, and does not complete his fast, because it is his *yom tov*," whereas when the *bris milah* is on Tisha B'Av itself, he is obligated to complete it—it is understood from this that, according to Halacha, we hold that Tisha B'Av that is postponed to Sunday is not as severe as Tisha B'Av that is not postponed, meaning that it is in the category of a postponement and not from the essence of the enactment (for if not, why would they be lenient regarding the *baal habris*?).

Therefore, it must be that even according to the Sages, the fast on Sunday, when Tisha B'Av falls on Shabbos, is in the category of a postponement, and therefore it is not as severe as a fast on the day of Tisha B'Av itself—and the dispute between Rabbi and the Sages is in the definition of this "postponement" itself, which can be interpreted in several ways, as follows.

D. It is explained in the *Acharonim* that in a fast there are two aspects (and modes): the application of the name "fast" upon the day, and the application of the laws and obligations of the fast upon the person.

According to this, it can be said that the dispute between Rabbi and the Sages is not regarding the severity of the fast of Sunday (which is postponed from Shabbos), but rather regarding the definition of the day of Shabbos upon which the fast is postponed—whether the postponement on Shabbos is only regarding the fulfillment of the laws of the fast, but the aspect of a "fast day" remains within it—and this is the opinion of the Sages; or whether the aspect of the "fast day" is also postponed on Shabbos—and this is the opinion of Rabbi, for Rabbi holds that the application of the "fast day" is entirely postponed, and in such a case there is no application of a "fast day" (rather, since the fast is only postponed, some laws of Tisha B'Av remain, such as private matters, and the like).

But this matter requires explanation, for how is it possible to say that there is an aspect of a fast on the day of Shabbos? After all, Shabbos is forbidden to be fasted upon, and on the contrary, there is an obligation to delight in it with eating and drinking, etc., and according to several opinions, the prohibition of fasting on it is from the Torah (and the Alter Rebbe wrote that the prohibition of fasting is derived from the verse, "Eat it today, for today is Shabbos to Hashem," implying that on Shabbos it is forbidden to fast).

It can be said in explanation of the matter (in the inner dimension of things):

E. The essence of the fast is (in the language of the verse) "a day of favor to Hashem." And even though this was said (primarily) regarding a fast that is for *teshuvah* and atonement, unlike the four fasts, and especially Tisha B'Av, which are in the category of mourning over the tragedies that occurred at these times—yet the Rambam wrote that even these fasts are "to awaken the hearts to open the paths of *teshuvah*, and this shall be a remembrance of our deeds and the deeds of our ancestors that were like our deeds now, which caused them and us those tragedies, so that by remembering these things we will return to the good, as it is said, 'And they shall confess their iniquity and the iniquity of their ancestors, etc.'"—from which it is proven that these fasts have both aspects.

This means that its being a "day of favor" is revealed through the fasting and the conduct of the fast on this day, as it is stated in the Gemara, "May it be that my fat and blood that were diminished be considered as if I had offered them before You on the altar, and You will favor me."

[And with this, one can explain what is said in Zechariah, and brought in the Rambam as Halacha, that in the future (not only will all the fasts be nullified, but furthermore) they will be transformed into *yomim tovim* and days of joy and gladness.

For according to the simple meaning, this is difficult: the nullification of the fasts in the time to come is understood, for since the fasts are in memory of the tragedies that occurred to our ancestors, therefore, when the memory of the tragedies is nullified (through the Redemption), the fasts are nullified automatically—but why will these days be transformed into joy and gladness? For seemingly, what does joy have to do with this?

But according to the above, it is understood, because the essence of the fast is not the affliction within it, but rather that it is a "day of favor to Hashem," except that in the time of exile, when our sins (which caused us to be "exiled from our land") have not yet been nullified, the way to feel and utilize the virtue of the day is through the affliction of the body, which awakens the person to *teshuvah*, etc.; unlike in the time to come, when "I will remove the spirit of impurity from the land," there is no place for the affliction (of the body), but rather for an increase in the service of Hashem, and this service is from joy and goodness of heart, and therefore these days of favor will be days of joy, and they will be transformed into *yomim tovim*.]

F. ...physical from the foods of Shabbos—on this Shabbos (on which a fast falls and is postponed), the power is given that he should have the vitality and the delight of eating and drinking in the category of "to sustain them in famine."

But the Sages did not agree with him, because as long as these fasts have not yet been nullified, there is a need for the affliction as it is simply (and all the more so since the Torah speaks for the majority, and it is impossible to demand from the majority that everyone reach this level, that he should not feel the physical delight from the eating and drinking of Shabbos, which is a *mitzvah* meal)—therefore they hold that even though there is on this day of Shabbos the elevation of a "day of favor to Hashem" (which is from the side of the fast), as explained at length above, nevertheless, there is a need for the act of fasting as it is simply, on Sunday, until the prophecy is fulfilled that these days will be transformed into joy and gladness and *yomim tovim* [and nevertheless, the fast of Sunday is lighter, because it does not have the essence of a fast day].

G. And according to all the above, we can explain the words of the Rebbe Maharash mentioned above (Section A)—that he mentioned several times that this Shabbos is called "postponed" because the fast of the 17th of Tammuz is postponed on Shabbos, and he concluded, "If only it would be postponed in truth."

By way of introduction, in the words of the Rebbe Rayatz there, after he related the words of the Rebbe Maharash mentioned above, he concluded (as above), "A blessing of a Tzaddik is fulfilled; if only it would really be so, and it should be postponed in truth and transformed into joy and gladness."

And seemingly, what is the addition in the words of the Rebbe Rayatz, "If only it would really be so, and it should be postponed in truth," to the words of the Rebbe Maharash—are they not the same words?

Also—the Rebbe Rayatz added to the words of the Rebbe Maharash, "and transformed into joy and gladness," and also changed his language, for the Rebbe Maharash said, "If only it would be postponed in truth," while the Rebbe Rayatz said, "If only it would really be so, and it should be postponed in truth, etc."

Also, the words of the Rebbe Rayatz, "A blessing of a Tzaddik is fulfilled," require understanding—for seemingly, this is an explicit prophecy and a clear Halachic ruling that the fasts are destined to be nullified and to be days of joy and gladness, in the language of the Rambam, "All these fasts are destined to be nullified in the days of Moshiach, and not only that, but they are destined to be *yomim tovim* and days of joy and gladness, as it is said, 'Thus said Hashem, etc.'" (and it is obvious that it is not meant in the *sicha* that it will be nullified before the time of the days of Moshiach)—and what is added by "A blessing of a Tzaddik is fulfilled"?

And according to what we have explained regarding the definition of a fast that falls on Shabbos—it can be said that the words of the Rebbe Maharash and the words of the Rebbe Rayatz speak of two different details in the matter, and the words of the Rebbe Rayatz are a continuation (and conclusion) of the words of the Rebbe Maharash.

The explanation of the matter:
The Rebbe Maharash mentioned several times that this Shabbos is called "postponed." It can be said that his intention was to speak of the virtue of this Shabbos, that there is a fulfillment of the definition of the fast in the service of man, and this is why this Shabbos is called "postponed," for because it is entirely a "day of favor to Hashem," the "fast of the 17th of Tammuz is postponed on Shabbos," and this means that the Jewish people have the power to act and reveal this elevation through the *mitzvos* and actions of Shabbos—eating and drinking and the delight within it; and regarding this he concluded, "If only it would be postponed in truth," that it should be fulfilled in the service of everyone that it will be postponed in truth, that the fulfillment of the "day of favor to Hashem" of the day of Shabbos should be in truth, that it should be felt by everyone in truth in the delight of the eating and drinking of Shabbos, which is entirely in the category of "to sustain them in famine."

And regarding this, the Rebbe Rayatz continues, "A blessing of a Tzaddik is fulfilled," meaning that the blessing for the fulfillment of the definition of the fast in the service of man on the day of Shabbos will be fulfilled in practice, "If only it would really be so" in his service,

While the continuation, "and it should be postponed in truth"—in this he speaks of the postponement of the fast itself simply (in the time to come), that it will truly be postponed, and he adds, "and transformed into joy and gladness"—

And these are akin to and along the lines of the two stages (and periods) that will be in the days of Moshiach: first, that "all these fasts are destined to be nullified," which is in the language of the Rebbe, "it should be postponed in truth," and the second stage, "and not only that, but they are destined to be *yomim tovim* and days of joy and gladness"—and this is the conclusion of the language of the Rebbe Rayatz, "and transformed into joy and gladness."

May it be the will that in this year it will be fulfilled (in the language of the Rebbe) that it will be so in truth, "it should be postponed in truth and transformed into joy and gladness," and automatically—on the 17th of Tammuz and all the more so on Tisha B'Av, the prophecy will already be fulfilled, "Thus said Hashem Tzevaos: The fast of the fourth and the fast of the fifth, etc., shall be... for joy and gladness and for *yomim tovim*, and love truth and peace."


## פינחס

## Pinchas

### Sicha 2

**1.** Regarding the verse, "And the sons of Korach did not die," Rashi explains: "They were involved in the counsel from the beginning, but at the time of the dispute, they contemplated repentance in their hearts. Therefore, a high place in Gehenom was fortified for them, and they sat there."

Commentators note that Rashi’s intent is to resolve a contradiction in the literal text. How can it be said that "the sons of Korach did not die," when it is explicitly stated earlier in Parshas Korach, "The earth opened its mouth... and all the people who belonged to Korach" (which, on a simple level, includes the sons of Korach)? Thus, Rashi explains that while they were indeed swallowed by the earth, they did not die, because "a high place in Gehenom was fortified for them, and they sat there." The reason they "did not die" is that "at the time of the dispute, they contemplated repentance in their hearts."

However, the verbosity of Rashi’s language still requires explanation—"They were involved in the counsel from the beginning, but at the time of the dispute, they contemplated repentance in their hearts." Seemingly, it would have sufficed to say briefly: "Because they repented, therefore a place was fortified for them," similar to Rashi’s phrasing earlier in Parshas Korach (where he explains why Korach initiated the dispute with Moshe, stating that he "saw a great chain emerging from him—Shmuel, who was equal to Moshe and Aharon, and twenty-four watches standing for his descendants," and Rashi concludes: "But he did not see clearly, because his sons repented").

On a simple level, one could say that Rashi intends to explain why the sons of Korach were punished at all (to be among those swallowed by the earth), given that they repented. Therefore, he writes that "they were involved in the counsel from the beginning," and furthermore, that their repentance was not complete, as they only "contemplated repentance in their hearts." Consequently, this punishment (being swallowed by the earth) was due to them, but because they at least contemplated repentance, they "did not die," but rather "a high place in Gehenom was fortified for them, and they sat there."

Yet, this still does not explain the precision of Rashi’s language:

a) From the words "they were involved in the counsel from the beginning" (rather than "they were in the dispute" or similar), it is implied that they did not merely join the dispute once it broke out, but were among the advisors whose counsel initiated Korach’s dispute. 
This is difficult to understand:
(1) What is the necessity, according to the literal text, to state that the sons of Korach were among those disputing Moshe from the start? (Note that Targum Yonasan and Targum Yerushalmi explain that they were not part of Korach’s counsel). It is understood that they were swallowed because they participated in the dispute, but how does Rashi know that "they were involved in the counsel from the beginning"?
(2) Even if we find a necessity for this in the literal text, what is the practical difference here in emphasizing the magnitude of their sin—that (not only did they participate in the dispute of Korach and his assembly, but) they were "involved in the counsel from the beginning" [when even without this, they would deserve punishment since they did not perform complete repentance]?

b) "At the time of the dispute, they contemplated repentance in their hearts"—why the precision that they contemplated repentance during the dispute itself, rather than afterwards, as a result of Moshe’s words? Seemingly, it is more logical that their repentance occurred after Moshe said, "By this you shall know that Hashem has sent me... if these die the death of all men..." and therefore their repentance at that time was insufficient to completely nullify their punishment, which is why they were swallowed.

c) "Contemplated repentance in their hearts"—seemingly, this phrasing does not emphasize that their repentance was incomplete, only that it was a thought in the heart and not a confession of the mouth, etc.

**2.** It can be said that Rashi’s intent is to explain the reason they were punished and subsequently saved in such a strange (and miraculous) manner, the likes of which we do not find elsewhere in the entire Torah: they were swallowed together with (Korach and) Dasan and Aviram and their families, yet in that very place, "a high place (in Gehenom) was fortified for them, and they sat there" (especially since there is room to say that by "they sat there," Rashi implies sitting in tranquility, similar to Rashi’s interpretation of "And Yaakov sat—Yaakov sought to sit in tranquility"). What is the nature of such a punishment, which is a paradox—they are in the earth (in Gehenom), and in that very place, it is a "high" place, and in a manner of being "fortified" (and sitting in tranquility)?

To resolve this, Rashi emphasizes, "And at the time of the dispute, they contemplated repentance in their hearts; therefore, a place was fortified for them..." That is to say: the deficiency in the sons of Korach’s repentance was that it was only a thought in the heart and not an open, verbal declaration, in a way that was not known to anyone. On the contrary, their repentance was at the time of the dispute, meaning that to the observer, they still appeared to be continuing the dispute, while simultaneously, a thought of repentance arose in their hearts.

[In Rashi’s commentary to Tehillim, he does write, "And at the time of the dispute, they withdrew." However, from the fact that in his commentary on the Torah he merely states, "And at the time of the dispute, they contemplated repentance in their hearts" (and does not write that "they withdrew"), it is understood that the literal meaning of the verses (in the Chumash) implies that they did not withdraw from the dispute (and the simple reason is that if they had withdrawn, they would not have been swallowed by the earth), and they only contemplated repentance in their hearts.]

Therefore, their punishment was strange, as mentioned, for it is *midah k’neged midah* (measure for measure): just as their conduct was paradoxical—in practice and in public, it was not evident that they had withdrawn from the dispute, yet in the secrecy of their thoughts and contemplations, they were separated from the rest of Korach’s assembly—so too was their punishment in this measure: all of Israel saw that they were punished similarly to Korach and his assembly, in that they were swallowed by the earth, yet in that very place, swallowed in the earth, they were separated from the rest: "a high place in Gehenom, and they sat there."

**3.** It can be said that in this, Rashi is resolving, in passing (as is his custom), a general question regarding this verse—what is the purpose of the entire statement, "And the sons of Korach did not die," here? The story that the sons of Korach did not die because they repented belongs earlier in Parshas Korach, when the Torah recounts the event itself.

On a simple level, it can be said that here in our Parsha, the text needed to inform us that they did not die, so that it would not be difficult for us that the "family of the Korchi" is counted among the families of the sons of Levi. Therefore, the text prefaces that "the sons of Korach did not die," and since they did not die when they were swallowed and "a high place was fortified for them... and they sat there," it is logical that over time they emerged from there, and from them emerged (as Rashi says earlier in Parshas Korach, as mentioned) Shmuel the Prophet and the twenty-four watches, etc.

[Several of Rashi’s commentators write that when Rashi says "was fortified... and they sat there," he means that the sons of Korach remained there forever. But their words are highly questionable, for since Rashi writes explicitly that Shmuel the Prophet and the twenty-four watches emerged from the sons of Korach because they repented, it must be that they eventually emerged alive from the earth. Furthermore—from the fact that in our Parsha, in the time of Moshe, the "family of the Korchi" is counted, it is proven that in the time of Moshe they were already living family lives.]

However, this still does not resolve the aforementioned question—why did the text not preface and write that the sons of Korach did not die in its proper place in Parshas Korach, where the "family of the Korchi" remained?

Some commentators say this is "out of respect for Moshe, for Moshe said, 'If Hashem creates a creation... and they descend alive into the grave,' and if it had been mentioned here that his sons did not die, it would be a disgrace to Moshe that his prayer was not effective, since a remnant of them remained on the earth."

But this answer is also questionable, for the fact that "the sons of Korach did not die" is not at all in contradiction to Moshe’s prayer, "If Hashem creates a creation... and they descend alive into the grave," since in practice his prayer was fulfilled and they also descended alive into the grave, but afterwards "a high place in Gehenom was fortified for them." Thus, there is no disgrace to Moshe here [and on the contrary, since Moshe wanted and strove for them to repent, it is to Moshe’s credit that he succeeded, at the very least, in causing the sons of Korach to repent].

**4.** One can say regarding this, by first noting the precision of the verse’s language in Parshas Korach regarding the punishment of being swallowed: "And they descended... alive into the grave, and the earth covered them, and they perished from the midst of the congregation." Seemingly, what is the precision of "and they perished from the midst of the congregation," rather than just "and they perished," or "and they perished from the earth," and the like?

Based on what we have explained regarding the sin and repentance of the sons of Korach, it can be said that the punishment of the sons of Korach was only that they perished "from the midst of that congregation"—that is, the congregation that saw with their own eyes their participation in the dispute, while they did not see their repentance. Therefore, it was necessary that they perish from *this* congregation; whereas another congregation (who were not witnesses to the matter) had no reason for them to perish from its midst.

This is the reason it was not said earlier in Parshas Korach that "the sons of Korach did not die"—because only after the congregation that existed at the time of Korach’s dispute was no longer present, as Rashi says earlier (in Parshas Chukas) that in this assembly "there was not one among them of those upon whom the decree was issued, for the generation of the desert had already perished"—could they emerge from their place, etc., and only then was it revealed to Bnei Yisrael that "the sons of Korach did not die."

**5.** However, the matter is still not fully resolved, for this verse, "And the sons of Korach did not die," comes (at the end of the counting of the families of the sons of Reuven—) in continuation of the story regarding Dasan and Aviram, the sons of Eliav (son of Palu, son of Reuven)—"These are Dasan and Aviram, the chosen of the congregation, who contended against Moshe... when they contended against Hashem, and the earth opened its mouth and swallowed them..."

Seemingly, it would have been more appropriate to write this matter in the place where the text counts the families of the sons of Levi, and among them, the "family of the Korchi."

On a simple level, one could say: since the text tells here of the swallowing of Dasan and Aviram, the text concludes the matter of the swallowing briefly by saying that "the sons of Korach did not die" (whereas if it had been written in the section of the families of the sons of Levi, the text would have had to repeat the matter of the dispute and the swallowing once again).

But this is somewhat forced regarding the content of the matters—for since the text comes to hint at the repentance of the sons of Korach, because of which they did not die—it is not appropriate to mention the sons of Korach in the same continuation as the story regarding these wicked men, Dasan and Aviram, who perished, they and all that they had.

To resolve this question, Rashi prefaces, "They were involved in the counsel from the beginning": the fact that the text attaches the matter that "the sons of Korach did not die" to the story regarding Dasan and Aviram comes to teach us about the comparison and similarity between them in the severity of the dispute—just as Dasan and Aviram were those "who contended against Moshe," and as Rashi explains, "They incited Israel to quarrel with Moshe," meaning they not only involved themselves in the assembly of the disputers, but they "contended" and "incited" to quarrel with schemes and counsel to dispute Moshe and the Ribono shel Olam [and this was the severity of the sin of Dasan and Aviram, because of which they perished, they and all that they had, a punishment we do not find (explicitly in the Chumash) throughout all the forty years that Israel was in the desert (and not even among the two hundred and fifty men of Korach’s assembly)]—so too it is with the sons of Korach, that they also "were involved in the counsel from the beginning."

And this is the magnitude of the wonder regarding the matter of the sons of Korach, that even though they were involved in the counsel from the beginning like Dasan and Aviram, nevertheless, since "they contemplated repentance in their hearts," they escaped the grave, to the point that Shmuel the Prophet and the twenty-four watches emerged from them, etc.

It can be said that this matter—the distinction between Dasan and Aviram and the sons of Korach—is in accordance with the general content of this counting of Bnei Yisrael, which was "after the plague," and as Rashi says, that the counting was in order "to know the number of those remaining"; and in continuation of this, the text tells of Korach’s assembly, that among those upon whom a decree of destruction was issued (that they perished, they and all that they had, without a remnant remaining) there were "those remaining"—"And the sons of Korach did not die."

**6.** From all of the above, there is a lesson in the magnitude of the power of teshuvah—that it has the ability to change and transform a person from one extreme to the other:

The sons of Korach were initially found at the lowest extreme, as mentioned, that they were involved in the counsel from the beginning, meaning that they not only failed in the sin of the dispute, but were in the category of those who cause the many to sin, to dispute Moshe and the Ribono shel Olam; and their repentance was not an open repentance, and on the contrary, it was only a thought of repentance in the heart, and in public it was—"at the time of the dispute," that their thought of repentance was not strong enough to cause them to withdraw from the dispute in public.

And nevertheless, not only did they escape the punishment that came upon Korach’s assembly by virtue of this thought of repentance, but furthermore, even at the time of the punishment itself, while they were swallowed in Gehenom, it was a sitting in "tranquility," in a high and fortified place, to the point that they reached the highest extreme—that "they said song," and furthermore—that the words of their song and praise were included in the Book of Tehillim, and even to the song "whose foundation is in the holy mountains."

From this, it is understood *kal v’chomer* regarding the future Redemption, that the Rambam rules "that Israel will eventually repent... and immediately they will be redeemed"—that after the abundance of Torah and mitzvos with mesiras nefesh, etc., throughout the long duration of the exile, it is enough and sufficient for Israel to have a thought of repentance in the heart to bring immediately and truly the redemption of Israel—"And it shall be said of Zion, 'Every man was born in her, and He, the Most High, shall establish her,'" in the true and complete Redemption through Mashiach Tzidkeinu.

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Pinchas, 5737)

## Sicha 3

### 176

A. In our Parsha, regarding the verse, "On the eighth day there shall be an Atzeres for you," Rashi quotes the text "Atzeres shall be for you" and explains: "You shall be restrained from performing work. Another explanation: Atzeres means 'restrain yourselves from departing'—this teaches that it requires staying overnight." (He then continues, "And its Midrashic interpretation in the Aggadah is...," as will be discussed in section 3).

The Ra’am writes that Rashi’s second explanation ("restrain yourselves from departing") is sourced in the Sifrei on this verse: "As it is taught in the Sifrei: 'On the eighth day there shall be an Atzeres for you'—the Scripture restrains you from departing. If he brought his holy offerings from Beis Pagei to Jerusalem, might I think he may eat them in Jerusalem and then go and sleep in Beis Pagei? The Torah says, 'On the eighth day there shall be an Atzeres for you'—the Scripture restrains you from departing... I only know this regarding the final festival day; from where do I know it applies to the first festival day? It is a logical inference: since this is called a *mikra kodesh* and that is called a *mikra kodesh*, just as this one requires restraint from departing, so too that one requires restraint from departing."

Seemingly, this requires study: It has been explained many times at length that it is Rashi’s way, in his commentary on the Torah, to explain the *peshuto shel mikra* (the plain meaning of the verse) and not to bring the teachings of our Sages on the verses. It is obvious that one cannot say Rashi relies on the student to look up the words of our Sages to understand his own commentary, especially in a place (like the present case) where Rashi does not cite their source ("as it is in the Sifrei" and the like). If so, we must understand what Rashi means here by "this teaches that it requires staying overnight," without explaining the nature and essence of this stay—its time, its place, (and its reason).

Furthermore: In his commentary on the Shas, Rashi explains in several places that the stay is on the night following the festival (and in Jerusalem). If in his commentary on the Shas Rashi finds it necessary to explain the time (and place) of this stay, then all the more so should he have explained this in his commentary on the Torah, which follows the *peshuto shel mikra* and is intended (also) for a five-year-old.

[Especially since in another place in his commentary on the Shas, he writes that the time for the stay (on the first festival of Pesach) is (not the night following the festival, but) "the first night," meaning the time for the stay is the first night of the festival (as is explicit in Rashi there, that the law of staying overnight does not come to forbid returning "to his actual home"—"for it is a festival, and furthermore, he needs to bring the *Olas Re’iyah*"—but rather that it is forbidden to sleep "outside the wall but within the techum").]

If so, Rashi should have explained here whether the stay is on the night of the festival or the night following the festival.

[For example: When Shemini Atzeres falls on a Thursday, does the "requires staying overnight" of Shemini Atzeres refer to the night between Wednesday (21 Tishrei) and Thursday (22 Tishrei)—i.e., the night after Hoshana Rabbah, the beginning of Shemini Atzeres—or the night between Thursday and Friday (the night after Shemini Atzeres)?]

### 177

Furthermore: Later in Parshas Re’eh, Rashi specifies "that it requires staying overnight on the night following the festival," and did not suffice with saying "it teaches that it requires staying overnight," even though there, seemingly, it is self-understood—for he explains the verse there, "And you shall turn in the morning"—"to the morning of the second day" [i.e., the morning of the first day of Chol Hamoed]. Therefore, even if he had only continued with "it teaches that it requires staying overnight" (without the words "the night following the festival"), it would have been understood that it refers to that night. Nevertheless, Rashi takes the trouble to explain "the night following the festival"—so what is the reason he omitted it here?

B. Also, it must be understood: It is known that when Rashi brings two (or more) explanations for a verse, it is because each explanation has a difficulty that the other does not have, but the difficulty in the first explanation is not as great as the difficulty in the second, and therefore it is first. We need an explanation in the present case: What is the difficulty in the first explanation that necessitated Rashi bringing a second, and conversely—in what way is the second explanation (according to *peshuto shel mikra*) more difficult than the first?

Some commentators suggest that for the first explanation, the difficulty is: "Why is this festival called *Atzeres* more than other festivals, when all of them are also 'restrained' from performing work?" And for the second explanation, the difficulty is: "The stay is on the night following it, so how can we say that the festival day itself is called *Atzeres* because it requires a stay (on the night) after it? Furthermore, the first festival day also requires a stay, so why is the last festival day called *Atzeres* more than the first?"

However, their words require study, for the reason for bringing the second explanation is (as mentioned above) because according to this explanation, the difficulties present in the first are resolved. But according to the explanation above, the second explanation also has the same difficulty as the first : "Why is the last festival day... different from the first festival day?" 

Also, the very difficulty mentioned regarding the second explanation requires clarification: (a) The objection that it is impossible for the festival day itself to be called *Atzeres* because it requires a stay on the night following it—Rashi does not explain the time of the stay at all, whether it is on the night of the festival or the night following it (and in his commentary on the Shas, we find that he explains (or calls it) that the stay is on the night of the festival, as mentioned above). Why not explain that it refers to the "night of the festival"? (b) The objection "the first festival day also requires a stay, so why is the last festival day different," is not explicit in the text (or in Rashi) that the first festival day requires a stay.

C. Further on, Rashi continues: "And its Midrashic interpretation in the Aggadah: Because throughout the days of the festival they offered sacrifices corresponding to the seventy nations, and when they were about to leave, the Holy One, blessed be He, said to them: 'I request of you, prepare a small meal for me so that I may derive pleasure from you, one bullock... stay with me a little longer...'".

We must understand: Since Rashi has already brought two explanations, both of which are according to *peshuto shel mikra*, what compels him to bring another explanation, especially since it is "its Midrashic interpretation in the Aggadah" (as Rashi says)? There is a well-known rule in Rashi’s words: "I have come only to explain the *peshuto shel mikra* and the Aggadah that resolves the words of the Scripture, each matter in its proper place", meaning he only brings Aggadah if "we need the words of the Aggadah".

Also: Rashi already brought the content of this Midrash earlier in Parshas Emor regarding the verse "It is an *Atzeres*." But there, he did not bring the two explanations he wrote here ("restrained from performing work" or "restrained from departing"), nor did he write "its Midrashic interpretation in the Aggadah" and the like. Rather, he took it there that the interpretation "I have restrained you with Me, like a king who invited his children..." is the simple explanation of "It is an *Atzeres*."

It can be said that the reason is because from the phrasing of the verse there, "It is an *Atzeres*"—and not as it is written here, "There shall be an *Atzeres* for you"—it is understood that this is not an *Atzeres* brought about and caused by and from the side of Bnei Yisrael. Therefore, he does not explain that this phrasing indicates a command to Bnei Yisrael (to be restrained from work or restrained from departing), but explains it in connection to the essence of the day itself, a day in which "I have restrained you with Me..."

According to this, the wonder is even greater regarding our verse, for since it is written here "There shall be an *Atzeres* for you," what is the need to explain it also according to the aforementioned "Midrashic interpretation in the Aggadah"?

D. The explanation for this is:
The difficulty in the verse here (which Rashi comes to resolve) is the change in the definition of the festival of Shemini Atzeres compared to other festivals. In all the festivals in our Parsha, it is stated "a *mikra kodesh*" [on the fifteenth day of the first month (Pesach), on the seventh day (of Pesach), on the day of the first fruits, on the first day of the seventh month (Rosh Hashanah), on the tenth day of the seventh month (Yom Kippur), on the fifteenth day of the seventh month (the first day of Sukkos)]. Regarding Shemini Atzeres, the verse changes here, and it is not stated "a *mikra kodesh*," but rather "there shall be an *Atzeres* for you."

From this, it is proven that the verse (here) comes to inform us that the festival of "the eighth day" is different from all other festivals, that it is not in the category of "a *mikra kodesh*," but has a special law of "there shall be an *Atzeres* for you." Regarding the definition of this special law, Rashi brings two explanations:

The first explanation is that the law of "Atzeres" is the prohibition of performing work, regarding which the verse continues, "You shall do no servile work," meaning "there shall be an *Atzeres* for you" means you shall be "restrained from performing work." This means that the law of the prohibition of work on Shemini Atzeres is different from the prohibition on other festivals. The prohibition of work on other festivals belongs to the holiness of the day, "a *mikra kodesh*", whereas on Shemini Atzeres it is due to a special command: "There shall be an *Atzeres* for you," that the non-performance of work on it is due to a separate law and obligation, "restrained from performing work" (which is a category of absence and negation), and not from the side of "a *mikra kodesh*." [And that which is stated in the verse in Parshas Emor regarding Shemini Atzeres as a *mikra kodesh*, it is not stated in proximity to "you shall do no servile work," but rather as a preface to "and you shall offer a fire-offering to Hashem"].

However, this explanation is somewhat difficult, because this entire section of the festivals deals primarily not with the prohibition of work on the festival, but with the sacrifices (the *mussafim*). If so, it is difficult to explain that specifically in this section the verse comes to differentiate between the eighth day and the other festivals regarding the prohibition of work (the appropriate place for this innovation being, seemingly, earlier in Parshas Emor).

Because of this difficulty, Rashi continues and brings another explanation—"Another explanation: Atzeres means restrain yourselves from departing," meaning that the command "there shall be an *Atzeres*" on the eighth day (does not refer to the prohibition of work on this day, but) relates to the sacrifices of this festival. This is that Bnei Yisrael are restrained on this day from departing from before Hashem (from the place of offering the sacrifices). Rashi does not need to explain their location during the festival (where they are "restrained from departing"), for it was already explained earlier in Parshas Mishpatim and Parshas Tisa, "Three times a year all your males shall appear before the Master, Hashem," that on the three festivals they come and are present before Hashem, in the Beis Hamikdash and in Jerusalem. And regarding the seven days of Sukkos, it is explicit, "And you shall rejoice before Hashem your God for seven days", that they have already come and are present in Jerusalem for the seven days—the Torah comes to add that they should linger there one more day.

### 180

However, this explanation by itself is not sufficient. If it were only for this, the verse would not need to describe the eighth day with the name "there shall be an *Atzeres* for you" to teach that Bnei Yisrael must linger in Jerusalem on Shemini Atzeres—for by the very fact that the Torah informs us there is an eighth day (following the festival of Sukkos) which (is also a festival where work is forbidden and) on which they bring sacrifices ("and you shall offer..."), it is already understood that on this day they linger in Jerusalem. What is added by the emphasis that this day is called "there shall be an *Atzeres* for you"?

Therefore, Rashi continues, "it teaches that it requires staying overnight," meaning the name "Atzeres" teaches us a new law regarding the manner of the lingering: that the stay in Jerusalem is not like the other days of the festival, but "it requires staying overnight" (that they must also sleep in Jerusalem), because on this day they are "restrained from departing."

Perhaps the reason Rashi did not explain the time of the stay is that since the obligation of staying overnight is learned from the words "there shall be an *Atzeres* for you," that on this day they are "restrained from departing," it is understood that the time of this stay is on the night of the eighth day of the festival (which is what is called "Shemini Atzeres," and not the night following the festival). This is the innovation of Shemini Atzeres compared to other festivals, where (according to *peshuto shel mikra*) one could sleep on the night of the festival in another place outside of Jerusalem and the like, whereas on the night of Shemini Atzeres one must sleep in Jerusalem.

[According to this—according to Rashi, the obligation of staying overnight in our Parsha and the obligation of staying overnight in Parshas Re’eh are two different stays: the law of staying overnight in our Parsha is on the night of Shemini Atzeres, whereas the law of staying overnight mentioned there (regarding the first day of the festival of Pesach) is on the "night following the festival"].

This matter is well-suited to the section of the *mussaf* sacrifices that are brought on the festivals, which are communal sacrifices brought for all of Israel in the Beis Hamikdash, and for which reason Bnei Yisrael linger in Jerusalem at this time.

However, this explanation, while it fits better (than the first explanation) the general content of the section, is on the other hand more difficult to reconcile with the simple flow of the verse—"there shall be an *Atzeres* for you, you shall do no servile work"—for what does the obligation of staying overnight have to do with the prohibition of work? Furthermore: according to the second explanation, only the reason for why "there shall be an *Atzeres* for you" is said regarding Shemini Atzeres is explained, but it is not resolved—why "a *mikra kodesh*" is not said here (as the reason for the prohibition of work). Therefore, Rashi brings it only as a second explanation.

### 181

E. And still, the matter is not entirely clear: the two explanations in Rashi explain only what the difference is between Shemini Atzeres and the other festivals (whether it is that the prohibition of work on it is only in the category of "restrained from performing work," or that "they are restrained from departing... it requires staying overnight"), but this itself requires a reason—why, in truth, is this festival different from all other festivals?

To resolve this, Rashi continues: "And its Midrashic interpretation in the Aggadah: Because... and when they were about to leave, the Holy One, blessed be He, said to them: 'I request of you... stay with me a little longer... it is a term of affection... your departure is difficult for me, stay one more day'" —according to this Aggadah, the reason and cause for the difference between Shemini Atzeres and all other festivals is well explained [both regarding the prohibition of work (as in the first explanation) and regarding the obligation of staying overnight (as in the second explanation)].

Meaning: Rashi’s intention here is not to bring a third explanation for the word "Atzeres" (similar to his explanation in Parshas Emor, where he explains what is written there, "It is an *Atzeres*—'I have restrained you with Me... stay with me one more day'"), but rather by bringing this Aggadah, his intention is to explain the two previous explanations (which is why Rashi did not write here the language of the verse "I have restrained you" and the like).

The explanation of the matter:
From this Aggadah, it is understood that essentially the festival should have been only seven days, but "when they were about to leave" (at the end of the seven days, then) the Holy One, blessed be He, says to them to linger one more day "so that I may derive pleasure from you." This means that this festival is not a day that is set apart essentially and by itself, but is in the category of an addition to the festival of Sukkos (which was innovated "when they were about to leave").

This is the explanation for the first interpretation, that the prohibition of work on Shemini Atzeres is different from all other festivals—that on other festivals it is (as mentioned above) because they are "a *mikra kodesh*," whereas on Shemini Atzeres (where "a *mikra kodesh*" is not stated (here)) it is a negation, that the prohibition of work is that they must be "restrained from performing work" —for since Shemini Atzeres is not a festival by its own nature, it is understood that the holiness of the day is not the cause for the festival and the prohibition of work, but rather the opposite—the fact that Bnei Yisrael fulfill the request of the Holy One, blessed be He (to linger with Him by) restraining themselves from work, etc., is the cause and factor that the day becomes sanctified with the holiness of a festival.

Similarly for the second explanation ("Atzeres means restrain yourselves from departing, this teaches that it requires staying overnight")—this Aggadah is the explanation for the special obligation of staying overnight on Shemini Atzeres:
Since the entire content of this festival is...

### 183

...restrained, etc."—the intention is not that the law of staying overnight is because of the honor of the festival (*mikra kodesh*) that is in it, but rather, since both (the first festival day and the last festival day) are called *mikra kodesh*, we learn by analogy from the second to the first. This means that "this is called *mikra kodesh* and that is called..." is only a teaching to equate them to one another regarding the law of "restrained from departing," and not a cause or reason for this law.

This is also explicit in the Sifrei on Re’eh regarding the verse "And you shall turn in the morning and go to your tents"—"this teaches that they are required to stay overnight. I only know this regarding these alone; from where do I include birds, meal-offerings, wine, frankincense, and wood? The Torah says 'And you shall turn in the morning,' all turnings that you turn from the morning and onwards" (except that there it is innovated that the law of staying overnight is not only for sacrifices that a person brings on the festival, but that every sacrifice one brings requires staying overnight). This stay is on the night following the bringing of the sacrifice.

It is found explained from the words of the Sifrei that the obligation of staying overnight is because of his private sacrifices that he brings on the festival (and likewise on other days of the year).

However, we find in the *Rishonim* that they explained that there is a law of staying overnight (also) because of the festival, that the honor of the festival requires staying overnight in Jerusalem.

According to these reasons, it would be difficult: why did the Merciful One inform us "Atzeres, restrain yourselves from departing" specifically regarding the day of Shemini Atzeres and not regarding the sacrifices of other festivals? Furthermore, after we learn from "And you shall turn in the morning and go to your tents" that all turnings that you turn from the morning and onwards, that every sacrifice requires staying overnight, why did the Torah need to say regarding Shemini Atzeres, "There shall be an *Atzeres* for you, the Scripture restrains you from departing" ?

However, according to Rashi it is explained that for Rashi, "And you shall turn in the morning and go to your tents" in Parshas Re’eh and "Atzeres, restrain yourselves from departing" here are two different laws: the law of "And you shall turn in the morning" refers to the law of staying overnight because of the sacrifice, and in this, "it requires staying overnight on the night following the festival." Whereas that which requires staying overnight on the day of Shemini Atzeres, "restrain yourselves from departing," is a law regarding the day of Shemini Atzeres itself. Therefore: (a) it is not on other festivals; (b) it requires staying overnight not on the night following the festival, but on the night of the festival (for even though he cannot leave outside the *techum*, etc., it is possible that he might leave Jerusalem within the *techum*); (c) this does not relate to the bringing of his holy offerings, the private sacrifices of each and every one of Israel (*re’iyah* and *chagigah*), but rather because of the *mussaf* sacrifices of the day, which are communal sacrifices—one bullock, one ram, which are for all of Israel.

### 184

It can be said that this also explains why on the seven days preceding it, there was no requirement to stay overnight, because since throughout all the days of the festival they offered sacrifices corresponding to the seventy nations, even though they were in Jerusalem for all seven days, there is no necessity that it be in such a manner that they linger there the entire time and do not leave to sleep.

H. The inner dimension of Torah in Rashi’s commentary:
It is known what our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe said, that "the seven Rabbinic mitzvos... emerge and are drawn from the mitzvos of the Torah and are included in them in the number of 613."

According to this, it can be said that the second festival day of the Diaspora, which is from the words of the Scribes (and from matters that were innovated in the Diaspora), is drawn from the festival (of the Torah) of Shemini Atzeres, which in the Torah itself is that which was added to the festival (from the three festivals) explicit in the Torah. Similar to how the second festival day of the Diaspora is an innovation of the words of the Scribes (the Sages of Israel)—something similar is also in the festival of Shemini Atzeres from the Torah, whose essence is that the Holy One, blessed be He, says to Bnei Yisrael—"I request of you, prepare for me... stay with me..."

Now, regarding the second festival day of the Diaspora, we find two extremes: its holiness is not as severe as the first festival day, and on the other hand, our Sages said, "The words of the Scribes are dear to Me," the words of the Scribes are more beloved than the words of the Torah. From this, it is understood also regarding the second festival day of the Diaspora, that there is in it a special dearness and love more than the first festival day.

Something similar to these two matters we find also in Shemini Atzeres—in accordance with the two explanations in Rashi:
The first explanation—"restrained from performing work"—indicates that the holiness of the festival is not as much as the holiness of the three festivals, in which "you shall do no servile work" is a result of it being a *mikra kodesh*, as explained at length above.

And the second explanation in Rashi comes—that specifically in this festival there is a special dearness and love, "Atzeres, restrain yourselves from departing... it requires staying overnight," and as the continuation of the Aggadah in Rashi—that then there is the connection and unification between Israel and their Father in Heaven, in such a manner that it is impossible to separate between them at all (even not at night), even more than the three festivals.

This is also the reason that this matter comes through the avodah of Bnei Yisrael, as mentioned above in Rashi’s language, "The Holy One, blessed be He, said to them, 'I request of you...'"—because the perfection of the dearness and love of the Holy One, blessed be He, is revealed specifically in that Bnei Yisrael, through their avodah and exertion, transform a weekday to become a festival (that they linger with the Holy One, blessed be He).

From this comes the empowerment, that even after the festival, when everyone returns to their home, and is not before Hashem in Jerusalem and in the Beis Hamikdash, and is occupied "in all your work"—in their weekday activities, they still linger and are restrained and are (—that only I and you)—attached to the Place, until the ultimate unification, "there shall be an *Atzeres* for you"—and there shall be no stranger with you—Israel and the King alone.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Pinchas, 5727)


## מטות

## 194

### Matos

1. Our Sages of blessed memory state that the days of *Bein HaMetzarim* are alluded to in the prophecy of Yirmiyahu (regarding the destruction of the First Beis Hamikdash and the Babylonian exile): "I see a *makel shaked* (almond rod)." The *shaked*—"from the time of its budding until the completion of its ripening is twenty-one days, corresponding to the number of days between the seventeenth of Tammuz, when the city was breached, and the ninth of Av, when the Beis Hamikdash was burned."

On the surface, it would appear that the time it takes for almonds to grow alludes to the period of *Bein HaMetzarim* simply because both share the same number of days (twenty-one). However, based on the principle that all matters of Torah are precise to the ultimate degree, it is reasonable to say that there is also an internal connection between them.

This can be explained based on the Mishnah, which notes that there are two types of almonds—bitter almonds and sweet almonds. Bitter almonds are sweet in their youth and become bitter in their maturity, while with sweet almonds it is the opposite: they are bitter in their youth, and subsequently, upon the completion of their ripening, they become sweet.

It is explained in *Tzafnat Paneach* (by the Rogatchover) that the primary name "almonds" (*shaked*) refers to the "large, sweet ones" (whereas those that are sweet in their youth are called *luzim*). Thus, the characteristic of the "almond" (and the function of its twenty-one days of ripening) is to transform from bitter to sweet.

It may be said that for this reason, the days of *Bein HaMetzarim* are alluded to by the "almond rod" (*makel shaked*)—for the essence of the *avodah* of these days is not merely to nullify the negative aspect (the "bitterness") within them, but furthermore (akin to the language of the Rambam regarding the fast days in the time to come) to transform them into "good days and days of joy and gladness," so that in place of their "bitterness," the days will become "sweet" and joyful.

[And according to this, the promise in Yirmiyahu, "And it shall be that just as I watched (*shakadti*) over them to uproot... so shall I watch (*eshkod*) over them to build," becomes understood—that the very concept of "watching" (*shaked*) is a total reversal from one extreme to the other, where instead of watching for the opposite of good (at the beginning of Yirmiyahu’s prophecy), the watching becomes for the good.]

2. Based on this, another detail in this prophecy can be explained:

The allusion to the days of *Bein HaMetzarim* in the "almond rod" is based on the speed of the fruit's growth. If so, further explanation is needed: Why was the prophecy given via an "almond rod" (where he saw "a staff from a tree upon which almonds grow," meaning the staff itself was "without leaves and without flowers"), rather than showing him a branch that had almonds on it?

Seemingly, it could be said that this alludes to an additional matter: A "rod" (*makel*) signifies striking—the punishments and calamities that Hashem brought upon Yisrael during these days. But on the face of it, this is not a sufficient explanation, for the essence of the prophecy here is the matter of "watching" ("I am watching over My word to perform it"), to the point that in this verse, the matter of calamity is not explicitly mentioned. If so, it would have been preferable to allude to the matter of watching in a clear and manifest way (by seeing almond fruit, or at least a flower), rather than obscuring this allusion by showing an "almond rod" (to the point that Yirmiyahu had to contemplate the rod until he "recognized that it was from an almond tree").

According to what we explained above regarding the essence of the "almond," it can be said that the reason he specifically saw a rod is because the aforementioned allusion regarding almonds (transforming bitter to sweet) is connected to the rod.

3. Explaining the matter:

It is explained in many places (regarding the two names by which Yisrael are called—*shevatim* and *matos*)—the difference between a *shevet* and a *mateh* is, simply put, that a *shevet* is a soft branch that still contains the moisture of the tree's nourishment (and all the more so when it is still connected to the tree), whereas a *mateh* is one that (after being severed from the tree) has already had its moisture of nourishment dried up.

However, precisely because of this, the *shevet* becomes hard and strong—a *mateh*.

Regarding the levels of a person's *avodah*:

"Shevet" alludes to Bnei Yisrael when they are in a state of "connection to their source," which generally occurs when the Beis Hamikdash stands in its place and Bnei Yisrael dwell on their land, and "we can go up and see and prostrate ourselves before You"—then the connection to their source ("the tree from above") is felt within Yisrael. Conversely, "mateh" signifies the state of Bnei Yisrael in exile, when their connection to their source is not visibly apparent, for "we have not seen our signs" (there is no vision of *Elokus*, etc.), to the point that there are concealments and hiddenness regarding His *avodah*, and obstacles and delays. But precisely through this *avodah*, the strength of a Yid is revealed, as he overcomes all the obstacles and delays, battles the opponents, and defeats them, until he reaches the perfection of *avodah*, transforming the "bitterness" of exile into the "sweetness" of *Geulah*.

According to this, it can be said that the allusion in the "rod" (*makel*) in Yirmiyahu’s prophecy is to the "strength" within the rod (similar to *mateh*)—for in order to transform from bitter to sweet (which is the characteristic of almonds, as mentioned), one requires the strength of a "rod."

[It can be said that the matter of strength alluded to in the "rod" is also related to the matter of speed in the almond—that it "hastens to bring forth a flower before all the trees" (and this is the simple interpretation of Yirmiyahu’s prophecy, that "almond rod" is an allusion that Hashem "hastens to perform" His words):

The explanation of the Alter Rebbe is known regarding the verse "and it brought forth almonds" concerning the staff of Aharon the Kohen—that the *Birchas Kohanim* acts to bestow *chesed* downward in a manner of "speed." For there is a bestowal of *chesed* that is "delayed and tarries until it is drawn and comes downward, for in every stage of *hishtalshelus* from chamber to chamber, it is through judgment to see if it is worthy for the bestowal to be drawn downward, and they examine its judgment, etc." Conversely, when the *chesed* is drawn through Aharon (and his descendants after him) in *Birchas Kohanim*, "then the *shefa* is drawn with speed through all the worlds without any preventer or delayer," similar to the analogy of "a very great river where the flow of water is with great force... it will not be delayed by preventers and delayers... but it will go on its way and wash away the trees and earth that hinder." It is understood from this that the "speed" (*shaked*) comes from overcoming with strength (*makel*) all the obstacles and delays.]

4. Now, the words of the *Shelah* are well known, that the three *parshios* Matos, Masei, and Devarim "always fall within *Bein HaMetzarim*, for it is a matter in its time" (and he explains several details regarding the connection of the content of these three *parshios* to the days of *Bein HaMetzarim*).

According to the above, there is a manifest allusion here to the matter of *Bein HaMetzarim* in the name of the first *parshah* (of these three)—which, according to the custom of Yisrael (which is Torah), is called "Matos" (similar to "almond rod").

It can be said that the aforementioned content of the "rod (almond)" and "staff" (*mateh*)—the power and strength to transform an undesirable state into good—is alluded to in the details of this *parshah*, which contains three matters: (a) the *parshah* of vows (*nedarim*), (b) the war against Midian, (c) the narrative of the tribes of Gad and Reuven.

*Nedarim*: The explanation is known regarding the statements of our Sages concerning vows—which appear to contradict one another, for in one place they said "vows are a fence for abstinence," and in the *Yerushalmi* it is stated "is it not enough for you what the Torah has forbidden you?"—

This refers to two different levels in a person's *avodah*: At the beginning of the *avodah*, there must be extra caution so that the matters of the world do not drag a person down to become immersed in physicality and materialism; therefore, there must be abstinence from the matters of the world, and "vows are a fence for abstinence." But after a person elevates himself in his *avodah*, and the light of *kedushah* shines within him to the point that he has the ability to clarify and refine the physicality of the world and elevate it to *kedushah*, then, on the contrary, he must engage in the clarification and refinement of the world.

From this it is understood that the *parshah* of vows is directed primarily to a person who is at the beginning or middle of his *avodah*, and therefore needs abstinence from the world.

But the ultimate purpose of the matter is the annulment of the vow (by the father or husband), and likewise the release of the vow (by the sage)—that the father, husband, and sage have the power to act so that even such a person, who by his own nature and state needs vows (to abstain him from the matters of the world), can elevate the matters of the world to *kedushah* (they nullify the vow and transform the prohibition into a permission).

This is the connection between the *parshah* of vows and "Matos"—for the purpose of vows, their annulment, is through the bestowing and activation of the strength ("staff") to transform the darkness of the world into the light of *kedushah* with "speed."

*War against Midian*: The primary length in our *parshah* is not regarding the war and the victory of Bnei Yisrael, but regarding the spoils and the booty of Midian, the purification and *kashering* of the vessels of Midian, and likewise the distribution of the spoils and the *terumah* of the levy to Hashem from it.

This is because the purpose of the perfection of the *avodah* of the war against Midian is not merely the nullification of the *kelipah* of Midian, but the transformation of bitter into sweet, to purify and elevate the spoils of Midian to *kedushah*, until a portion of it was raised to be a "levy to Hashem."

*Bnei Gad and Bnei Reuven*: Moshe’s concern was, "Shall your brothers go to war while you sit here?"—meaning, that they did not want to fight against Canaan. It is the same in spirituality: the reason Bnei Gad and Bnei Reuven wanted to remain on the other side of the Jordan, "a land of livestock," is because they chose to be shepherds, an occupation that allowed them to be in a state of seclusion (in the field, far from the troubles and noise of the city) so that they could be in constant *dveikus* with Hashem, etc. This is the argument of Moshe Rabbeinu in his response to them, "Shall your brothers go to war, etc."—meaning that the essence of the *avodah* is that a person should come and fight against the materiality of the world, in order to clarify and refine it, etc. Therefore, he stipulated with them, "If you will do this thing, if you will arm yourselves before Hashem for the war... (until) the land is conquered before Hashem."

And in this is also the conclusion of the *parshah*—the story regarding the building of the cities of Sichon and Og by Bnei Gad and Bnei Reuven, and in a manner of "changing their names," for these cities belonged to idolatry, "and Bnei Reuven changed their names to other names"—the transformation of darkness into light and bitterness into sweetness.

Therefore, we read this *parshah* during *Bein HaMetzarim*—to teach that the purpose of these days is to transform them from bitterness to sweetness, that they should be transformed into "good days and days of joy and gladness," in the *Geulah* that is to come, speedily in our days, truly.


## מסעי

## Sicha 2

A. "And the congregation shall save the murderer from the hand of the avenger of blood, and the congregation shall restore him to his city of refuge... and he shall dwell in it until the death of the Kohen Gadol...".

In his commentary on the verse, Rashi brings two reasons for this law (that the murderer must dwell in the city of refuge "until the death of the Kohen Gadol"):

1) That he (the Kohen Gadol) comes to cause the Shechinah to dwell among Yisrael and to lengthen their days, while the murderer comes to remove the Shechinah from Yisrael and shortens the days of life; therefore, it is not appropriate for him to be before the Kohen Gadol.

2) Because the Kohen Gadol should have prayed that this mishap would not occur to Yisrael during his lifetime.

The commentators write that according to the second interpretation in Rashi—"because he should have prayed..."—the intention of the verse "and he shall dwell in it until the death of the Kohen Gadol" is that "the Kohen Gadol is punished... for not having prayed".

Seemingly, one must wonder: Where is it hinted in the verse and the law of "and he shall dwell in it until the death of the Kohen Gadol" that there is a punishment here for the Kohen Gadol? And what is the punishment to the Kohen Gadol in the fact that the murderer must sit in his city of refuge until the death of the Kohen Gadol ?!

The *Shevilei HaPardes* writes that this is "as it is written 'until the death of the Kohen Gadol,' the verse tells you that the murderer will live long after the Kohen Gadol, because the Kohen Gadol will die soon. And why? He explains: because he should have prayed..."

Seemingly, this explanation is very difficult, for in the simple meaning of the verse there is no implication that the murderer will live longer than the Kohen Gadol. The intention of the verse "and he shall dwell in it until the death of the Kohen Gadol" is simply to impose an obligation upon the murderer (for the sake of saving his own life) to remain in his city of refuge until the death of the Kohen Gadol, but it does not say that the Kohen Gadol will die before the murderer; it is even possible for the opposite to occur. On the contrary—the simple intention of the verse is to emphasize the length of the murderer's stay in his city of refuge.

Some commentators say that "because the Kohen Gadol did not pray, etc., therefore the Torah obligated him [the murderer] to sit in the city of refuge until the death of the Kohen Gadol, and he will suffer that he cannot return to his home as long as the Kohen Gadol is alive. Through this, he will pray for the death of the Kohen Gadol, and his prayer will be accepted, and the Kohen Gadol will receive his punishment... and another will be appointed... and there will no longer be a mishap in Yisrael."

Seemingly, it is a great stretch to interpret Rashi's language this way, as he did not mention at all (not even by hint) that the murderer should pray for the death of the Kohen Gadol. If this were Rashi's intention (and he begins with a five-year-old student of Scripture), he should have stated it explicitly.

Furthermore, the content of this interpretation—that the reason the Torah said "until the death of the Kohen Gadol" is so that the murderers will pray for the death of the Kohen Gadol—is very strange. In the words of the *Gur Aryeh*, "these are not the ways of the true Torah."

B. Therefore, it can be said that, seemingly, Rashi did not intend to say (as the aforementioned commentators suggest) that the fact that the Torah said "and he shall dwell in it until the death of the Kohen Gadol" is a punishment for the Kohen Gadol. Rather, in the second interpretation as well, Rashi's intention is to explain why the murderer's punishment (to be in exile) continues until "the death of the Kohen Gadol."

[This is also implied by Rashi's language, for in the first reason he concludes, "it is not appropriate for him to be before the Kohen Gadol," which clearly indicates that he is speaking about the murderer and his sin. If, in the second interpretation, he wished to explain the verse (contrary to its simple meaning) as referring to the Kohen Gadol and his punishment, he certainly would not have refrained from stating this explicitly.]

The explanation for this is as follows:

Earlier in *Parshas Chukas*, Rashi wrote that "the death of the righteous atones." From this, it is understood in our case as well that the death of the Kohen Gadol serves to atone for the sin of the murderer. However, Rashi is troubled by the question: What is the specific connection between this sin of killing a person unintentionally and the death of the Kohen Gadol specifically (and not some other righteous person)? Therefore, Rashi explains, "because the Kohen Gadol should have prayed that this mishap would not occur..." This means that the prevention of this mishap is specifically connected to the (prayer of the) Kohen Gadol. Since the Kohen Gadol has a share in the cause of this mishap (by not having prayed), his death acts (to complete this and) to atone for this mishap, the sin of the murderer.

However, it still requires explanation: What is the difficulty in the first interpretation that necessitated Rashi to also bring the second interpretation?

Especially since the second interpretation is (somewhat) far from the simple path of the text, for it is difficult to say that the determination of the (time of) the murderer's punishment, where his stay in the city of refuge is extended until the death of the Kohen Gadol, is due to the lack of caution of the Kohen Gadol ("that he should have... prayed").

Should Rashi not have sufficed with the first interpretation, that this is from the side of the murderer (that "it is not appropriate for him to be before the Kohen Gadol")?

Some commentators say, "If it were for the first reason, he would never be permitted to stand before a Kohen Gadol, even another Kohen Gadol, because one brings the Shechinah and the other removes it, as is written (by Rashi) on the verse 'for your sword...' that it is not right that he who shortens should be waved before he who lengthens."

But seemingly, this interpretation is forced, for it is understood that the murderer's sin and the effect of the sin occur at the time of the sin—during the lifetime of *this* Kohen Gadol. His act of murder "weakened" the activity of *this* Kohen Gadol to cause the Shechinah to dwell and to lengthen the days of the people of his generation, and therefore "it is not appropriate for him to be before *this* (specific) Kohen Gadol."

C. This will be understood by prefacing what we find in Rashi on the Torah: it is not his custom (in general) to explain the reasons for the laws and mitzvos of the Torah (for this is not necessary for the study of the simple meaning of Scripture), unless the reason touches upon the understanding of the simple meaning. From this, it is understood in our case as well: the fact that Rashi brought here the reasons for the murderer's stay in the city of refuge "until the death of the Kohen Gadol" is not merely to expound on the reason for the verse, but to resolve a difficulty in the verses here :

The law that "he shall dwell in it until the death of the Kohen Gadol" comes in continuation of what was written previously: "And the congregation shall save the murderer from the hand of the avenger of blood, and the congregation shall restore him to his city of refuge." Seemingly, it is puzzling: What is the connection between the rescue from the hand of the avenger of blood and "the death of the Kohen Gadol"? If the city of refuge is because the murderer requires protection from the avenger of blood, what changes in this regard—to the point of nullifying the necessity—through the death of the Kohen Gadol ?

Furthermore:

According to the simple meaning, the fear of revenge—"lest he pursue... his heart becomes hot" —does not last for a long time, as is understood from the story of Yaakov and Esav. Even though "Esav hated Yaakov" and wanted to kill Yaakov (because of something done to him personally), Rivkah nevertheless said to Yaakov, "Flee to Lavan... and dwell with him for a few days (a short time) until your brother's wrath turns away." How much more so in our case, where one kills a person unintentionally (who "is not an enemy to him, nor does he seek his harm"), and the avenger of blood is only a relative of the murdered person—after a few days, his wrath will certainly turn away and his heart will not be hot against the one who killed his relative unintentionally.
